Let us now consider what is best for the two side-pieces seen from within; the\

which attends the middle piece to hinder their naturalness: the summer-house\
heing roofed in, the light therefore falls more advantageous on these two places than\

CH AP. XII. Tables or Ordonnances of Venus and Adonis, for the\
Embellishment of Landscapes.\
HAT  may conceal nothing from. the artists, but as much as possible\
rouze and enrich their genius,  have pitched on this subject far the or-\
nament of landscapes : and, though it be common, yet  question whether it\
was ever handled in such  manner.   divide it into three ordonnances.\
The firft is, Venus\'92 making love to him.\
The second, his taking leave of hee, to go  liunting, or rather to be killed.\
And,\
The third, her finding him dead.\
The fable is this. Venus was, according to the Poets, very much enamoured\
with the youth Adonis, notwithstanding his coldness and insensibility : and yet he\
refrained not from kissing and caressing her for  season ; which much incensed\
Aiars, and raised his jealousy and rages as often as he saw the youth in her lap-\
The first Ordonnanse.\
The place opens,  pleafant and agreeable country, stored with every thing\
that can delight the eye, woods, hills, valleys; rivers, and stone-work, except\
houses\

houses and temples. Iset th\'e9 point of sight in the middle of the piece.\
Be-\
tween it and the left side, on an hillock,  place the goddess and her spark, at-\
tended by the three Graces, who are to adorn her ; one of these is twisting  wreath\
of flowers, another is crowning her with  chaplet of them, and the third is\
bringing  basket of fruit. Some Cupids are toying about her; one especially is\
fitting at her feet, blowing Adonis\'92 horn: at whom he smiles; when Venus, with\
her arm about his neck; with her hand presses his against her breast, or kisses it.\
Behind the aforesaid hillock, against the off-scape,  place some thick-leaved\
trees; the highest in the middle of the piece, and those to the left somewhat\
lower and thinner. Behind them we discover the remains of  colonade, rising\
and appearing:half behind the hillock, and running towards the point of sight.\
On the same side forwards,  set  crofs low wall, which the hillock stems.\
Against this wall, which is but three or three feet and an half in rise,  place \'e0\

knobby mossy body of  tree, about six or seven feet high; and close behind it,\
 large and beautiful one fully leayed. Somewhat bevond appears an high square\

which is wide forwards, and diminishing at 'the end of the fore-ground; where the\
second begins, and runs out into an open field ; whence to the horizon are seen\
some faint hills.\
Let us now come-forward again. In the right side corner, Envy kindles the\
fire of war ;\'92sh\'e9 is flying with  broken stinking pitch-torch in her hand, and her\
head beset with twining serpenits, secretly fhewiing Mars the: two lovers. And\
now we see the devouring god of war an his bellys with-ione-leg over  stone,\
lurking behind the pedestal, and staring eamnestly, between it and ithe green of\
the trees, at the cause of his jealousy; nis spear and fhield lie at his feet.\
 once saw  print of Julio Romano, wherein he-has placed Mars in the off-\
scape, purfuing Adonis sword in hand ; which  think too obscure and far-fetchect.\
The sense may be tolerabl\'e9; yet it is against the fable; for Ovid dues not men-\
tion that Adonis was killed. by. Mars wath.  sword, but by . wild boar, through\
his instigation.\
In the mean time,  doubt not but this my fketch and disposition will seem\
strange : nevertheless, if well executed, it will certainly appear sine with the pe-\
destal, low wall, and- colonade ; since such things create great decorum and variety\
in  landscape.\
The light;  assign, is bright sun-shine-\
My intention her\'e9 is, to represent the month of May, or the Spring, when every\
thing is coming forth and blossoming; though  am very sensible that the green\
of the trees, by the diversity of colour, is, in the summer, more painter-like ;\
however this must not be like  summer; besides, the island of Cyprus is not like\
Holland, or other cold countries, where the greens come up late; for, otherwise,\
 should not introduce \'e0 basket of fruit.\
NOW,\
\
\

Now, if it be asked because  still set on the large pedestal  vase, and that\
directly against the hillock, where Venus and Adanis are with the Graces, whether\
this would not throw  very large ground-shade over those figures ;  say it would\
not ; because  assign the sun  meridian altitude. Moreover,  do not set the\
pedestal so near the hillock as to be any obstacle to. the sigures. The foremost\
tree, because it rises so high, can also as little prejudice them, its ground-shade\
passing by them, over the willow in the corner, or at least  part of it; which\
makes the colonade, against which it spreads, fall back, though the trees behind\
the hillock can sufficiently effect the same; since  make them either dark green,\
or else in shade, and the little leafing hanging over the lovers, in the light; in\
order thus to have below some darkness for setting off the lovers: my intention\
being to place that group directly in the sun, in onder to have there the principal\
light.\
But here  may be reproved on  supposition that  act counter to my own po-\
sition; namely, that in sun-shine people do not stand talking without shading\
their eyes : which  do not deny ; but let it be considered, that the gods are not\
subject to human frailti\'e9s, and therefore they can look against the sun : and to\
solve the difficulty with respect to Adonis, who is not  god,  make his upper\
parts in shade, receiving agreeable and strong reflexions from Venas and the\
Graces.\
From whence arises another difficulty; namely, by what means this ground-\
shade can fall on him only, fince they are sitting so close together, that Venus\'92 arm\
is about his neck, and one of his hands presses her breast; and therefore she must\
needs take part of the same shad\'e9; To which,  answer, that there are means\
enough, by one thing or other, to find that shade. And as for Venus, she may be\
so disposed, either  little backward or forward, as. to receive light enough.\
Now, that Mars and Enzy, at the stone, may not draw the eye too much from\
the principals, by making them in the light,  bring not much sun into that\
quarter;  mean that  set the foremost whole stem of the tree, and  great part\
of the hindmost, with part of Mars, in  ground-shade, occasioned by something\
without the piece; and to let that shade run, on the fore-ground, just to the\
low wall ; breaking the residue here and there somewhat with bushes and shrubs :\
 might also place there  priapus-term, or other object, in order  little to fill that\
corner.\
We have before said, that neither houses nor temples must enter the compofi-\
tion. Why not they, say some, as well as the terme To which  answer, that the\
fable makes no mention of any such objects : and let me afk, who should live in\
the houses ) It is not said, that Adonis, though  man, had any houshiold, or that\
he worshipped in  temple.\
This piece may be richly embellished with ten or twelve figures, though. Mars\
and Envy are but partly figures. Some landscape-painters may possibly object\
against so great  number, for that those, well executed, would better become\
an history than  landscape: but the answer is easy; the figures are small, and\
the landscape large.\
We shall now proceed to the colours and actions of- the figures.\
We\

We represent Venus in her linen, yet with her upper parts and legs almost\

Adonis\'92 garment is greenish blue, or dark violet.\
The two Graces, standing next to Venus, are drest in light-coloured garments of\
changeable stuff and broken colours, preserving, about that group,  great mafs\
of light : for which reason,  chuse such colours as do not cause any unseemly\

Caesar Ripa relates enough touching them. Nothing is more proper for Mars,\
than  rusty fillemot or blood-coloured coat;, and for Envy, than  black one-\
As for the motions of Venus and Adonis, they are fronting in both; but their\
feet more or less turned to the light.\
Adonis, on the right side, inclines his upper parts towards Venus, with his right\
hand on her breast, and his right shoulder coming forwards ; his under parts are\
fronting, and his leg extended, and his right drawn in, as if he were about to\
rise; his face fronting inclines  little over his shoulder to the child who blows\
the horn. Contrarily, Venus, resting on her right thigh, applies, in some mea-\
sure, both her knees to his extended leg ; her face, in profile, turning towards\
him, fronts the sun; her breast is also seen fronting ; she draws back her left el-\
bow, in order to press his hand to her breast.\
On due consideration, these two figures will be found to have  natural and easy\
contrast or opposition, in motion; since  have endeavoured to give myself fuil\
satisfaction touching all the actions exhibited, before  set the layman.\
But  must return again to the ordonnance.  forgot to place two children be-\

there with the children,, to the detriment of the principal : besides  further pur-\

the one awaking, stares back with prick' up ears, at the sound of the horn.\

 piece, thus executed, is sufficient for the production of many others ; espe-\

It is unnecessary to enlarge on this table or ordonnance further than to observe,\
that Mars here signifies vengeance ;, Adonis, the cvinter; and Venus, the spring;\
which is the reason why these two last cannot agree.\
The Poets write, that there were four who went under the name of Venus. The\
first was the daughter of Calum and the Day. The second was brought forth of\
the froth of the sea, being conceived in  mother of pearl, and conducted to Cyprus\
by the airy zephyrs : it was she who bore Cupid to Mercury. The third was the\
daughter of Japiter and Dione, who was wedded to Vulcan, chief of the cuckolds;\
and the fourth was the Syrian, called Astarte, who courted the love of Adonis, and\
to whom Solomon erected altars to pleasure his concubines. Whence we may\
judge what great disparity there is between these Venuses.\
As for the wild boar, it implies the night, ignorance, impiety, filthiness,\
lewdness, &.\
The second Ordonnance, or Sequel of the foregoing Story.\
When Adonis was now tired with kissing and flattery, or, to say better, when his\
forrowful f\'e2te drew near, and the dogs, scenting the boar, set up  cry, he, deaf\
to Venus\'92 intreaties, wrested from her embraces, and jumped up eager for sport.\
We must previously understand, that we are obliged to confine ourselves to two\
principal points; namely, the general disposition, and the light: and though,\
on  due consideration, it may possibly seem to be chosen less advantageous than\
if it were  single piece (which  willingly allow), yet, as it now serves to match\
another, it therefore requires the same light, though  reversed one might bet-\
ter become it; because then  should be at greater liberty : but even then, the\
dispofition would not be different enough from the former.\
We have, in the book or ordonnance, shewed, that when two pieces hang to-\
gether, they ought to have  certain conformity ; especially landscapes with small\

visto on the left, in the other or matching-piece it must be contrary; and yet, not-\
withstandingthat necessity or rule, which however subsists, Ifind something which gives\
me greater satisfaction, and better expresses the sense, as may appear in the sequel.\

Perhaps you did not expect this fort of management, but on due consideration\
of the case, it will be found both natural and artful.\

 king Lyncus approaches the bed of his sleeping guest Triptolemus with inten-\

Thus Ihave affirmed, that cloaths serve gods and men only for distinction :\
which brings to my remembrance,  print of Poussin, confirming what  have said.\
It exhibits the Elysian fields, with the happy souls at rest, and Youth, or eternal\
Spring, dancing and strewing flowers. Here we see Hyacinthus, Narcissus, Crocus,\
Adonis, Ajax, and many others, in sitting postures, as when living: whence we\
may easily perceive, how difficult it would be to know them without their partir\
cular badges: of distinction, as the spear, horn, fountain, helmet, chaplet of\
roses, &. and how impossible it was Poussin, so excellent and learned  man, to\

 have found, in general, that the leaves are round ; which at the rising sun-\

As for the misterious sense, we must know, that the Agyptians paid more ho-\
nours to this tree than any others, on  belief, that it was  mediator between.\
heavenly and earthly things. It is moreover used to represent the sun\'92 rising and\
setting; especially with the addition of  child sitting on it, by which they signified\
the morning vapours, which the sun\'92 approach dispels. And because it opens\
and shuts its leaves with the sun\'92 rising and setting, it is sacred to Apollo, as  tree\
peculiar to him, and out of respect shewing its leaves to him only.\
The rough or hairy Lotos was also much venerated by the Romans, who offered\
the vestals locks of hair to it, \'ab as they did those of young men to Apolle, or to\
his son Asculapius.\
The Gree\'e8s sacrificed their hair in the same manner\'92to the rivers of their country,\
as having  certain relation to this tree,-which they imagined had such intercourse\
with the gods, that they made it their seat : and therefore it was planted in\
morasses.\

The story is this. As soon as Andr\'e6mon was advertised of the sorrowful accident\

with the child, at her request, kissed her for the last time : whereupon she was\
divested of her human shape at once.\

 have largely handled the aforesaid three ordonnances, to shew that landscape-\

And then, for the conclusion of landscapes, make  comparison between what is\

CHAP. XIV. Table or Ordonnance of Erifichton; and the Emblem\
of  Satyr  Punishment: Both serving for the Embellisbment of Land-\
scapes.\
VID relates that Erifichton, avery vile man, was, by the goddess Ceres,\
).\
whom he had highly offended by cutting down an exceeding high oak-tree,\
consecrated to her, punished with insatiate hunger; insomuch that, for want of\
food, he was obliged to sell his-own daughter. See Plate LVI.\
 represent this in  delightful landscape, or without-door prospect. The light\
comes from the right side; and the point of sight is in the middle. On tlie\
left side  exhibit  stately building. with  beautiful frontispiece, of the Dorick\
order, ascended by three steps running towards the point of sight. Beyond the\
steps  place an hand-rail, four feet in rise, running from the house by the point\
of sight. In the return of it stands  vase. On the right side is -river, with \
wooden bridge over it. By the water-side appears part of  town-wall, which the\
water walhes and runs round. The residue is an off-scape, here and there planted\
with trees. Next the hand-rail  pl\'e2ce the hungry Erisichton; who, with his cap\
in his left hand, is tumbling his told money into it with his right hand. His\
daughter Mestre stands behind him, near the steps; and the merchant stepping up\
shews her the door, with his right hand, wherein he has  bag half full of\
money;\
\
\
\
\
\
\
money ; at the fame time holding her with his left, by  flappet of her garment.\
Lean Hunger behind, between her and her father\'92 right side, pushes her forwards.\
with both hands. This is the main of the subject.\
The merchant looking proudly and gravely at the daughter, is dressed in  violet-coloured garment, reaching just below the knees ; it is girt about hia\
middle ; he has  fillet about his head, and he is loosely stockined and shoed, ac-\
cording to the Spartan custom : he is seen mostly from behind, resting with his\
right foot on the upper step, and drawing up the left from off the middle one.\
The daughter stands on her right leg, with her left foot just on the lower step, .\
little drawn back ; her under parts are almost fronting more or less from the light,\
she-sways her upper parts to the right, wishfully looking at her father, whoin she\
is unwilling to ieave : with sorrow and tears fhie seems to move the merchant\'92'\
pity, and to follow him against her will; she has an handkerchief in her right\
hand, with which, up at the left ear, sne seems to wipe her face, supporting the.\
elbow of that arm with her other hand. Her garment is pale yellow, with green,\
reffexions, and being slovenly gathered under the breast and tied with.  ribbon,\
hangs in tatters below the calfs of her legs ; she is bare-footed, has  beautiful\
mein, yet is somewhat thin ; her hair is light, twisted with small blue ribbons.\
Erifichton stands quite stooping, with bent knees ; his garment tied about the\
middte with  rope, is fillemot, and reaches behind to the calfs of his legs, being\
so open on the side as to discover his bare hip and leg; his left shoulder is also\
naked, his hair and beard grey, and he is lean and swarthy : his stick stands against\
the hand-rail. As for hunger, Ovid describes him thus; With frightful hair,\
eyes sunk in; mnouth and lips livid, teeth yellow and slimy, and  thick skin dis-\
covering the bones and entrails : he is seen almost to the middle above the back of\
Erisichton. The pillars of the frontispiece are grey, the house and steps free-stone,\
and the pavement the door is of, large blue ftone; and from thence down to the\
river, the ground is plain. In the front of the house are carved two Cornua Copi\'e6.\
The vase is of  reddisn stone. On the left side of it, behind the hand-rail; rifes .\
great spreading tree in full verdure, which gives  large shade against the house ;\
the stem of it is encompassed with ivy and other green, which take away the light\
of the off-scapebetweenit and the vase, together with the sharpness of the hand--\
rail, against which the daughter is brightly set off with decorum. Against the wing\
of the house, without the hand-rail,   vine. At the door waits  young,\
servant. Quite forward in the left corner, stands  watchful dog, tied with  chain:\
and barking.\
In this representation  have had an eye to three principal circumstances; indi-\
gency, necessaries of life, and opportunity. Indigency seeks relief where it is to\
be had; if not in town, elsewhere ; wherefore,  represent necessity in both father\
and daughter, coming for relief to the substantial man\'92 country seat, who lives in\
plenty. The further circumstances, as, the bridge, town, and horns of plenty,\
explain themselves.\
Ido not place lean hunger near Erisichton, contrary to what Ihave formerly said,\
namely, That, when  pafsion can be expreffed in the person himself, we have no\
need of an emblematic figure to make it known-: Hunger is placed here for two\
reasons :\
\
\
\
\
\
reasons : first, because want cannot be perfectly expressed here in its full force,\
through  present intermixture with something else ; as, the happiness of having\
found the means whereby to relieve it ; to wit, the money. Secondly, because\
Erifichton is not so naked, that his consumed body, according to the poet, can be\
shewed as occasion requires.\
The reason of my putting in the dog, is not only for the enrichment of the dis-\
position, but also to shew, that he who possesses much wealth, should likewise\
watch it. Moreover, it is usual for the country people, but chiefly men of sub-\
stance, to keep those creatures as well for pleasure as use.\
This fable is seldom seen in painting or exhibited in  print otherwise than in\
Ovid\'92 metamorphases, and that in so simple  manner, that without the explana-\
tion under it, it is scarce intelligible : for, What can be inferred from an old\
meagre man\'92 receiving  purse of money from  gentleman, with  young woman\
appearing between them ; How can the inequality between riches and poverty\
be conspicuous, when they as like in dress as if they were brother and sister; and\
this in  landscape, or the middle of afield, where is neither house, nor other token\
of their habitation ?\
The conclusion of  story is not all that is necessary to be red, we ought to\
know the origin, the fact and sequel of it. First, it is necessary to know the man\
and who Erisichton and his daughter were, to express this naturally in their persons\
and dresses. Secondly, we should know by whom they are, punished, and in\
what manner : and lastly, by whom, and by what means made easy. After  full\
all their circumstances, most naturally ; such as the place, &. After which, the\
enrichments and diminutions will follow of themselves. We may at least con-\
ceive, that they, who will not study the point, cannot go such lengths as to per-\
form so small  story as this, much less one of greater dignity, in  natural and ju-\
dicious manner.\
Ishall now, agreeable to my promise in the conclusion of the last chapter, give\
another embellishing example, in an emblem of my own invention, for the sake of\
those who will not inure themselves to historians or poets, nor confine their free and\
rich thoughts to such.  restriction.\
Sweet Repose disturbed by Lewdness. An Emblem.\
Here are seen three young nymphs of Diana\'92 train, tired with honting, reposing\
in the shade of the trees  little off from the road, and near  foamy water: which\
some fauni and satyrs espying, they were resolved to have some sport with them.\
Wherefore, acquainting their affociates with the matter, they silently advanced\
towards the place in abody ; bringing with them one of the largest Priapus termis\
they had, together with two panthers  vessel of wine and some grapes. Being\
arrived, and seeing the nymphs almost naked, and fast asleep, they planted before\
the place the aforesaid hideous scare-crow; and then softly ftole their hunting\
equipage, as quivers, arrows, bows, &. and hung them on its genitals, fasten-\
ing them with the straps, which they buckled. They moreover decked its head\
with\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
and adorning them with vizors. Not stopping here, they seized as many of the\
virgins garments as they could, and tossed them upon the high limbs of an ad-\
joining tree ; and to prevent the nymphs climbing up in order to regain, they\
tied the two panthers under the tree ; and after having set down the wine and\
grapes, pleased with the project, they covertly retired to  peeping place to wait\
the issue on the nymphs awaking. Each of the gang had brought with him his\
instrument, as, the double hautboy, cymbal, tabor, timbrel, &. wherewith,\
because it was evening, and they might sleep too long, to beat up their quarters.\
But the plot soon miscarried, through an unexpected accident; for another nymph,\
who was possibly seeking for her company, happened to arrive at the place, and\
seeing the panthers lying under the tree, and thinking they were wild, shot at\
them and killed one. The satyrs seeing this, came out of their lurking-hole, and\
pursued her, but she escaped by flight. They then concluded, they had waited\
long enough; and observing that it grew late, and that the aforesaid little bustle\
made the nymphs begin to stir, they in  full body of satyrs, fauni, bacchanals,\
even all the tribe of Bacchus, set up with their instruments so loud  noise, that the\
nymphs started up on  sudden; and, full of fright, looked for their cloaths: but\
being now thoroughly awaked, the term presented before them, with their hunt-\
ing equipage hanging about it. This sight, but especially that of their cloaths on\
the tree, much surprized them and put them to the blush; not knowing what\
course to take in the exigence. Not one durst approach the block in order to take\
her weapons. The vile crew all this while kept perdue, laughing at them unob-\
served. The distressed nymphs perceiving no body near them, run to and fro,\
considering how to get their cloaths again; but on their approach to the tree the\
panther arose, making so great  noise that they knew not whether to run. Cries\
and lamentations here were useless : they above an hundred times invoked the aid\
of Diana; yet in vain. The eldest, named Cleobis, at last took courage, and went\
up to the term, with intention to get the veil from it to cover Carile, who was na-\
ked ; saying,-Ahl Why are we such fools to be thus scared, and only by \
wooden black ; Why are we afhamed ; Some-body has certainly been here ;\
but now the coast is clear,  am resolved to throw it down. Come, sisters, and\
boldly give an helping hand.-\'97 But she had no sooner uttered these words, but\
all the gang appeared, mocking, scoffing and hooting : any one may determine\
who was on that overture, most dashed and concerned.  little satyr shot at the\
term, and took the quivers from it, shewing the nymphs the unseemly member,\
with an hearty laughter. This (but especially when other scoffers shewed them\
the cloaths on the tree) highly provoked them. To take to flight was not ad-\
viseable ; one pushed them this way, another that way. During this game, \
noise of cornets was heard, which suddenly put an end to the laughter ; each\
made off, leaving all things as they stood. The term of Priapus fell to the ground,\
and the panther at the tree endeavoured to get loose. Now, Diana appears, at-\
tended by her train of nymphs, who shot their arrows at the lewd crew, the dogs,\
at the same time, tearing the panther to pieces. The fearful nymphs appeared\
much afhamed, and prostrated themselves at the feet of the goddess; to whom\
they\
\
\
\
\
\
they related their misfortunes, and the affront put upon them by the gang of\
satyrs; shewing her at the same time, the term, the vizors, their cloaths on the\
tree, and what else was done in despite to them. The goddess, to shew her\
resentment, gave immediate order to pursue the rioters, and would not enlighten\
the night till she had revenged the insolence. Some accordingly made towards\
the woods, others to the brooks, and the residue took the field : in  little time,\
part of them were made captives; for of the three who pursued the nymph for\
shooting the panther, one was catched in the net, and five others, together with \
bacchanal, were hawled before Diana in irons ; whom she sentenced to be tied, two\
and two together by the feet, and whipped by the three affronted nymphs with\
thorns and holm-leaves so severely, as almost to. kill them. Three others fhe\
judged to be hung by their tails on the limbs of trees, with their heads just touch-\
ing the ground. Not yet appeased, she caused him who was taken in the net, to\
be therein plunged into the water, by two or three nymphs, till he was just ex-\
piring, and the water came out of his mouth. The bacchanal must see all this,\
on whom was bestowed an hunting knife, wherewith if she thought fit, to release\
the delinquents, to cut off their tails : which after much reluctance, she was at\
last prevailed upon to do; and then, tying their hands behind them, Diana said,\
Go now, and shew vourselves to the rest of your wanton gang, and tell them, that\
thus  will punish all those who dares to mock the chaste Diana and her retinue.\
Is not this now, though  feigned story, matter sufficient to furnisn many\
landscapes; The landscape-painter ought to observe here  representation of\
different paffions ; bashifulness in the nymphs ; wanton joy in the saytrs ; severity\
\
and resentment in the goddess and distress in the insolents.\
You see here the alluring pleasure of committing  crime, and the bashfulness\
and distress of those who suffer the evil ; but at the same time, the grievous conse-\
quences, and punishment attending wickednefs and insolence. In fine, the sweets\
and punishment of evil, and the reward and unexpected relief of virtue.\
Can it be denied, that such  representation in landscape will not generally\
please; Surely it is not impossible to make other such designs. On wnich oc-\
cafion,  hope it will not be tiresome to the reader if  now shew what is understood\
by the word (painter-like) as  very necessary point for  landscape painter.\
CHAP. XV. Of the Word Painter-like. )\
THERE is scarce any thing in the world which is not liable to  good or bad\
construction ; and judgment alone chuses in all things  medium, out of\
these two contrarities, which is certainly the most beautiful and best. This is an\
especial truth in the art of painting; which has such  power as to affect people\
two different ways : first, by virtuous and agreeable representations; and in the\
aext place, by those which. are mean, mishapen and contemptible ; both equally\
efficacious\
\
\
\
\
\
\
efficacious in contrariety.\
The former recreates and charms  judicious eye, and\
the latter is its aversion.\
It is therefore indisputable, that the painter-like, or\
most beautiful choice, implies nothing else than what is worthy to be painted ;\
and that the most mean, or what is not beautiful, least deserves that honour : as\
for instance, suppose there were brought before me  basket of ripe, unripe and\
rotten fruits mixed together;  must, having my judgment, chuse the most re-\
lishing, or those which appear most beautiful to the eye, and reject the rest.\
 landscape adorned with sound and straight grown trees, round bodied and\
finely leaved, spacious and even grounds, with gentle ups and downs, clear and\
still rivers, delightful vistos, well ordered colours, and an agreeable blue sxy,\
with some small driving clouds; also elegant fountains, magnificent houses and\
palaces, disposed according to the rules of architecture, and richly ornamented;\
likewise, well-shaped people agreeable in their action; and each coloured\
and draperied according to his quality ; together with cows, sheep, and other\
well-fed cattle. All these,  say, may claim the title of painter-like ; but \
piece with deformed trees, widely branched and leaved, and disorderly spreading\
from east towards west, crooked bodied, old and rent, full of knots and hollow-\
nefs; also rugged grounds without roads or ways, sharp hills, and monstrous\
mountains silling the off-scape, rough or ruined buildings with.- their parts lying\
up and down in confusion; likewise muddy brooks,  gloomy sky, abounding\
with heavy clouds; the field furnished with lean cattle and vagabonds of gypsies :\
such  piece,  say, is not to be called  fine landscape. Can any one, without\
reason, asfert him to be  painter-like object, who appears as \'e0 lame and dirty\
beggar, cloathed in rags, splay-footed, bound about the head with  nasty clout,\
having  skin as yellow as  backed pudding, killing vermin; or in fine, any\
fuch paltry figure  Would younot rather conclude suchthings to be the jest of\
 painter \'e0\
For my part, Ibefieve that the difference between the fine and the ugly, is too\
great not to make  distinction between them. Iam well pleased, that some call\
the works of Bamboccio, Brouwer, and Moller, and the landscapes of Brueghel, Bril,\
Bloemart Savry, Berchem, and such masters, painter-like : but  oppose to them,\
Raphael, Correggio, Poussin, Le Brun, &. and in landscape, Albani, Genouille,\
On this occasion,  shall, before  conclude, also- consider the word designer-like,\
 word which is as much perverted as the other : for instance; crooked trees\
abounding with knots and hollownesses, rugged clods of earth, bursten and sharp\
rocks, human bodies robustly and roughly muscled in Michael. Angelo\'92 manner,\
faces large featured, long nosed, wide mouthed, hollowe eyed like Tefta\'92. These\
objects we have extolled for designer-like, though as absurdly and improperly, as\
The masters therefore are very imprudent, who encourage their-disciples to\
seck and draw in so troublesome  way, after such objects, as tending to nothing\
else than learning them to make out-lines. Do they nor ehuse  round-about-\
way to bring them into the right path; Nay, How many die in the purfuit,\
who, had they taken the other way, might easily have got throught Where-\
fore,\
\
\
\
\
\
\
fore, it is more adviseable to draw after the beautiful and sedate fimplicity and\
greatness of Raphael, Poussin, and other excellent mafters, than after any of those\
other paltry and mis-shapen objects. This must be agreed, thar if the bad and\
deformed be painter or designer-like, the beautiful is not so, the case admits of\
II\
no alteration; and consequently the worst must be best, and the best worst.\
both be good, there is no room for choice ; and you may at that rate mingle\
beauty with deformity, joy with sorrow, ripenness with unripeness, gods with\
beggars : but since beauty is attracting, and deformity offensive, this certainly is\
true painter-like, which supposes the bestand most agreeable objects; which alone\
ought to be called fo, and sought for.\
Yet there are occasions, wherein both must be observed; either that the story\
requires it, or that, by means of deformity, we are to set off what is beautiful,\
and make it predominate : but theu the painter who understands beauty, may\
more easily abate, than the other exalt himself above his knowledge and capacity.\
Wheresore  conclude, that beautiful nature is the best choice, and the most\
painter-like.\
 shall now for the benefit of such artists as are not rich in invention, give \
compendious description of variety of objects in  fictitious view.\
CHAP. XVI. Of Painter-like Beauty in the open Air.\
HE dav was almost shut in, and the agreeable western sun giving long and\
charming ground-shades, when  purposed to divert myself with  walk ;\
not without reflecting, how many fine observables-are overlooked, which if hand-\

thoughts to six on things of most importance.\
where nothing was-wanting which could tend to the repose of the mind; every\

At proper distances, along both sides of the road, were placed for the ease of\

CHAP. XVII. Of Things Deformed. and Broken, falsely called\
Painter-like.\
HANGING the scene, we shall now consider what is also, though unjust-\
 ly, called painter-like ; and this in an imaginary way, like the pre-\
ceding.\
In walking,  saw  large gate, the door whereof was broken to pieces by an\
huge oak blown down against it. Creeping through it,  found myself as in \
strange country, so very rugged, desolate and rocky, without paths or roads, that\
 knew not where to walk ; the ground was no where so even as to reston. Here\
 saw the fragment of  column ; yet, lying so obliquely, that  could not sit on\
it : near it lay  piece of frize and cornice, with an end sticking up; and not\

vermin.  nevertheless endeavoured to get upon this last stone; and then, with\
my cloak under me, laid myself down upon it. Which  had no sooner done,\
but somebody called,\'97-Hark ye ; go from it ; you lie in my way.\
, not\
dreaming any person could be here, suddenly looked back in surprize, and sawa\

laid on. But, on recollection, he again called out, that, if  would stay there\
but half  quarter of an hour,  fhould do him  great favour. This  consented\
to, not without afking him, What he was going to do with such paltry fragments  answered, ee They are the finest things in the world to. introduce into our\
pieces. When  have such  fine parcel as that piece of  column, and this water\
before me, with the addition of  stump of  tree, and  small dark off-scape be-\

here now. \'92\
\

\

Upon this prattle,  viewed him from top to toe ; he sat all in an heap, with \

The truth is, the more  viewed him and heard his talk, the more  blamed my\

CHAP.  Of Portraits in general.\
SINCE we meet with no presidence in the art, nor pretend to insist\
on ceremonies, we shall treat of things as they occur to -us, and as :\
\
clearly and profitably as possible.\
But first, give me leave to say, that  have often wondered how any man\
can, prefer slavery to liberty, and, by departing from the essence of the art,-\
subject\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
Of Portraiture.\
subject himself to all the defects of nature.  speak of such great masters as\
Van Dik, Lely, lan Loo, the old and young Bakker, and others, who though pos-\
sessed of great talents in the art, postponed what is noble and beautiful, for what\
is nore ordinary and common. The truth is, and we have seen, that sooner by\
this means than others, men have obtained the honour of gold medals and chains,\
&. Nay, the liberty of prescribing laws to princes ; staring them in the face,\
drawing their pictures, and many other privileges, whereby they have acquired\
great riches. What an unheard-of reward did not Apelles receive, when Alexan-\
der gave him his dear Campaspe, in order to save the life of that great artist, by\
satisfvinghis love, inslamed by drawing the picture of that beauty  When \
consider these things,  am surprised, that all painters do not give in wholy to\
pertraiture ; since now--days money is preferred to learning, lucre to virtue, and\
honours dispensed to men in proportion to their riches. But, leaving this sub-\
ject, we will proceed thoroughly to consider every thing relating to that branch\
of the art.\
As in music and singing  good ear is requisite, so in portraiture it is impossible\
to excel without  good eyc ; such an one,  mean, as is governed by sedate and\
sober sensation, and not by self-love or passion. Next is required  regular design,\
containing an exact proportion or division of the parts, not only of the face, but\
of the whole body, that the sitter may be known by his picture, which may be\
most agreeably done by mixing the fafhion with what is painter-like; as the great\
Lely did, and which is called the painter-like or antique manner, but by the ig-\
norant commonality, the Roman manner.\
Next, we must be thoroughly judicious in the graceful choice of the light, and\
the place where the person is to sit, that the face may appear to the best advan-\
tage-; and then the body is to be disposed to the most natural and becoming\
posture.\
The next business, and which gives it the greatest lustre, is, the colouring, that\
each person and his parts may have their proper colour, and such as appears in\
his daily converse ; not such as proceeds from extraordinary emotions. Let the\
artist beware of inelining to any particular manner, like some, whose work is\
thereby better known to be theirs, than the friends of the sitter know the picture\
to be his.\
As for the choice of light, in order to apply it most advantageously for the be\'ac\
nefit of either sex, it is certainly  matter of great moment; since the fair sex\
commonly partake of more delicacy and grace than men, so they must have \
light as beautiful and agreeable as their persons.\
But ere we proceed further, it will not be improper to look into the origin of\
portraits, in order thereby to shew-the aim of those who cause themselves to be\
drawn, and the profits which masters get thereby.\
The ancients used to cause those, from whom the Common-wealt had received\
extraordinary benefits, either in war or civil affairs, or for imminence in religion,\
to be represented in marble or metal, or in  picture, that the sight of them, by\
those honours, might be  spur to posterity to emulate the same virtues. This\
honour\
\
\
\
\
\
\
honour was first begun with their Deities ; afterwards it was paid to heroes, and\
of consequence to philosophers, orators, religious men, and others, not only to\
But now\
perpetuate their virtues, but also to embalm their names and memories.\
it goes further ;  person of any condition whatsoever, have he but as much mo-\
ney as the painter afks, must sit for his picture : this is  great abuse, and sprung\
from as laudable  cause.\
In noblemen indeed it is  very commendable custom; because, being descend-\
ed from great families, the lustre of these ought to shine, to encourage their suc-\
cessors to keep up their glory, and to prevent sullying it by unworthy actions.\
As for \'e0 general, or admiral, who has died in the bed of honour, gratitude, \
think, obliges us to raise  monument to his glory, and to animate brave souls\
in future times, to immitate his virtue. But what is this to the vulgar ; pride on-\
ly spurs them to it. The rich do it that their children may boast of it; the\
master of  numerous family does it, that the world may know he is  father;\
he who has fired  magazine of the enemy must be drawn, with this great ac-\
tion, though perhaps tnere was no body to hinder him. Has  citizen\'92 wife but\
an only babe, he is drawn at half  year old ; at ten years old he sits again,\
and, for the last time, in his twenty-fifth year, in order to shew her tender folly ;\
and then she stands wondering how  man can so alter in that time. Is not this\
 weighty reason;  reproveable custom, if painters did not gain by it. But\
again, portraits are allowable, when  lover is absent from his mistress, that they\
may send each other their pictures, to cherisn and increase their loves ;  man\
and wife so parted may do the same.\
But to return to the original matter;  must warn the artists not to give in too\
much to what is common; or humour ignorant people so much, as not to re-\
serve to themselves some liberty of doing what they think proper for the sake of\
reputation : surely this cannot be strange advice; for  master who prefers money\
before art has no more dangerous  rock to split on, since the ignorant multi\'ac\
tude usually insist to be drawn according to their own whims. One says \'92to \'e0\
good master,\'97te Draw me thus, or thus; let me have one hand on my breast,\
and the other on  table;\'92 another must have  flower in his hand, or  flower-\
pot must be by him; another must have  dog, or other creature, in his lap;\
another will have his face turned this or that way ; and some who would be drawn\
in the Roman manner, must be set off by  globe or clock on  table, whether\
such ornaments be proper or not. On mentioning the Roman manner,  find that\
it signifies  loose airy undress, somewhat savouring of the mode, but in no wise\
agreeing with the ancient Roman habit.\
But many other inconveniences attend portraiture ; as first, the ignorance of\
those who sit; for some of them, having no right notion of their own mein and\
shape, often refer the judgment of  fine portrait to the eye of achild, or servant;\
and what they say, Monsieur and Madam believe, either to its praise, or dis-\
commendation.\
 second inconveniency arises from  wedded inclination which any one has to\
fuch and such objects; judging, as they like or dislike, not only of pictures, but\
even the life itself ; for though they may be afraid to pass sentence on \'e0 fine\
history\
\
\
\
\
\
\
hiftory or landscape, yet  portrait must not escape them, as thinking it within the\
reach of their capacities.\
Thirdly, we nnd many artists never pleased with other mens works, but being\
full of themselves despise every thing they see, though as good as their own; and\
this perhaps on no better bottom than  pique against the artist\'92 conversation,\
talk, dress, or money ; or else because of his greater fame: and yet if ten per-\
sons happen to applaud  fine picture of this eye-sore master, they will at that\
juncture, chime in with them, to sereen their prejudice. And on the contrary,\
if but  single person afterwards find fault, they immediately turn the tables against\
ten others. Again, if apiece of their friend be brought on the carpet, though\
never so faulty, they will applaud and justify it at any rate, though against their\
own convictions of conscience. But this partial and prejudiced humour is most\
prevailent in those who know least.\
 fourth set of men are those, who, being always of an unealy temper, dislike\
their own, but applaud every thing other men do: these indeed are not so noxious\
as the former, because they only hurt themselves, whereas the others hurt every\
body.\
Fifthly, there are  prejudiced set of men who find no taste but in easv and-\
grave airs and postures; others in stirring and hurrying ones ; others in violent:\
ones : some think that womens draperies ought to be loose and soft; others will\
have them of velvet or fattin, or else party-coloured : this thinks that  dark or\
brown ground beft sets off  figure ; anether chuses  landscape, or green curtain,\
right or wrong. Are the- colours beautifully chosen, the picture smells of them;\
are they broken, they seem muddy and foui. How can  portait please so many\
opinions ? It is not like an history full of figures, where we can introduce va-\
riety of sedate and stirring action, more or less beautiful colouring, loose or set\
draperies, dark or light grounds, &. Because this is but  single figure.\
Our businefs then, must be to find  way between soylla and charybdis, to enable\
the artist to paint  good portrait ; for he who makes due reffexion on every thing,\
can prepare himself to overcome the aforesaid difficulties.\
CHAP. II. Of the Defects in the Face and other Parts.\
HE defects, which are seen in nature, or in simple life, are three-\
fold.\
 Natural ones.\
 Accidental ones.\
 Useful ones.\
The natural ones are,  wry face, fquint eyes, wry mouth, nose, &.\
The accidental ones are, loss of an eye,  cut on the cheek or other part of the\
face; pits of the small pox, and the like.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
The\'92usual ones are, those. habitsito-which we aecustom gurselves from- our in\'ac\
faney ;. to wit, contraction of the eyes and. mouth, or closing, or gaping of the\
latter, or drawing. it in. somewhat to this or that side, upwards or down-\
wards, &.\
As for-otherbodily infirmities; how many have wry necks, hunch backs, bandy\
legs, withered or short arms, or one shorter than the other ; dead or lame hands\
or fingers ? Among these, some are unavoidable, and others may be either left\
out; or handsomely hidden. The necessary ones ought to be secn, because they\
help the likeness; such as  wry face, squint eyes, low forehead, thinnefs and\
fatness,  wry neck, too short or too long  nose, wrinkles between the eyes,-\
ruddiness onpaleness of the cheeks, or lips, pimples or warts about the mouth,\
and such like: among those which may be hidden or left out,  count  blind\
eye,  wound, wen, mole, pits of small pox, too many pimples, &.  red, blue,\
oghairy spot; as also habital usages; such as- hanging lips, pinchings or draw-\
ingsof the-mouth and eyes.\
Ithink also, that the common and useful dress of  person is  great addition\
te-likenefs ; for no sooner is the dress altered, but the look does the same, and\
shews itselfeither more or lefs pleasing and agreeable ; and thereby the person be-\
comes more tor lefs known : to obviate which,  advise the artist above all things:\
to get first  true likeness of the face, and paint it. to the sitter\'92 satisfaction; and\
then he may freely manage all the rest as he thinks fit, and thereby get honour\
and commendation ; since the life itself in such  dress, cannot any more alter.\
The painter should likewise discover and- know, as much as possible; the nature\
and temper of the person sitting, and in what circumstances lies his favourite plea-\
sure ; that he may, when sitting, be entertained with talk pleasing to him, and\
his air thereby kept steady and serene, and his posture natural and easy; avoiding\
every thing tending towards sorrow or frightful relations ; for these are apt to ruffle\
the mind, and so to discompose the face, that it cannot easily be got right again :\
but if the sitter himself do by his talk, difcover his own bent, the painter ought\
to humour it to the last, whether if-be jocose or moderate, without exaggeration-\
or diminution ; yet with such  variety, as not to prove tiresome, and make the\
face alter.\
He who cannot thus-manage and furnish outa discourse, will be the longer ere\
he hit the likeness. Some will even sit three or four times, and each time with -\
different air; and were they to sit ten times;  fancy something new would still\
offer.\
Another hindrance may be, that painting-rooms are often hung with such sinutty\
But\
pictures as frequently put virgins to the bluss, or alter their countenances.\
though for improvement, fine pictures are necessary to be always in view, yet in \

the sight without disturbing or tireing the mind, or altering the countenance ;\

such methods as these  painter may become great.\
We will now proceed to consider how many mistakes some painters commit in\
relation to the first observation of natural defects; these endeavour to their utmost\
power, to express punctually the deformities and defects of  face, without\
scruple, to wit,  blind or squint eye, or the like, though they know that it is an\

What would become of grace, which teaches, that  painter-\
fully hidden ;\
should make as beautiful  choice as possible ; which these blemisnes obscure. \
think therefore, that we cannot lay too great  strefs on what concerns the make,\
position and turn of  face, that the eye be not offended with blemisn, or deformi-\
ty, or the posture look disagreeable.\
How monstrous is the picture of  certain admiral, who seems to stab himself\
with his staff of command, and has  defective eye turned directly to the light; be-\
cause, according to the saying, he is best known by it. Would not -more pro-\
file view have suited him better, or to have flung the side with the blind eye into\
fhade ; Would it not be ridiculous to paint the Duke of Luxemburgh in profile, to\
represent him the better, and that his hunch-back might be the more vifible, for\
no other reason, than that most people knew he had one )\
Nature abhors deformity, and we cannot behold it without aversion, and \
quick turn of the eye from it :  squint-eyed person cannot see himself in the glass\
without inward trouble ; especially one of the fair sex, who; in other respects,\
tolerably handsome, cannot bear to see an instance of her deformity in another,\
but will basfifully look off or down to the ground. How much worie then must-\
it look in  picture ; The life may be sometimes seen on an handsome side; which\
in an ill chosen picture we can never expect : whence it is natural for one who has\
 blemish or defect in an eye or cheek, always to turn the best side to. the light-\
In short, we do not desire to do any thing, walk, stand; fit, talk, but with  be-\
coming air. Have we fore eyes, we hide them under our hats ; or if  lame hip,\
we endeavour to walk briskly ; have we fome humour or pimples in  cheek, we\
either hide them with  patch, or paint the other side like it; have we bad teeth,\
we keep the mouth shut ; ora lame hand, and hide it not under our coats, or in\
our pockets. If nature acts so, how can such defects please in  picture ; Such \
flattery then as is agreeable to art, is not only aflowable, but commendable; es-\
pecially when the sitter is so disposed in posture, that the painter himfelf cannot\
perceive it.\

CHAP. III. The Observables in  Portrait,\
particularly that of-\
 Woman.\
ELF-CONCEIT and self-love seem nathral to all, but especially to the female\
sex ; who, whether their pictures are drawn on their own accounts, or thro\'92\
\
the desire of others, imagine they deserve such homage; nor stops it here, for\
although they may possess  tolerable share of beauty, yet that is not satisfactory\
enough; they must be flattered, and their pictures painted in the most beautiful\
light; and unhappy is the painter who abates but half  drachm of such \
beauty.\
For these reasons the master is-obliged to have  principal regard to light and\
colour; but to the light chiefly, since it is well known that nothing gives greater\
offence to ignorant people than shades, and still more, when they are strong and\
broad : they believe they speak to the purpose in objecting, Well, how can it be\
possible that my neck and cheek should have such large shades, when Idaily con-\
sult my glass, and find my skin all of  colour and white , And \'92then the painter\
is blamed. But are not such reasons weak and absurd ; Since, ifa man, how\
tenacious soever, meet another, who by long. absence and alteration of dress, is,\
got out of his memory, he will naturally turn him to-the light, in order to know\
him and his features. This conduct has been wonderfully observed by Barocci in-\
his\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
his picture- of Mary paying  visit t Elizabrth twhen big with-child; in-which, by\
his method-of placing the figures, and the ataegtion of the faces, we seem: tot\
hear them. talk, looking earnestly at each other.\
 think those masters have made the best choice who have chosen  front-lighto,\
and thereby kept.their colours most natural and beautiful ; since thig-light is,cer-\
tainly most advantageous, whether the picture hang-against  wall, or where-else.\
But here seems, to-arise .difficulty, since we formerly said, that we ought. to fix,\
 certain place, and the, point of sight and distance, and to. dispose the light so\
as it can fall on that place ; to-which the answer is easy ; porfraits have,no fixed\
place, as we shall further shew in another chapter, as also how far and-on: what.\
occasions we must- confine ourselves to that rule.\
The best way to settle this- point is, to follow those who have chosen their-light:\
almost fronting, and, as before said, such  colouring as naturally appears to the:\
eye, besides  good choice: since . think the case of placing  portrait te be-the-\
same as that of curious china, which, whether it stand high or low, shews- itself\
every where beautiful. My reason for this is, that objects, which have such .\
front-light, have an exceeding fine effect, and great relief, when, they come\
against  dark ground; and still finer when the light falls on, them somewhat\
from on high, if tha-sitter. and some accidents do not hinder it; in whigh case,\
reason and our eye must best-direct us. Viewjbut this fineness in asposture painted\
leaning over an hatch, or out at window, and what great decorum the-\
touches and shades about the most relieved parts cause in such an object; as\
Leonardo da Vinci has well observed.\
Of the accidents which  just now spoke off,  shall mention two or three ; some\
persons may be. toa long and sharp-mosed, or too hollowreyed; for such,  low\
light is most proper; but where it is otherwise, an high light : in this manner \
judicious master ought to help the defects of \'92nature, without adding to, or ta-\

is too much seen ; for; as.  have said, that history-painters chuse and follow what\
they have the greatest inclination for, so it is with many portrait-painters, their\
work is better known by their particular manner than the sitter by his picture.\
Permit me here to make  comparison between those two great masters, Titian\
and Van Dyk, with respect, to the judgment  have heard made on their works.\
Of the latter it is said, that in the delign, grace, and choice of  portrait-figure,\

But not to go abroad for comparisons, with respect to particular climes, our\

CHAP. IV. Of the Choice of Lights, Draperies, and Grounds in \
Portrait--and of the Point of Sight.\
 the preceding chapter we have laid down as  rule, that  front-light is\
the best to be chosen, and the most beautiful, especially in the fair sex; and\
 think it the more necessary when the face itself is also chosen in front, because\
then the greatest force will fall directly upon the most rising, or relieved parts ;\
but  shall now subjoin, that since the life, however we dispose it, either from or\
near the light, fronting or in profile, yet supports itself, though the light be not\
advantageously chosen, which  portrait cannot do ; we therefore must needs, in\
order to make it appear as it ought to be, accommodate the light to the dispo\'ac\
sition of the face ; for instance, when the face turns somewhat sideways, the light\
must be adapted to it; when it is quite in profile,  side-light will be best, be-\
cause\
\
\
\
\
\
\
cause then  great mafs of light remains together, to wit, in the forehead, nose\
and cheeks, which are not broken by any ground-shade, but united by the\
roundness; which shews us how to represent rising nature, and causes  becoming\
relief.\
We see that many, without difference, be the figure in full proportion, or in\
little, give the touches under the nose so black and dark, that it seems as if \
black beetle were proceeding thence ; whereas it is certain, and nature teaches it,\
that when the light falls strong on the nose, the nostrils and their ground-shades\
can never appear so black ; and yet some think they have done great feats in using\
force and strength, and will do it even in  fair and tender face, and no bigger\
than the palm of the hand, although the deepest black should not have force\
enough to shade the other objects of \'e0 darker colour, such as hair,  cloak or\
other garment; by which sort of management the face seems to jump out of the\
frame, and to desert the wig, hair, and garment. We must not so understand,\
when we teach that the face must have the main light; we mean only, that all\
ought to keep due order, that it may look natural. Each colour of the by-\
work ought, according to its lightness or darkness, to have its moderate shade\
and dark touches, as the matter it consists of is either solid or thin and transpa\'ac\
rent; and in proportion as the objects lessen, so must the force of their colours\
diminish, as shall be further illustrated in-the following chapter, to which for\
brevity we refer. We see an excellent example of this management in the fa-\
mous Netscher\'92 artful portraits, wherein he has judicioussy handled the darkest\
shades, and main-light-touches, according to the natural force of the colour.\
For the better understanding of further observables,  have found it proper to\
mention some other particulars concerning the disposition of lights, according to\
occasion, consisting in light against dark, and the contrary; and though every\
thing thereby becomes relieved, and is set off, yet that is not sufficient; for the\
placing of colours against each other on suitable grounds, and  contrast in the\
objects, whether moveable or immoveable, is of great consequence and decorum :\
and although we have handled these things at large in the Book of Colouring, yet\
we find it necessary to recapitulate them here, with respect to portraits, and the\
retiring grounds or vistos behind them.\
Observe then, whether  fair and beautiful face will become  light grey, or\
lightifh blue ground; and whether  warm complexion and strong colouring,\
against  glowing or yellowisn ground, will please the eye.  speak of the face,\
not the draperies, though both together make  portrait. But let the fair\
and beautiful face of  woman be placed against  warm ground, and then the\
light parts will not only be thereby flung off, and look more agreeable, but the\
shades will also be softened, and appear more tender; for it is unnatural to force\
 fair and tender virgin, who shews little or no motion, out of her seeming apart-\
ment-; as some by their glowing shades and reflexions have endeavoured to do;\
whereby their faces, on the shaded side, look as if  lighted candle stood behind\
them, which penetrated their skin : this is as unnatural in the open air, as with-\
in doors.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
This example of  woman is enough to prove the contrary conduct with re-\
spect to  man\'92 face, according to the aforesaid rules touching the disposition\
and placing of colours on suitable grounds; namely, that the strong ought to be\
painted against the weak, and the weak against the glowing and strong ; wherein\
is also comprehended light against dark, and dark against light.\
Whence it is evident, that back-grounds contribute very much to the charm-\
ing grace of objects; nay,  dare say, that the decorum mostly depends there-\
on : and though many imagine, that  dark or black ground always becomes \
portrait, yet it is no rule, since, as before has been said, each individual colour\
of the objects requires  particular back-ground : besides, if such things were to\
be taken for rules, the art would smell too much of an handicraft ; for  dark\
colour against  dark ground can have no good effect, and that of  white or pale\
against it will be too hard ; therefore  medium must be judicioussy observed in\
both, that one colour may suit with the other. In the draperies the conduct is\
the same; one person best becomes light, and another dark cloaths ; blue suits\
one, and red, yellow, or green, &. another: the artist must then take care not\
to force Nature, but help her as much as posfible, and represent her always most\
beautiful.\
If any one would know my reason for thinking that many errors arise in this\
part of the art, it is, that the colours of the naked receive more or less, or too\
much force by the by-colours of grounds and back-ornaments : It fares with\
them as it is said of the camelion, who changes his colour as often as he is pla\'ac\
ced by different colours; though this is occasioned by his elegant and shining\
scales, when, contrarily, the human skin is dull and not shining. However we\
shall find, that he who paints  portrait twice, and each time on  contrary\
ground, yet with the same temperament of colours, will perceive  very great,\
nay, incredible difference : as . have on several occasions experimented in the\
life ; to wit, that when some virgins were in  room hung with yellow, they look-\
ed sickly and grey, notwithstanding their fresh colour ; but, contrarily, being in\
 room hung with violet, their colours shewed themselves very beautiful ; whence\
it appears, that the alterations are oftentimes occasioned by the adjacent objects.\
Let him who doubts this make trial of it in  portrait, by laying  ground with\
water-colours on paper, and after the face is cut out, placing it against the pic-\
ture instead of  back-ground. But  think there is  convenient way of pre-\
venting the aforesaid alteration; namely, by fixing against the wall, behind the\
sitter,  garment, cloth, or something else of the same colour, or near it, which\
we chuse for our back-ground: thus we may be sure of hitting the right colour,\
and make the painting look agreeable.\
In order to represent an extensive back-ground, and chiesly in  small picture,\
be it an apartment or landscape, some shadiness should be contrived between the\
figure and the distance, as  column, curtain, body of  tree, vase, &. These\
objects being in shade, or of  dark colour, the lights falling on the off-works\
will not prejudice either the face or drapery, though both be light; but, on the\
contrary, the figure, as receiving the foremost and greatest light, will thereby be\
relieved, and look more agreeable.\

As for the draperies, fince they confift of different and various colours, each\
of  particular nature, and littie agreeing with the colour of the face, they also\
require each \'e0 particular ground, beft suiting and uniting with it; to the end\
that, though differing among themselves, they may have  perfect harmony with\
each other, so that the eye be not taken alone with the face, or the draperies, or-\
naments or by-works; but, by this sweet conjunction, insenfibly, conducted all\
over the picture.\
It will not be improper to treat also about easiness and sedateness in posture,\
opposed to stir and bustle, and the contrary : namely, that the picture of \
gentlewoman of repute, who, in  grave and sedate manner turns towards that of\
her husband hanging near it, gets  great decorum by moving and stirring hind-\
works, whether by means of waving trees or crossing architecture of ftone, or\
wood, or any thing else that the master thinks will best contrast, or oppose the\
sedate posture of his principal figure. And because these are things of conse-\
quence, and may not be plainly apprehended by every one,  shall explain my-\
felf by examples in plate LVII. touching the elegance and harmony of back-\
grounds with the figures.\
In No.   reprefent  beautiful face against the light of the ground ; and\
the drapery, which is white, or of light colouring, against the dark of it; these\
oppofitions thus meeting, produce  sweet mixture above, and below an agreeable\
relief or rising of the under part of the body ; whereas, were it otherwise, the\
face, as but  small part of the body, would look too fharp and disagreeable,\
and the under part of the body would have no force.\
In No.  being the portrait of  man of  more warm and swarthy complexion,\
we see the reverse of the former, because his colour, and that of his dress, are\
of  different nature ;, yet the ground is very ornamental, and each sets off the\
No.  shews  man with  drunken face of red, purple and violet, and some-\
what brown and darkisn; which is set off by  white marble or light stone ground,\
and gives it  fine air.\
In No.  is an example of the contrast in off-works with the drapery of the\
figure ; shewing the opposition of moveable objects with fixed ones ; for herein\
are seen rounding and crossing folds against straight and parallel off-works. And,\
In No.  appears the reverse ; where the folds hang straight and mostly dovn-\
wards, and the off-works crofs them.\
No.  gives us an example of the opposing action and posture of bodies in two\
fellow-portraits ; for the man; being on the woman\'92 right side, turns his face\
sideways towards her; his body is fronting, receiving the light from the right\
side.\
In Ne.  we see the contrary in the woman\'92 posture ; her face is fore-right,\
and her body sways sideways towards the man; she also is lighted from the right\
side.\
The figures numbered  and  represent also, yet in  different manner, the\
contrast in the motion; for the woman, standing on the right side of the man,\
has\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
has  sedate motion, and set and hanging shoulders: but the man, contrarily, is\
in active motion. And,\
No.  shews  proper method to exhibit  great extent, or seemingly such, in\
 small piece ; for the figure stands in  strong light; the by-ornaments, viz.\
curtain, vase, pillar and walling, are in shade; and the off-scape or hind-work is-\
light again, but somewhat broke by reason of its distance.\
To conclude this chapter, Ishall say something of the placing of portraits ;\
and of their point of sight.\
As to the former, it is certain that when we see any painted figure, or object in-\
 place where the life can be expected, as standing on the ground, leaning over\
 balcony or ballustrade, or out at window, &. it deceives the eye, and by being\
seen unawares, causes sometimes  pleasing mistake ; as it frightens and surprises\
others, when they meet with it unexpectedly at such places as aforesaid, and\
where there is  likelihood for it. If we are thus misled by  representation of\
nature, how great must the master be who did it  The knowing esteem him, ig-\
norants cry him up: if this be the case, we ought to endeavour to follow nature\
and likelihood, and principally to observe the rules of perspective ; for who can-\
doubt, that  standing, sitting, or moving figure, artfully painted, and placed as,\
aforesaid, will not have the same effect as the life itself ?\
Hence it follows, that low horizons, or points of sights, are the best and most.\
natural in  portrait, and will most deceive the senses, if the light and distance,\
with respect to the place where the picture is to be set, be well observed;, other-\
wise the effect will be contrary to what we expect.\
This conduct is chiesly necessary in portraits hanging high ; for being. so much\
above the eye, they must needs have  low horizon. But as portraits are move-\
able, how natural and like soever they be, and well handled, if they hang not in.\
proper places, they will not have  good effect : hence the mischief attending them.\
is, that by continually changing their places, they cannot always be painted\
to  certain height and distance, and consequently baffle our rule :  difficulty\
which the greatest masters must struggle with, and this branch of the art is\
liable to.\
Having now shewn that  low horizon and point of-sight are best and most na-\
tural, as supplying, in some measure, this inconveniency : How much must they\
mistake, who always chuse anhigh horizon, They are on  level with the sitter,\
and yet place the horizon manyfeet higher; nay, they think, those who do other-\
wise act against nature and art. Some will have two points of sight in one piece,\
one for the figure, another for the ornaments; one level with the eye, and the\
other for the off-scape; one hand higher or lower at pleasure, or about three or\
four fingers breadth above the middle. Although these are inexcusable errors, yet\
 think it vain to attempt their redress; but hope the judicious artist will weigh\
what Ihave said, and endeavour to avoid them.\

CHAP. . Of Portraits in Little.\
THERE are many things, as  have formerly shewed, which if we will have \
them transport the senses by their natural representation, we must always\
exhibit in their natural proportion and force of colouring: but in  portrait it is\
otherwise ; for this may as well be done in little, as in full proportion, provided\
the diminution be well observed ; and besides, it has some relation to historical\
management. We could say the same of  little flower compared with  great\
one ; for if it were coloured in proportion to its distance and diminution, it-would\
be in the same case with  portrait in little:. But it is nevertheless certain, that in\
festoons, garlands, flower-pots in niches, groups of flowers, &. serving for\
ornament of chambers, little slowers are of small account ; nay, never seen wove\
or embroidered in any stuff: whence some may be induced to think, that  por-\
trait in little, as big as the palm\'92of the hand, has as little property in  square\
against the wall, especially when it receives its light from without the frame, and\
is handled with as much force as the life itself; which, with respect to force, Ial\
low : nevertheless,  small portrait may, in order to make it look more natural,\
be more easily helped, than small flowers placed against  door, window or other\
flat; which, in my opinion, can in no wise be made good; but  small portrait\
may, as  shall prove in what follows : in order to which the artist must previously\
consider.\
First, How much the life in proportion diminishes; and consequently, how\
faint it must be.\
Secondly, that the picture cannot receive its light from without the frame, as\
being too far from it.\
Here, perhaps, it may be afked, Whether  portrait of  lady or gentleman\
leaning out at window, in the manner of old Mieris, Mezzu, Vonder, Neer, and\
others, would not be good and natural ,:  say, Yes. But then the window\
must also go back ; for as it would be difficult to represent its- going back from\
the frame, since no object, whether ceiling-or sloor, &. is between them, in\
order to create distance, and make the picture fall back ; something may be intro-\
duced in full proportion, to shew the depth and distance, according to the differ-\
ence to be seen in the following instances in plate LVIII.\
In No.  we see  common fault in the figure leaning on aframe : in this there\
is no other way to make the figure go baek, than by taking away the frame. And,\
although,\
No.  shews itself within the frame, yet it would be to no purpose, did we\
not assign  sufficient large breadth or thickness to the frame ; for in such case, we\
must not regard an hand\'92 breadth of cloth, whereon to repiresent something in full\
proportion, as an orange, flute, book, &. Yet,\
No.  shews  good method, and in my judgment, the best and most natural.\
 remember,:\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
 remember, amongst the paintings of  certain lover, to have seen one of \
Doctor with an urinal in his hand, thrusting his arm out at window, so that the\
shade of it, and the glitter of the water plainly appeared on the sill of the window.\
Next the window  maid-servant was seen standing at the door, speaking to \
woman in the street with  child in her arins : some other figures appeared in the\
front of the picture, seen to the shoulders only, as if standing in the street. On\
the sill of the window were lying  bottom of blue worsted stuck with needles,\
also  pair of sciffors,  piece of dark blue cloth, and  thimble, all in full pro\'ac\
portion : to be short, this picture was by an artist, with the owner\'92 leave, sen-\
tenced to be docked; in order to which, he drew  square chalk-line round the\
window, which contained the Doctor, and cut away all the rest round about it,\
hitting here an head, there an arm, without sparing any body but the Doctor,\
who was instantly put into  smaller frame : thus the piece was half cut away,\
and for noother reason, as the artist pretended, than that the Doctor alone was\
sufficient to satisfy the eye, the rest being superfluous.  wretched fate for so\
good alpicture  But some painters will keep the old road, because it is difficult\
to correct  rooted evil : they do as the old woman did, who being exhorted in\
her last sickness to embrace the true faith, answered,\
ce She would follow the steps\
of her fore-fathers, were they all gone to the devil.  it is with  portrait in little, which has nothing of nature but the features,\
and looks like  puppet ; whereas there are well-known methods to make it appear\
as big as the life; nay, to move and speak, as  may say : but being slighted,\
the figure seems immoveable, dumb and little, and therefore unnatural.\
On this footing, Imean to shew, that all things may be naturally represented\
in little, except  moon-light, which baffles all our fkill.\
Now, if itbe afked, Whether too nice an expression of parts in  small portrait,\
would not be superfluous and unnatural, with respect to distance, and whether less\
finishing would not be better ;  say, No; provided it be not so strong and warm\
as the life ; for the figure not being exhibited in open field, it cannot have so much\
mistiness and vapour about it : and therefore the neat pencilling cannot be ob-\
structive, especially if managed with sxill, as the principal parts well touched,\
and the tender and melting smallness in the broad parts the same, so that at the\
proper distance the one is seen more, and the other less.\
Here may arise another question : Whether such pictures are not of the same\
nature with what is seen through  prospective-glass, since every thing appears so\
plain, elaborate and neat \'e0 But  answer, that they are not, nor can ; because\
the glafs exhibits the life without the interpofition or mistiness or vapours, and\
with strong and warm shades, which overcome its smallness.\
 have often wondered at such small paintings, because they seemed as if  was\
looking in  Nuremberg looking-glass, or through  prospective, since they appeared\
not like the life, but little moving puppets.\
Now, another difficulty is, that since such paintings cannot, according to our\
position, be made good without the addition of some by-ornament, as imagery in\
whole or half figures, vine-branches about the frame, or something lying on it, in\
order to fling them off, it would be hard for those who can only paint  whole or\
half\

half length figure, and aim at nothing else than to become masters therein, where-\
as, he who is better versed, may by  due observance of what has been said, easily\
overcome the before-mentioned difficulties.\
CHAP. VI. Of the Application of Requisites with respect to the different\
Conditions of Persons.\
 will not be foreign to our main design, to put the artist in mind of the appli\'ac\
cation and right use of such materials as may enrich  portrait, and make it\
look the more noble. This is so great  point in portraiture, that when well\
known, we need never be at \'e0 stand through the mishape or defects we often\
meet with in the disposition of  portrait, and which sometimes must not be hid ;\
since we have often means enough for obviating them with seeming reason, and\
without foreing nature ; as  long and narrow face may be helped by  hood, or\
other head-dress;  thick and too round  face by the contrary :  figure too\
lonesome may be embellished with  pillar, pedestal, slower-pot, table, and such\
things as are proper to it, which serve nor only for ornament and grandeur, but\
also to express the sitter\'92 lustre and virtue : but care must be taken that the figure\
of the sitter, as the principal object of the piece, sill up the major part of it, either\
by  spreading sway of the posture, or by the addition of some proper by-work ;\
by which means it will have  good effect.\
Since it is certainthat the vices as well as virtues have two powerful qualities,\
and though contrary to each other, yet both tend to good purpose; nay,  wicked\
person, may, by  virtuous example, be rescued from evil ; and  virtuous per-\
son, through bad example, led into error and ruin ; but virtue being joined to vir-\
tue, fears no evil ; contrarily, the evil will make us avoid evil. So pictures\
should create an ardour for virtue, and especially those of religious and good\
persons : since this, as we have said in Chap. . gave the first rise to their repre-\
sentations, in order to perpetuate their memories, as well as their virtues and\
glorious actions.\
To come then the better to this excellent point; let us by noble by-works make\
them with their persons, in  conspicuous manner. Wherefore  shall lay down\
some examples, though drawn from heathen story.\
Among the Heathens, some were so virtuously endowed, that they need not yield\
to christanity itself ; Lucretia and Penelope in chastity; Cato in steadiness and cou-\
rage; and many others whom we shall for brevity omit, to pass to the sketching\
chamber, or other apartment; and an example of  chaste virgin shall be that of\
Lucretia. It is said, to her honour, that she was descended of  noble family,\
and so virtuously educated, that she delighted only in that. Now, whether we\
represent\
\

represent her living or dead in that character, we may adorn her apartment with\

chastity and honour. It statues, or houschold goods be necessary, let them be\
Pallas, Diana, Hymen, and especially Vesta : her bed may be ornamented with\
Chastity and Stedfastness ; and on her couch may be seen some Cupids lighting\
each others torches, or playing with palm-branches and olive-leaves : the apart-\
ment may be here and there furnissied with gold and silver vases, cups and other\
house-plate, wrought with virtuous significations ; but herein, care must be taken\
not to introduce any thing foreign to the matter, or against history, which ought\
to be consulted.\
The handling this example may sufficiently usher in the method of treating\
others, such as of Julius Casar, Augustus, Marcus, Aurelius, Crasus, Solon, Seneca,\
&. and contrarily, the stories of Sardanapalus, Semiramis, Faustina, Phalaris, &.\
As for  cruel prince, or tyrant, either in his court, apartment or other place,\

master, like man.\

CHAP. VII. Of the Saiting of Colours in Draperiex.\
 \
HE\'92 suiting of colours in-portraits c\'f4mes: now before us, \'e0 matten\

Many think, though without ground, that deep red best becomes  red-faced\

strange, black and dark colours,  swarthy persou : but this must be rediculous\

fillemot. Yellow or fallow-faced persons durst not wear red ; or red-faced ones,\
green : but enongh of this. Let us now return to what sober art dictates.\
Beginning with the head and its hair,  say, that deep or strong colours, such\
as deep red, deep yellow, deep blue, &. best fit  person who has brown hair.\
Those who have fair hair, beft become half or weak colours, such as purple,\
lightblue, violet, green and rose-colour.\
 yellow-haired person best becomes violet, blue, and whitissi yellow, as masti-\
