the hair, the stronger the drefs.\
He is  prudent master who well knows how to express in his pictures the dif-\
ferent naturez and complexions of people, and to distinguish persons full of fpirit\
and fire from the meek and dead-hearted ; the sickly and weak from the healthy\
and strong; as to whom we may use draperies of the following colours : with the\
red or firey fuit best draperies of half or broken colours, with little red in them ;\
the pale suits no yellow, or other pale colour; the fallow the same ; but white is\
very agreeable : brown complexions become not dark or strong, but white and\
all light colours. The Blacks love white above any colour, and think no dress\
better beconses them than  light coloured one; and not without reason; for,\
Would not  black man with black. or dark cloaths be frightful to look at ; And\
how ridiculous it is for  pale virgin, to dress in all sorts of light and pa\'efe-colours,\
in order to look less pale ; and that one who is red, wear nothing bat red for the\
same re\'e2son. Hereby, mstead ofhiding  small fault, the mafter would commit\
 greater; and yet this is the common notion. But if  may give my opinion, \
red and fiery faced person, dressed in red, seems to me like  red painted statue ;\
and  pale looking person in  light or yellow dress, as sick or dead. Wherefore,\
if we would be artful, we must manage otherwise, to wit, that those whom we\
would\

\

lively colours for  sick or unhealthy person; and weak and faintonesfora healthy\

the art teaches; for she is not deficient : but as the drapery sometimes over-rules,\

Since it is an undoubted truth, that we can perform nothing but what passes\

Natura incipit, ars dirigit, experientia docet.\
That is,\
Nature points out the way, which arts improve,\
And settled practice makes  picture move.\

The like heedless mistake we see in the copies of manydisciples and young\

Although it is commendable to follow great masters in general, yet it is  fault\
to dwell upon some of their particulars, as an ornament, urn, vase, term, &. with-\

for our practice; but this is weakness; since art and nature have such  fund of\
objects, and our time for learning and living is long enough, and by consulting\
within ourselves we may spur pur genius.\
Others commit the same fault by  contray impulse ; for so violent is their in-\

think it lost time to employ their thoughts on the works of any other good artist,\
and being thus wedded to him, they wishi, drudge and plod to be like him as well\
in errors as perfections; by this means, and by  punctual imitation of blunders\
and mistakes, it sometimes happens, that the copy and the original are not to be\
distinguisned, both being so wounderfully like each other; nay, their own pro-\
ductions are taken for copies. For,  tyro of good ability may at first use him-\
self to  good manner of designing, which he ought to be master of, before he\
takes to painting, and to understand this well before he proceeds to ordonnances ;\
and then, if he have  thorough knowledge of the latter, he will paint  good\
picture. Yet it is often seen, tnat his work is but taken for that of  pupil, nay\
worse than  copy; and what, is still lefs, it is not like his own manner or hand-\
ling: and Why ; such artists, being advanced thus far, endeavouring to produce\
their\

CHAP. . Of Archit\'e9cture in general.\
AFTER having handlad sol. many diffegent parts of the art, we meet\
\
also with architecure ; an art full of noble performances and fine usez\
But our purpose is not to insist on all its advantages, or give  system\
\
of it ; since such  work would be too tedious, and calls for Homer\
or Virgil\'92 eloquence : and having been copioussy handled by several\
learned pens, we shall treat noffurther of it than what concerns  painter, leaving\
the rest to architects.\
An ingenious history painter, if he would be universal, must needs understand\
architecture and statuarry ; because he will otherwise be at  nonplus in some\
things ; he ought even to be as knowing as an architect, and how to order  good\
building, though it is an architect\'92 daily practice, and \'92ghat but  part of the\
painter\'92.\
It is certain that the human body is, in its symmetry, proportion, majesty\
and grace, the most perfect pi\'e9ce of work in the creation ;, architecture is no less\
perfc\'e9t in. its \'f4perations ; it has even preduced ghe first wonder of the world, and\
thereby obtaied the laurel and palm of fame.\
Writers say, that Babylonians were the first, and after them the Agyptians, Greeks\
and Romans, who brought it to perfection; until in the emperor Augustus\'92 time, it\
arrived\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
arrived at its-highest pitch; but sunk, afterwards by the irruption and barbarity\
of the Goths, Vandals, Hunns, and Longobards, who burned and destroyed all\
before them.  true proof that nothing in the world is permanent and\
stable !\
But to come to our purpose, we must \'92premise that the word architec-\
ture) simply signifies draughts or designs, after which,  building is carried\
up and constructed ; and comprehends the five following Orders, namely,\
The Tuscan, Doric, lonic, Roman, and Corinthian : according to which, all buil-\
dings are regulated, whether palaces, tomples, town-houses, triumphal arches,\
bagnios, theatres, town-gates, galleries, tombs, and, other-magpificent buil-\
dings, round or square, or both :  speak of their outward construction.\
The word Order, is of large extent : but in architecture is, as Vitruvius\
defines it.  joining of different proportionate and symmetric parts, as pe-\
destals, columns, andjtheir ornaments, in such manner as to compose  perfect\
order and body.\
As for the entablatures over columns, to wit, architraves, frizes, and cor-\
nices (which for their richnefs, have got the name of ornaments) they may\
as little as the Orders, themselves, , be gither, mixed among one another, or\
changed from one Order to another.\
 careful painter will not only distinguisn one sort from another, from\
cornice to, base, but will also take care not to put an lonic moulding on\
 Doric pedestal ;  Corinthian on  Tuscan;  Roman cornice on an loni\'e9\
frize, &.\
Further, it is certain that the Orders do not promiscuoussy suit all sorts-\
of buildings, but ought so to be applied (respecting their parts and orna-\
ments, which also differ in general from each other) as to have an har-\
mony and agreement with the whole: buildings, with respect. to their situa-\
tion and quality.\
These Orders must be enriched in their several kinds, to fhiew  suitable\
decorum, especially the Capitals, except the Tuscan, which iis- throughout\
\
\
plain and simple.\
The Doric Order excells: in its triglyphs and metopes.  The Jonic, in its\
valures, medillions, &. The Roman, in the elegance of cornices, and beauty\
of capitals with their volutes, and oak-leaves.: And the Corixthiaa, by its\
mouldings of victorious olive leaves, and its- excelleat and agreeable\
capitals.\
The metopes in the Doric frize imay be-enriched. according: to-the qualities\
and uses of buildings, whether temples, town-houses, honourary arches, or courts\
\
\
\
for priests.\
\
In the first suit best carved challices, books, wases, mitres, &. \
In the second, the coats of arms of the Republic, or chief men in the governt\

of Mercury, twined with serpents, as denoting peace-\

In the, third,, various arma and trophies taken from the enemy; or all\

In the last suit best carved ox-sculls, adorned with garlands, betokening sacrifice\
which the ancients made to their deities ; oftentimes the utensils of those offerings\
were introduced, to wit, altar, vases, three legged kettles, vinegar-cups, censers,\
candtesticks, basons, dishies, hammers, axes, knives, &.\
Among the works of the ancients we see in the frizes of the lonic, Roman and\
Crinthian Orders (especially in the two last) some foliage of cak lcaves ; which\
has  fine effect, when twined with shrubs and vine branches, interspersed with\

and running animals : also festoons of fruit, leaves, and various kinds of the most\
beautiful and agreeable flowers; these tied together look nobly, especially when\
judicioussy placed : but enough of this. He wno would know more sorts of frizal\
ornaments, must consult the works  of the ancients, in which he will find.\
them, though the before-mentioned are the principal, and most in use.\
Although the ancients teach, that the fronts of buildings (which are the parts\
most in view) ought to be more adorned than the flanks and rears, yet some mo-\
dern masters have misunderstood this, and apprehended, as if in those parts the\
ornaments could not be too many ; nay, they have crowded the mouldings of the\
architrave, frize and cornice, and of the pedestals under columns, with small\
carving, in such  manner, that it rather causes confusion than ornament, as ap-\
pears by their works : but when used in moderation, and between tbe principal\
ornaments  part is left plain and blank, it causes grandeur and decorum.\
Something is also to be remarked about pediments and key-stones. Pediments\
(or tops of fronts) like the forehead of  man, snew the principal aspect of build-\
ings, especially when their spandrells (or faces) are agreeably enriched by good\
masters with histories, sacrifices, arms, or the like, in marble, according to the\
quality of the fabric.\
Sometimes also are, put over pediments, trophies, coats of arms, or shields ;\
which if well cut and placed, have  noble effect.\
As for key-stones of arches over gates and niches, these may be en-\
riched, \

In the lonic Order, with tame beasts, or heads of Pallas or amazons, with their\

CHAP. II. Of the Rises of the Ornaments, Columns; and their\
Pedestals.\
AVING spoken in the former chapter, of the decoration of the Orders,\
we shall now, in  brief manner, treat of the rifes: and divifions of the\
\
\
columns, with their ornaments and pedestals.\
The ornament (or entablature) of the Tuscau column rises one mod\'fble and\
seven-eight parts of  module (by module is meant, throughout the Orders, the\
pillars diameter next above the base). The column, with its base and capital\
rises seven modules and  half, and the pedestal one module seven eights ; this\
being agreed, the entablature and pedestal are each one fourth of the column\'92\
rise ; the base under the column, and gapital over it, are -in each in rise half \
module; the lessening (or diminution) of the shaft of-the column at the neck,\
under the capital, is one fourth of  module, and it begins below at one fourth\
\
\
\
of the column\'92 rise.\
The entablature of the Doric column rises two-modules and one eight ; the co\'ac\
lumn, with its base and capital, is eight modules and  half, and the pedestal\
two modules and two fifteenths : this being fixed, the entablature is one fourth,\
and the pedestal throe and three fourths-of the column\'92 nise. : The base and ca-\
pital are each, as in the Tuscan, half  module. The diminution at the neck of\
the shaft is one fifth of  module, and begins below at three parts one fourth of\
The entablature of the lonic column rises one module and three fourths ; the\
column, with its base and capital, is eight modules and three fourths, and the\
pedestal twe modules and  half : this settled, the entablature is one fifth, and\
the pedestal three parts and  half of the column\'92 rise. (But if the frize be\
carved, the entablature is four parts and  half of the column\'92 rise, and in the\
following Orders the same). The capital with its volutes, is little more than\
half  module, and the base is just half  module (and in the following Orders\
the same). The diminution at neck is one sixth of  module, and begins below\
at three parts and  half of the column\'92 rise, divided into twelve parts, as afore-\
said.\
The entablature of the Roman column rises one module and twelve twentieth\
parts of  module; the column, with its base and capital, is, nine modules and\
three fourths; the pedestal rises three modules : which laid down, the entabla-\
ture is one fifth, and the pedestal three parts and one fourth of the column\'92\
rise :\
\
\
\
\
\
\
rise : the capital rises one fnodule and one flxth; the base as before ; the diminu-\
tion at neck is one seventh of  module, and begins below at the rise of three\
parts and one fourth of the column, divided as aforesaid.\
The entablature of the Gorinthian column rises two modules ; the column, with-\
its base and capital, is ten modules in rise; the pedestal three modules and one\
third : after which, the entablature is one fifth, and the pedestal one third of the\
column\'92 rise; the capital rises one module and one sixth; the base as before ;\
the diminution at neck is one eight of  module, and begins below at one third of\
the column\'92 rise.\
The breadth of the Tuscan and Doric pedestals ought always to be equal with\
the plinths or bases of their columns; and though the plinths in the three other\
Orders project more at their bottoms, by reason of their sweeps, their pedestals\
must neverthelefs be alike perpendicular with the upper, points of their plinths.\
Let me here six the height of  statue on  pedestal placed next  column ;\
since many mistake in it. In right proportion it ought not to rise higher than\
two thirds of the column; but then the column should not be too high, or too\
low, but stand on  base only, which is frequently continued through the build-\
ing. As for pedestals, they serve only to raise  column, and augment its orna-\
ment. We ought also, for elegance, to take care, that the figure and its pedef-\
tal be proportionate to each other; because,, if the latter be too great or too\
small, the figure would become too small, or monftrous.\
In fine, as all the parts of  building ought to answer in  proportionate dis-\
position, so should the figures, whether carved or painted, be neither too big or\
little : wherefore they must be governed by the height ef the place where they\
are to stand.\
CHAP. III Of the Property of \'e0 Bailding, and Observations\
thereon.\
we \
Must believe, according to the evidence of writers, and the tradition of tra-\
vellers, that the Italians have the best taste, as well for architectur\'e9 as paint-\
ing; and though it is certain, that Germany, France, England, Holland, and othet\
countries, have produced fine architects, yet at this time they are not comparable\
to the italians, whose manner, which is the antique, is now followed by the most\
polite nations. The old taste was known by the name of the Gothic, as  certain\
writer affirms, faying, ee That the Gothic manner of building of the ancient Ger-\
mans (which at that time gave law to all other nations); is quite abolishied by the\
Italians: nay, he cries ont, What magnificent and choice wonders do we not\
see in proud ltaly, lofty Rome especially, where it seems as if Nature and Ant have\
mutually agreed to establisn their thrones, and exert their powers, in order to\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
make this famous city the mistress of the world, and the beauty of the universe !\
St. Peter\'92 church, the Vatican, the Rotunda, and many other structures (serving\
the whole world for examples, and without which architecture is but  confused\
mass), draw yearly thousands of people and young artists thither to improve\
themselves by them, for the service of their own countries ; so that the finest and\
newest things which we see in those countries, lately built, and still going for-\
ward, are designed in the Italian gusto. Wherefore it is to them that those fine\
piles, the Stadthouse at Amsterdam, the new Lutheran church, and divers other\
structures, as well without as within the town, do owe their origin and beauty.\
We are then much indebted to those great artists, Vitruvius. Serlio, Philibert de\
'Orme, Palladio, Cataneo, Leo Baptista Santoritio, Vignola, Scamozzi, and many\
others, who have enlightened the world with their works and writings. And\
\
think that no one can be  good architect who has not studied those authors. The\
French acknowledge, that their great improvements in this art are owing to the\
works and precepts of those excellent masters.\
We shall now speak of intire buildings so far as they serve for ornament in\
painting. The goodness of  building springs not merely from the aforesaid rises,\
breadths or depths of the Orders, but from an apposite conjunction (or bring-\
ing together), of different proportionate parts into an exquisite body, which, by\
reason of its excellent form, whether in height or breadth, appears to the judg-\
ment of the knowing both admirable and beautiful ; especially when sitly adapted\
to the quality of the owner, and has general conveniency with respect to cus-\
In its particular parts  building requires,   firm foundation.   large\
and convenient stair-case.   spacious entrange.  An elegant division of-\
doors, windows, and other openings.  An Mandsome frontispiece, &. These\
Kkilfully worked, and judiciously disposed, must needs produce  sine effect.\
Thus. much for the outward face of  building.\
If any object, that decorum consists more in inward contrivance, let me sup-\
pose  stranger to come into  town, and, passing through the streets, he were\
asked, What he thought of the buildings: What answer would he make\'e0\

 must\

its in-side. Let it be afked then, Wherein consists  good division within ; whe:\

and what those must be ; whether there must be one, two, or more stacks of stairs ;\
whether each room must have one chimney or two;, and-whether, the floors must\

than  common or citizen\'92 house ?\

But, waving other mens opinions, we shall proceed in our purpose, so far as\
eoncerns  painter in these countries, and no further. Our judgment is, that the\
best proportion in- building is one third. higher in, rise than breadth, espe-\
cially if it be covered in with  compass-roof and its appertunances; but if flat-\
roofed,  third wider than high, and to be commanded by  single Order rising\
from bottom to top. It would be improper to adorn them with statues, bass-re-\
liefs, festoons, &. For such heavy and close structures, without weight, and\
moreover open on top, are proper for an amphitheatre, but not for  temple or\
palace ;  speak with respect to custom and decorum, which must allways go to-\
ther, since nothing is beautiful without its natural qualities. The case is as \
woman in  man\'92 habit, and the contrary ; or  water-vase adorned with an\
olive-branch and thunder; or an oil-vessel with tritons and dolphins.\
In painting  good building there must appear, besides the architecture, per-\
spective and colouring, an orderly disposition, producing elegance, otherwise it\
is of no worth. Orderly disposition consists in so joining the parts, that they\
mutually set off each other in  pleasing variety, and thereby exhibit  fine piece\
of work, and an agreeable figure : this variety springs from the inequality of\
openings, or windows, whether oblongs, squares, circles or ovals ; the disposi-\
tions of these openings, near and over each other, are founded on reason, as shall\
be explained by an example.\
We exhibit in-plate LIX. -temple topped with  cupola, or rather an house\
round-roofed. On each side of the door  flight of steps descend ballustraded;\
underneath which is  vault; and over the entrance  balcony : now, beginning\
from below, we shall shew what figure each opening ought to have in an orderly\
disposition. The door under the steps is square, rising somewhat more than its\
diameter: that of the entrance is circular on top, and rises one third more than\
its diameter: that of the balcony also rises one third more than its breadth, but is\
square or flat on top; and then we meet the roof rounding again. Now let us\
dispose it otherwise, and make the door below circular, the next square, the next\
Thus much for doors; for we find no other than\
round, and no roof appearing.\
round and square ones. The same method may be taken with windows ; when\
there are two ranges, one above the other, the undermost may be oblong, and\
the uppermost compass, but lower: if there be another range of windows above\
these, next the roof, they ought to. be perfect squares. This rule we have taken\
from the works of the ancients, who always gave their openings or windows more\
rise in the first story than the second, still lessening in the third, yet all alike in\
diameter. Windows should never be lower than about three feet from the floor\
within. If there be windows in the basement-story (where the walling is thickest,\
and is usually finished with  Rustic Order), they must be square, and above ei-\
ther scheme (little rounding) or flat; and thus the one snew the other, with re-\
spect to roundness and squareness. If-now there were compass-windows in the\
basement and upper stories, the- uppermost should be circular, and the undermost\
oval, with their longest diameter parallel with the level of the building, because,\
being pressed by the weight, their arch is dilated ; when contrarily the others\
do not bear any weight. But circular windows are grown out of use, as not ad-\
mitting light so well as the square ones.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
If in the piers between windows there were niches and figures in them, and\
over head, room for  bafs relief, than the table for ie must be square, but circu-\
lar, where it is over  square window.\
As for doors over each other, Isay, that if there were over the cornice  com-\
pafs pediment, and on both sides  ballustrde with figures; then the bal oony door\
ought not to be circular but square and ornamreated with pillasters; yet the door of\
the entrance may be circular. We ought even not to set on the balcony  ballustrade\
with figures, but ornament it with balls or other low things. The one or other\
ballustrade must be also diversified ; for two parts alike in two such emminent\
places have an ill effect; wherefore one of them should be close walled (parapet-\
ways) and the face of it may then be enriched with bafs reliefs of figures,\
festoons, &.\
 think what  have said  sufficient guide for the other kinds of buildings; for\
these observations are on all occasions unalterable to  painter, with re-\
spect to agreeable disposition.\
There is still somewhat behind, which, though contrary to the dispofition\
aforesaid, must be animadverted ; it is touching  slower garden, which if fine,\
must needs be regular, as well with respect to its general form, as its particular\
division ; at least the two sides ought to be alike, whether set off with pots, vafes,\
statues or other ornaments. We are taught indeed, that uniformity is stiff, and\
not painter-like; wherefore we Thould avoid it as much as posfible : but\
weighing the precept maturely, we shall find the fauit to lie in dur misapprehen-\
fion of it : questionless things proceeding from rul\'e9 and order must be regular,\
but that regularity may nevertheless be somewhat hidden on occason : wherefore,\
though  garden be uniform on both sides, we are under no necesfity of shewing\
all that uniformity : one side is sufficient, the rest may be handsomely hid, or\
broke with  tree, piece of stone work, column, or  corner of the house. But\
let us not from hence absolutely conclude, regular objects to be unnecessary in an\
ordonnance ; for they sometimes furnisn all the decorum of  picture ; yet if we\
see but  part of them, we may conceive the residue.\
More examples of breaking uniformity may be thefe : when  figure stands on\
each side of  gate or alcove,  man may be placed or sit down before one of them\
in order tocreate inequality ; so also if by means of \'e0 curtain, one of those figures\
be shaded. If  bafs relief be set on each side of the gate or alcove, one ofthem\
may partly be covered by the personages, to answer our purpose. If without\
doors on both sides of an entrance, there be  carved lion or sphinx, we may break\
of one of their heads. Solomon\'92 throne was adorned with twelve golden hons : we\
need not see them all, the fix on one side fhew sufficiently, that there must be as\
many on the other : two orthree entire are enough, of the rest we may see only \
part here andthere. Thus we must always manage in such Cafes.\

CHAP. . Of the matching of the vario\'e9: coloured Marbles, as\
well without as within  Building; with the Management of Tombs,\
Vafa, and Bacchanalian Terms.\
BJECTS have \'e0 fine effect, when nature and art are joined together by al\
skilful hand; and though all eyes are not alike qualified to apprehend the\
it is beautiful, and soought to be, though the one person, as  say, understand\
it, and the other do not : and forthis reason; art has such  power, that though\
Nature, be beautifut in her productions, yet they would not perfectly please our\
eges, without the help of art.\
Nature produces an infinity of fine stanes of various colours and qualities; but\
art alone judges of their fitness and orderly looation as to rank and dignity, inso-\
much that, though ignorant in art, we oan. clearly apprehend that it ought to be\
so, and no otherwise.\
We know, that ths white is foft and tender, and lovely to the eye ; the black\
contrarily is melancholy and disagreeahle. We place then the black among the\
red, and upon the red, white. These three coloured stones are capitals, and can-\
aot be otherwise dispofed as-to th\'e9ir natufes and qualities, without foreing nature,\
and zunning counter to art.\
However, their rank and application in architectonic use may be these.\
In the Tustan Order, as undermost, black marble.\
In the Doric, green.\
I the : Janic, yeHow.\
In the Roman, red. : And,.\
In the Coriathian, white.\
If any afk why the ned is not set before the yellow, since the red is in its nature\
daykerrthanthe yellow Ianswer, That it is because the red and green are op-\
posers in strength ;. coatrarily, yellout is proper to green, since yellow and blue\
 green.\
 at any time we are obliged to place between two stones of one colour,  stone\
of another, the following mixtures are mostly in use.\
Between two black marbles suit best jasper, copper or brafs.\
Between two serpentine, or green marble stones, the same.\
Between. two red stones, white.\
Between red, black or serpentine stone, Pisan white-eyed marble.\
Between two grey stonies, free-stone or yellowisn white marble.\
Between two dotted long veiny stones;: one that is speckled ; aad the contrary-\
Between two jaspers, yellow, or ffestry white marble.\
\'92marbl\'e9-painter miist observe the conveniency and place for marbling : if the\
plaoe be large, or an hall, then he may doit with force ; but in  smaller com-\
pafs,\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
pass, he ought to moderate it, and keep it faint, that the place may not seem\
thereby lessened, or the eye offended. If the room be hung with pictures, he\
should consult men of experience, especially the master who painted them, what\
colouring will fit best.\
\
\
About light pictures, dark marble is best, and about dark pictures, light marble, \
as Pisan, jaspar, or any tending to  light yellow. But if it be  single\
colour, such as bass reliefs, then free-stone suite best.\
In bringing many sorts of coloured marbles together, we may, for instance, in\
 frontispiece either single or double-colonaded and pedestrated, very agreebly\
dispose them thus : viz. plante IX. The base and pedestral-mouldings may be black, \
little eyed : the block of square of the piedestal, dark red, much\
dotted, less veined: theplinths of the columns and pilasters mey be white,\
the columns, light red, or Pisan marble with large white-eyed veins on both sides\
the eying to be alike ; the pilasters also light red, moderately eyed, the capitals\
white, the architrave black, like the base and mouldings of the pedestals ; the\
frize may be dark red, like the blocks of squares of the pedestals ; and the cornice\
black again like the architrave ; if the frize have raised ornaments of foliage,\
children, triglyphs, ox-sculls, &. they ought to be white : if over the cornice\
be  parapet, it may be entirely of another colour, and the pedestals and the\
members of Pisan or other cross-veined marble, and the panels of faces of grey\
marble, or white, if adorned with bass reliefs ; the figures, or vases on top also\
white.\
This distribution may be doubled, and varied on occasion : the frizes and co\
lumns may be white, the bases and capitels gold ; and so may also the ornaments\
be to wit, triglyphs, little blocks and foliage; the rest may remain as before.\
In an hall of red or other marble (where the mouldings of the ornaments are\
different, larger or smaller) we may make  door frontispiece, or alcove of white\
or other marble; but if the members continue along the hall, \'92the frontispiece or\
alcove ought to be of the same colour with the room. The room may be of one\
order, and the frontspiece, alcove and chimney of another. Thus the room may\
be lonic, and the rest Corinthian or Roman. The pavement of the floor must cor-\
respond with the building,  mean, if the room have pillasters, the bands (or\
bordering marbles tying the pavement) must run up to them, whether the\
pilasters stand wide or close, for they ought to be so laid, as to shew every where\
-regularity, whatever jets or breaks the door, frontispiece, alcove, &. may oc-\
casion; as  prudent gardener disposes his parterres, one round, another square,\
octangular, &. always contriving such an uniformity as closes with the borders.\
In the middle of the hall, may be introduced such figures or compartments, as best\
answer the general purpose, and they may be of what colour you please.\
In chambers or galleries, where the sides are unlike, we are obliged to part\
them by some figure coming between; and yet the bands, which bind the sides,\
must come every where alike. If there be columns on both fides of the gallery,\
the bands must run, croffing it, from one to the other.\

CHAP. VII. Principal Directions for painting the Ornaments off\
Halls, Rooms, &.\
In this sort of work we must, in the first place, have an, eye. to, the regu-\
larity and division of the architecture, and, if that be. beautiful, not in the\
least hurt it under pretence of decorum, or acting- painterelike; and if at any\
time we are obliged to. alter this conduct, it must nevertheless be in favour of the\
architecture.\
 That the painting, of what kind soever it be, must tend to the lustre and\
magnificence. of the building; . speak with respect to painting the wood-work,\
whether it be marbled or plain.\
 That the ornaments to be painted agree with shose of the room, and be\
governed by the same Order in architecture.\
ia\
 That in pannels, niches and windoms. ought to be artfully painted, whas\
you would have appear to be real on nattirally there, whether tapestry or prospect :\

with the light of the room-\

hours of the day at the same time ; nor in histories, unless they be in the mannee\
of tapestry.\
 He must never inclose white marble bafs-reliefs wigh wood-work, as being\

Lastly, fince excess often abates the majesty of  fabric, the artist\'92should avoid\

And now, that  may conceal nothing from the artists,  shall subjoin the pic-\

brary, and such like. In fine, we may introduce any thing that is different from\
the furniture of the apartment. But care must be taken, that the painting have\
 natural and high horizon, with little or no sky, to gain more depth; vistos of\
apartments one within another are also not improper; but if we represent with-\
out-door-prospects as aforesaid, it is more proper to paint doors, seemingly to give\
the room-air: and seeing it often happens, that such  painting cannot have the\
most advantageous light, and is sometimes in shade by the projecture of the chimi-\
ney, we should contrive the work accordingly, and so as not to appear like paint-\
ing, but nature itself.\

CHAP. IX. Descriptian of several Pictures adapted to the five\
Orders in Architecture.\
INCE no manner of describing fine apartments is more proper than this,\
 which exhibits things as if we really saw them,  shall therein give an\
architectonic view of each Order, and in as conspicuous  manner as  myself\
conceive it. The particulars must then be well regarded, because they are so-\
linked. together, that, by overlooking  small circumstance, the whole chain\
may be broke, without ever getting  true idea of the thing.\
We shall confine the subject to five apartments, and describe in each the pic-\
tures, which shew the nature, height, custom, and other properties relating to-\
the Orders of this building: and since the Tuscan Order, either in parts or alto-\
gether, is. rough and massy, we shall exhibit here,\
The Picture of POLYPHEMUS and GKLATEK.\
Polyphemus, on the sea-shore, inflamed with the love of the beautiful Galateay\
who came to divert herself on the pleasant, surges of the billows, strove to please :\
her with-his singing and music, and thereby to gain her favours; but she was-\

aversion; wherefore she shuns him, and derides his addresses.\
 calm sea was seen. On tlie second ground, to the left, appcared  vast high:\

his side ; his raiment was goats-skin sewed together, which he had shook from off\
his shoulders, possibly to discover to Galatea his conceited fine shape : this garment\
was cream-colour, spotted with black. He \'92sat very rudely, leaning  little back\
against the rock ; his left leg was stretched out towards the water, and his right,\
with the foot fore-shortened, lifted up, lay over  piece of the rock ; his flure,\
with an hundred pipes, he held, in his left hand, up at his mouth, as if he had\

Commentonthe Characters in the aforesaid Pictures.\
Polyphemus, the Sicilian herdsman, the most savage and gigantic of all the Gclops,\
was, according to Homer, son of Neptune and the nymph Thosa; the word Gyclops\
signifies, having but one eye in the middle of the forehead, whereby some would\
imply, the thunder and lightening, according to the Greck names of his com-\
panions, Brontes, Sterope and Pyradmon, and other effects of the air, round which\
they are always attending in readiness at the command of Jupiter; the air, they\
say, being placed in the middle of Heaven, as an eye in the head. Thus the\
commentators on Hefiod in his Theogonia (Deorum Origo deliver.\
Hesiod says, that Galatea, daughter of Nereus and Doris, is so named from her\
whiteness, signifying parabolically, the froth of the sea ; wherefore this poet af-\
cribes toher white hair and  face like milk: he says further,that some writers would,\
by Galatea, allude to the sweet water which falls into the sea, because nothing is\
sweeter than milk ; and by Polyphemus, the air, which loves the sweet food.\
The youth Acis, is called by Ovid, son of the river Faunus and Simethis, being\
both young, beautiful and well shaped.\
The Tritons are counted by most of the poets, sons of Neptune and Amphitrita,\
because the sea, says Vermander, is esteemed the mother or producer of many\
ftrange creatures, which its element is very inclineable to ; and the ancient hea-\
thens perceiving thus somewhat wonderful, ascribed to the sea some divinity, as\
they also did to those tritons, whose help they implored in dangers at sea. But they\
who examine more narrowly into the Agyptian hieroglyphics, say, that the tritons\
by their amphibious form of being human upwards, and dolphin-like dovnwards,\
are compaired to the two watry virtues, saltness and sweetness, teaching us that\
both good and evil spring from their nature and constitution, to wit, good from\
the\
\
\
\
\
\
\
the human nature, and nothing but evil from the fifhinefs ; for ths human form,\
says Phurnutus, is compared to sweet water, which is proper for the aliment of\
noxiousto the animals of the carth and air, and also to plants, causing them to\
die and wither, as we read in Plutarch, of the nature of things.\
Touching the Nereids, we find in Plato, that there were an hundred of them;\
H\'e9fiod says hfty, and gives us their names ; of which Glauce, Cymodoce, Galatea,\
Cyrene, Drimo, Deiopeia, Xantho, Arethusa, Phillodoce, Euridice, Nes\'e6e, Leucothoe,\
Spio, Thalia, Cydippe, Pafithea, Lycorias, Ligea, Ephyre, Opis, Asie, Clymene and\
Halia, are the principal : their lower parts being hsni-like has given the poets oc-\
cafion to feign, that they were very beautiful nymphs who accompanied their\
gods, viz. the Ocean, Thetis, Neptune, and Nereus and Doris their father and mo-\
ther, and many others, who signify the different qualities and various effects of\
the waters : they were stiled mothers of the floods, because the rainy clouds, be-\
ing exhaled from the sea, are the origin of floods ; wherefore, on account of the\
virtue of the earth\'92 moisture towards the procreation of animals, trees, fruits,\
flowers, &. they were worshipped by the heathens as the nurses of them.\
Having largely handled the off-spring and signification of the characters in both\
Har-\
the aforesaid pictures, we shall pafs to  general explication of the latter.\
mony in music arises from an agreeable mixture of discording and flat sounds with\
concording and sharp ones ; but in love it is otherwise, where dissimularity can-\
not be brought to agree, or two hearts to join which do not sympathise by an\
harmony of humours. The hideous make of the Cyclops is frightful to the beau\'ac\
teous Galatea, who shuns him for her dearer Acis ; by Pobyphemus, in this last storys\
we learn that those persons sue in vain, who flatter themselves that their trouble-\
some addresses gain the aff\'e9ctions of those who hate them : contrarily, Acis blessed\
with the smiles of his mistrefs, shews us the danger of exposing ourselves to the\
resentment of  powerful rival, from whom at any time we must expect nothing\
but death. It may also,  say, serve for an example of the power of beauty,\
which so bewitched Acis, that he could not forbear loving, though at the expence\
of his life. Thus we are bewildered by our own inclinations, and brought to the\
place of inevitable misfortunes, where we are plunged in tears to the weakning of\
our vital strength, as in this fable of the young and amorous Acis when Galaiea\
transformed him into  fountain.\

Let us now confider both the pictures, but chiesly the signification of Samson\'92\
hair, and the love of Delilah.\
We read briefly in Scripture, raany things touching the hair of Samson, of which-\
he was very careful, because, whilst it grew, it became longer and thicker, where-\
by he gained greater strength for breaking the ropes witn which he was at any\
time bound ; but, being cut off, his strength forsook him, and his whole body.\
was subject to weakness.\
By the person of Samson the Nazarean, we understand,  man ehosen by heaven;-\
and devoted to its service; for the mei: of that order took, as  have said, especial-\
care of their hair; which gave them virtue, adorning the head, . . the under-\
standing, which, the more it encreases, the more courageous we become against\
assaults of our enemies. By enemies. endeavouring to bind us, we understands.\
h\'fbman inclinations, and the fire of concupiscence. When now, through frailty,\
we are seduced by this Delilah, those corrupt affections, whereby the lust of steep-\
overpowers-us, and we slumber in her lap, right reason becomes useless, and we\
cease todo good. Thus we are shorn by the wiles of women ; that is, by means-\
of voluptuousness we are deaf to the impulses of the spirit, and then of course lie\
apen to our enemies, both to scorn and crush us;\'92for-worldly affairs are so affecting,\
that\
\
\
\
\
\
\
that they have no sooner got the mastery, but we find ourselves crossed, either\
by covetousnefs, love, Ratrid, jealousy, or other disquiet : but returning  our-\
selves, or awaking, we become sensible of our folly, and\'92 through contrition, gra-\
dually recover our hair, and thereby our strength; and then, dying to sin, we at\
once overcome both ourselves and our enemies.\
The hair cnt off, also implies, the weakness of the faculties of the soul or spirit,\
or even death itself.\
Euripides testifies, that Alcestus could not die before Mercury came from heaven\
to cut off his hair. Minos likewise could not overcome king Nijus, unless his fatal\
hair were cut off by his daughter. And Dido, says Virgil, could not die before\
Juno, who pitied her long agony and lingering death, sent lris to release the soul\
from corporeal ties, by cutting off her white hair, and offering it to Pluto.\
These two last hisforical pictures differed from the two preceding in this, that\
they were not mixed with poetic figures, as Cupid, or Love ; Mag\'e6ra, or Rage,\
and such like, to help the expreffion of the paffions, or. meanings; fince it is cer-\
tain, that real truth could not be discerned from fiction by  mixture of both.\
And although the statue of Venus, in 'this matter of fact, seem to be of that nature,\
yet it is nothing to the mean point, but ferves only to shew that the plaoe was\
heathenifh, and where probably such figures were common among that people.\

This piece was particularly remarkable for the death of the heroe; and did\

slackness of the optical nerves, drew towards the utmost corners. The sweat\
broke out, and he shined with wetnefs, chiefly about the breast, over which\
waved  thin damp, like the fumes of boiling water; which made his out-line\
unite with the ground : in this part it was, that the unhappy heroe had the most\
feeling ; and where the blood, leaviag the members and seeking for shelter, was\
retiring to the heart ; his breast was swelled, and, as he fetched breath, heaved\
and set ; his belly was fallen in, and the ribs were prominent ; his upper parts\

also his face ; his lips were not as yet dead nor pale, but his hands and feet al-\
most burned black ; his eye-brows appeared drawn somewhat upwards, as one\
who, though sleepy, strives to keep awake; the arms and legs were bare, pale\
and shrunk, as partaking of death; but the fingers, knuckles, knees and toes,\

red and violet spots of the poison; and his linen, shoved underneath at the navel,\

It is certain, that if any have been there, Hercules frighted them away in his\

lastly, because the matter clears itself so well, that any addition would alter it,\
and, instead of an unexpected act, make it rather appear as  premeditated fune-\
ral solemnity. Whence, we may well infer, that the pile was not prepared for\
him, but that he himself made it on  sudden, as the poet relates.\
This artful piece was remarkable for these three things naturally and plainly\
expressed ; to wit, the fact itself ; what preceded, and what followed. The be-\
ginning of the tragedy was, when, having received the poisoned shirt of Nessus\
by Lychas, he offered it at the altar to Jupiter his father. The sequel of his rage ap-\
peared by that unhappy wretch\'92 being cast into the sea, and metamorphosed in-\
to  rock ;. after which he burned himself; and his succeeding triumph was shewn\
by the chariot which-Jupiter sends him for his deification. Renascitur ex funere\
Phaenix.\
The conclusions to be made from the persons of Nessus, Deianira, and Lychas,\
may be these.\
We learn from the Centaur, how dangerous the gifts of enemies are ; the cause\
of the great heroe\'92 death. In Deianria we discover her imprudent and indisereet\
pafsion, and the effects of her jealousy, which made her the instrument of her\
-husband\'92 death; and in Lychas we observe the miserable reward of his services,\
and that the misfortunes of servants are sometimes by the great construed as to\
render obedience and disobedience equally culpable.\

to the wick, covered by the clouds under Juno; which, as far as  could appre-\
hend, was an artful slight of the master, in order to render the foremost light the\
brighter and stronger; and to avoid the necessity of making the whole piece dark ;\
which otherwise he must have done for the sake of naturalness.\
Juno had  diadem, and  light blue garment, her head-attire was wild, and her\
locks flying about like serpents.\
The poets mention; that Hercules was represented by the ancients, as an example\
of all virtues, as well of the body as the soul; squeezing serpents to death with\
his hands, even in his cradle; by which they give us to understand, that  man,\
sitted for heroism, ought, from his infancy, to shun, pleasures, and mortify carnal\
affections.\

Jupiter sat in the middle, high on his eagle. Hercules, crowned with laurels,\

making  semi-circle ; so that the winged horses were seen mostly from underneath;\
their breasts fronting, and heads towards the right. Mercury held the reins in with\
his right hand, close to his mouth. The chariot was surrounded with many Cupids,\
having garlands and branches. Mercuary looked towards the right at Jupiter, who,\
with his scepter directed him to  circle of twelve glittering stars in the firmament,\
which enlightened some small clouds in that quarter. The whole Celestial body\
sat on waving clouds, exulting and clapping their hands. The sun shined bright.\

neverthelefs, hisfrizzled hair and beard, and fine mein, convinced me, that it could\

after another, and began to know them all, to the very least : Apollo, by his radiant\

by his jolly cheeks and members : Asculapius, by his long tressed hair and beard ;\

blue, Bacchus\'92 purple, Ceres\'92 ftraw-colour, Monna\'92 green and yellow, &. which\

Before we proceed in our relation, let us shew what the heathens understood by\
the deification of Hercules.\
Hercules, the glory of valiant men, shews us, by his deification, that those who-\
attempt that honour in their life-times, as Anthony with his Cleopatra did, or strive\
to obtain it by intreating and cajoling the people, as most of the Persian kings\
and Romulus did, mistake the right method ; whereas Hercules\'92 whole life was ta-\
ken up in freeing the world from monsters and tyrants; and no divine honours\
were paid him until after his death ; for eternity, which he obtained only by\
death, teachs, that true virtue will not be flattered in this life; as Aleander prov-\
ed to those who were beforehand for calling him  god, by shewing them the\
blood which issued from his wounds, in the same manner as. from other mortals.\
How powerful and virtuous soever  man may be, as long as he draws breath he\
cannot call himself happy, as being no more exempted fxom the teeth of biting\
envy, than Hercules was in his life-time. The heathens worshipped him as  god,\
according to their superstition ; beliving also, that though alk souls are immortal,\
yet those of valiant men, pursuing virtue, attain an higher pitch of honour, and\
partake of the Deity : they even affign him, in heaven, Hebe, the goddess of youth,\
for  confort, on account of his strength, which is found only in youth.\
Thus, in after-times, the philosopher and poet Empedocles, (vainly, in immitation\
of Hercules, who made his frind Philoctetes swear never to reveal the place where he\
burnt himself, nor what was become of him, in order to induce the people to\
think he was taken up into heaven) threw himself into mount Ama: but his iron\
slippers being cast out with the fiery stones, discovered the case andthe truth-\
But, to return to our relation.\
In going out of the apartment,  saw on the pavement  sphara: mundi or ter-\
restrial globe, curiously inlaid, divided on each side with compartments, and cut\
with elegant bands of costly marble and jasper, which ran to the center : each\
stone shewed.  monster running off from. the globe, and such as Hercules, in his.\
life-time, had delivered the world from.\

Second Picture.\
Here Semiramis was seen setting out from her court, with an extraordinary majesty\
and courage. she descended tne steps very airily.  martial fire seemed to in-\
flame her heart ; which gave  glow to her cheeks; her eyes sparkled like two\
stars. If she had not an helmet,  should, by her dress and accoutremens, have\
taken her for  Diana going  hunting. Everything was in readiness for her march,\
even to her robe; which she refused to put on contented only with  bow and\
arrows and her authority. The waiting woman ran up and down stairs, one bring-\
ing this, another that; one of the chief put the royal helmet on her head;  foot-\
stool was set for her, below on the stairs, whilst the other was girding the sword\
about her. The curvetting horse, inured to war, stood ready at the stair-foot.\
The trumpets sounded, and the people, full of desire, crowded about. The pas-\
sage was cleared. The horse, divided into troops, were drawn up in the inner-\
court. The Messenger ran down the further steps; and the matron above, in the\
gateway, was gaping and staring at the preparations. The sky was clear, and:\
seemed to favour the princess\'92 enterprize.\

After  thorough view of this picture,  began to consider, wherein its good-\
ness lay, which was what  chiefly wanted ; wherefore, taking my pocket-book,\
 set down in it the general heads in the following manner.\
First, The dispofition of the irregular objects against each other, whether high\
or low, standing or lying.\
Secondly, The disposition of the grounds behind each other.\
Thirdly, The placing of the lights.\
Fourthly, The motion of the moving objects.\
Fifthly, The proper by-works, climate, and customs.\
Sixthly, The conditions or characters of the persons, with the dresses and\
Syrian equipage.\
Seventhly, The particular postures and paffions.\
Lastly, The harmony of the colours.\

Thus was the plan of this artful piece, which  thought no less wonderful\

As the sense of the story is very particular, so the three pictures were as ex-\
cellent from first to last. In the first, we perceive the lucky chance of arms, or\
the valour of the heroe, whereby he gained the repute of  deliverer of his\
country : in the second, we consider him as  murtherer, or, the accident as \
bloody triumph, and him elated with his success: and, in the third, we see him\
 malefactor, condemned to be put to death, or one who had transgressed the laws.\
Truly, those three events, may serve for instructive examples to all men. Do\
we not see in them the common course of the world, and that too great success\
and prosperity make many men proud and insolent; And what do not their\
blind pafsions lead them to  certainly, unthankfulnefs to heaven is the prelude\
to many disasters and errors, leading them into the greatest dangers: however,\
all things are governed by Providence.\
The middle of the cieling had  large oval piece, wherein Providence was,\

Now, we ought to weigh the meanings of these things. Providence is to\
be considered as the chies ruler of worldlv affairs, debasing and raifing empires\
as she pleafes. The three gifts of long Life, Health and Prosperity, are bleffings\
flowing from her. The corporeal virtues are the effects of might, whenee they\
proceed: the first is Religion, the second Valour, and the third Concord;\
these established Roma, in her power, and increased it. In relation to art, let\
us observe, with what ingenuity and uncommonnefs the master has executed\
those pictures :  say uncommonness, since  never saw them handled by any\
other after such  manner. First, Providence is in the greatest depth, and,\
according to guefs, thrice as big as the life. The three gifts, which sne sends\
down, being somewhat. lower, are not half so big; and the undermost, to wit,\
Roma, and the characters accompanying her, are still smaller, yet somewhat\
larger than the life-\
Providence has no bounds, always maintaining her uncontrolled power with-\
out diminution; and though the three gifts, which slow from her, are but small\
parts, yet, with respect to the undermost figures, they are much bigger, and\
keep among them their own forms, as reigning over them. The three others\
on the undermost clouds being but corporeal virtues, are therefore much smal-\
ler than the preceeding, and appeared with less majesty : nevertheless Roma\
excels, and shews herself bigger; intimating thereby her growth and improve-\
ment. Her sitting on clouds implies, in my opinion, her rising above all\
other powers of the world.\
This would be  monstrous design, if art, with respect to perspective, were\
not duly observed: but, by this means, the piece looked so perfect, that \
judged it could not otherwise be good ; for the undermost group, as quite low,\
was very strong ; the middlemost, according to its distance, somewhat fainter;\
and the uppermost, very faint, and almost imperceptible.\
This emblem bears  mysterious interpretation, and may, in general, be\
applied to all the governments in the world, provided the figure of Roma be\
altered, and another substituted, as things require. Instead of Asculapius we may\
represent health, by the figure of  woman, and in the place of Hercules, the\
fame; taking for Valour an heroine, holding. an oaken branch in her right\
hand, with  lion on her fhield.\
In treating formerly of this sort of fables, we have called them emblema-\
tical, carrying  mystic sense, whether they be mundane or spiritual ; however,\
as  distinction between boch, and to shew that this is mundane and historical,\
we must observe, that it is not intermixed with any emblematic figures, which\
have  spiritual\
sense, except those of Asculapius and Hercules, which therefore\
in this work  reject as unfit, and only-proper for poetic and fabulous sub-\
jects;\
\

\
jects; as if, instead of Roma, were introduced Troja or Aging, which are do-\

of Horatius proves, but that therein is also exprest the force or mysterious sense\
by those heathenisn figures.\
Now, if it be afked, why this cieling piece does not allude to the person of\
Horatius, as that in the tenth chapter to Hercules: my opinion is, that the con-\
clusion of the story, so far as it respects him, is contained in the second picture :\
for here we cannot expect any deification, nor do the gods interfere in the\
matter : they regard only those who are reckoned in their number, such as\
AEneas, Hercules, Memnon, and others of godly race.\
CH AP. XIII. The Fable of Calisto, adapted to the Corinthian\
Order, in the Upper-story.\
AM delighted to relate here, in four pieces, the wonderfully embellished\
story of Calisto, and her deification; as not unworthy, in my judgment, to\
adorn so fine an apartment as this last, which was of the Crinthian Order, and\
very magnificent, as well with respect to the extraordinary thoughts as their\
artful turn; the conclusion whereof renders this work most perfect.\
The first piece was as follows.\
Calisto, tired with hunting, went to repose in the shade of the trees ; Jupiter\
enamoured with her, came to delude her in the shape of Diana, and gratified\
his paffion, notwithstanding all her efforts to the contrary.\
There, on the right side of the piece, on an eminence, the innocent creature\
was sitting under the trees, not at rest, but full of concern, shame, and dread,\
melted into tears, with her hand on the edge of  fountain; her tresses, half\
loosed, hung carelesly over her naked shoulders; her chaste bosom was above\
half bare, and her legs uncovered to unseemliness, sufficiently shewed her sor-\
rowful fate. Jupiter, the author of it, was seen  little off, next the middle of\
the piece, above the horizon, not as  disguised or pretended Diana, but the\
chief of the gods, shining with majesty, with his diadem on his head, and in\
his purple robe; not as  thunderer, with lightnings and tempests, but only\
attended by his eagle. The cruel and degenerate lover seemed to deride her\
forrow, having his left hand up at his breast, as if he meant, that he had got\
his will; wherefore, penetrating the clouds, he advanced through the air, for-\
saking the miserable woman. The unmerciful incendiary, Cpid, was extin-\
guishinghis torch in the fountain, looking at Jupiter, who, with his pointing\
sceptre, commanded him to do so. Diana was seen in the off-scape, in  valley,\
with her retinpe of nymphs. The landscape was delightful and woody : here\
and there appeared some river gods. Behind Calisto, among the trees, stood \
term of Priapus in shades.  attentively viewed the aforesaid three figures, and\
reflected to myself, how well they acted their parts ; clearly opening the matter,\
even to th\'e9 very term, which, though it might be placed there accidentally, yet\

The unhappy Calisto, bemoaning her misfortune, and full of shame and fear,\
and discarded by her mistress, was seeking shelter in solitudes : yet the jealous\
Juno spyed and found her there.\
On the left side of the piece, appeared the superior goddess of heaven,\
glittering, coifed with peacock\'92 feathers, instead of  diadem, or royal head\

attended and was at her back, to punisn the innocent Calisto; and lifted up\
her left arm, and the fingers streight up, she with  severe and envious look,\
reproached the oppressed creature, with lying with her confort. Revenge was\

serable Calisto; who now had no more of her former shape, except her cloaths,\
which fell  prey to the hellisn fury; there lay the quiver, here the bow,\
yonder the girdle; as  conceived, it was  she-bear who shook off those cloaths,\
and was taken to flight. Being now metamorposed into so frightful  monster,\
by the immoveable jealousy of Juno; she, in her flight, looked up to heaven,\

pared to shoot her.\
Jupiter, from heaven, seeing this in pity hindred the\
matricide.\

Animadver sion.\
This fable clearly stews how beautiful bodies are polluted by uncleannefs; for\
in  fhort time after Calisto was delivered of her son Arcas, Juno transformed her,\
as  punishment of her unchastity, into  she-bear,  beast so deformed, as to be\
reckoned among monsters. The aforesaid evil has such direful effects, that the\
fruit or children of unlawful love mortally hate their guilty parents ; foi-beauty\
stained with unchastity; is of no account in the eyes of the virtuous, and what be-\
fore created wonder, is now  mark of infamy. Ovid, in an elegant and artful\
manner, assigns Calisto  notable place in the northern hemisphere, and shews Ju-\
no\'92 intreating Thetis, that those stars (according to the belief of the heathens) might\
never refresn themselves in the sea, in order to pourtray wonderfully her eternal\
shame, as surpassing the other capital stars, and having such  station near the\
Northern Pole, that, as this pole or point of the axis is above our horizon, this\
star, whatever course it takes, can never be out of our sight, and therefore her\
crime be as little out of our memories.\
But  more christian-like inference may be, that the polluted soul abhorring her\
crime, by true repentance gained  most glorious and shining aspect, besides \
fixed station in the heavens ; setting an example to others like Mary Mugdalen,\
whofe crimes, through repentance, were not only expiated, but, by an incorrup-\
table glory intirely blotted out.\
The truth of this story, taking off the poetic mafk, is, that Arcas, son of Jupiter\
and the nymph Calisto, taught the Arcadians (who pretended to be the most ancient\
people of the earth, nay, older than the moon, as Plurarch intimates in his  gzd Roman questions, boasting to be sprung from the earth, and therefore\
made great account of the oak and beech-trees and their fruit, after king Pelasgus\
had taught them to make it their food, which before was only herbs and roots)\
to till the ground and sow corn; which knowledge he learned of Triprolemus, son\
of Cres; and afterwards to make bread of it : also how to weave woollen cloths\
for covering their bodies; likewise inuring them to many civilities. In acknow-\
ledgnent of which benefaction, and in honour to him, they named their country\
Arcadia, which before was called Pelasgia, as Pausanias, in his Arcadia testifies.\

CH AP. XIV. Description of the inward View of the Temple of\
Apollo.\
We hhave before, in treating of Painter-like beauty, described the outside of\
\
this temple ; we shall now, according to promise, shew the inside of it,\
Stepping into the portico,  saw over the door of the entrance  carved lyre;\
whence . inferred that this edisice was sacred to Apollo. Going into it,  was\
transported with the sight of all the fine things so artfully worked and of such\
rich materials.\
In the middle stood the figure of the god on  high pedestal. At the four angles\
of this pedestal sat the Four Seasons, each holding an horn silled with the particu-\
lar fruits and flowers of the scasons. All these were of beautiful plain white\
marble. The figure of Apollo was naked, crowned with laurel; and holding \
sceptre in its hand.\
The floor was inlaid with variety of costly stones in the form of  terrestrial\
globe, in the center whereof stood the aforesaid figure.\
The arch-work was azure blue, but  could not certainly perceive it to be Mo-\
faic ; it was adorned with the seven planets, and other constelations, all in gold.\
Near the windows, between the two pilasters, were niches silled with figures, each\
representing one of the months of the ycar; they had the form of young men, and\
were cut in whitish marble.\
The whole building consisted also of marble, but not so fine as that of the\
figures ; for here and there under the niches in the mouldings, and about the\
windows it was veiny. In  basement running round the temple, was carved \
continued bass relief; the figures ofit were about four feet high, and of fine white\
stone. The other inside division was the same as we have already described it to\
be without ; the undermost part being composed of the lonic Order, the middle-\
most of the Roman, and the upper of the Corinthian.\
Over the sirst cornices appeared terms, instead of pilasters; these represented the\
hours, and with their heads supported. the cupola; they were inthe shape of young\
virgins, to the number of twenty-four. It would be tedious to describe them,\
and their badges of distinction singly; and the rather, since Caesar Ripa has so\
handsomely done it.\
Next,  took notice of the orderly disposition and proportion, which was ju-\
dicioussy observed throughout the building ; for Apollo\'92 figure was, as  guessed,\
eight feet high, and those about him seven feet and an half; the young men, re-\
presenting the months, were seven feet, and the terms for the hours, six,or six and\
half. This proportion not only seemed so large, but the imagined height really\
appeared to me to be such, without abatement for distance, as seen from under-\
neath. Reflecting on this neatness, Ithought it strange for people of sense, nay,\
great\
\
\
\
\
\
\
great masters, to agree, that  large window should come over  small one, or \
giant be set above  young child, and how such things should look becoming.\
The undermost bafs reliefs consist of smaller figures than those in the upper work,\
not without reason, for the walling wherein they stand, as well as that figure-\
work, bear throughout the building; nevertheless, he, who duly considers the\
matter, and such  sight, will soon alter his opinion ; for since Apollo, or the sun,\
is the largest of all created things, and the chief of the universe, observed by the\
heathens, by his quality among irrational creatures, as the father of the four sea-\
sons, he is the biggest and principal figure. The four seasons, brought forth by\
him, are somewhat less, and the months inferior to them in bigness, to which the\
hours must give way again, because twenty-four of them make but one natural\
day. We ought also to observe, that the four seasons are of  more composed\
countenance : the months reprefent young men still growing, and the hours shew\
nimble virgins.\
Is not this division very elegant, with respect to architecture, since every thing\
keeps its relation and property ;  good architect employs his thoughts about\
all those particular objects, in the compartion ofhalls and apartments ; according\
to which,  good master ought to accommodate himself in the painting of\
buildings.\

Of the Painting of CIELINGS, or PLaFonds.\
\
CHAP  Of Cieling-Painting in general.\
\
AMONG all the parts of Painting, none is so difficult as that of\
\
\
\
\
\
than an upright piece on  wall or over  chimney : this is owing to\
\
\
\
ignorance, and an indifference in some people what their cielings\
\
are daubed with, so as they be but quickly finished, dazzle the eye\
and cost little. Formerly they were contented with foliage slightly painted, for\
saving expence, and that in places of consequence only ; whereas now, according\
to the present state of the painters and times, they can have other things for the\
same price, and the painter making no great matter of it, they lay hold of the\
opportunity, causing the whole cieling to be silled with histories and emblems,\
whether they be suitable or not.\
We sce that all things from small beginnings improve, and at last come to per-\
fection, through the industry of judicious artists : even so it is in painting ; for \
remember to have se\'ebn many cielings with figures, landscapes, sea-fights, battles,\
&. without any fore-shortening, as if painted on an upright wall; and others\
which were represented from underneath more or less, and yet withoJf fore-\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
shortening; as also some which fore-shortened, but had no point of sight : whence\
it is evident, that without regard to perspective, such pieces cannot possibly be\
brought to the aforesaid perfection. Now, for order sake, let us examine into\
the name of this branch.\
The word (Plafond) is French, and signifies  flat or level superficies, fit to be\
covered with boards or cloth, whereon to paint or plaister such representations or\
ornaments as we think proper, consisting mostly of histories with flying figures,\
skies with birds, flowers, and many other things: but the true sense of the word\
(Plafond) imports,  cieling of Halls, Apartments, Temples or Galleries, even\
all that hangs over head and is parellel with the ground. Such pieces are called\
Opticat, because they must be viewed from an assigned distance, without which,\
they ainavoidably appear mis-shapen, as we shall hereafter shew.\
In the matter itself we ought to consider the nature of  plafond, or cieling\
painting, and wherein it differs from  wall painting; as first, in the fore-shorten-\
ing of the objects, and secondly, in the colour;  speak with respect to the objects\
contained in one and the other, such as buildings, balustrades, figures, and other\
things occurring in ordonnances ; all which, in an hanging picture, retain their\
perfect heights and breadths, shortening in thickness only ; whereas, in plafonds,\
or cielings, neither height, measure nor proportion are to be observed; in  word,\
every thing foresshortens, except the bafis and the cap or top : what is round re-\
mains so, and what is square keeps its angles, whether in the middle, in profile,\
high or low. As for the colours, they doubtless must also differ much from those\
of hanging pictures, for they ought to appear more beautiful, not only in the\
light, but affo in the fhades,  mean in  clear light, as we may eafily appre-\
hend.\
We ought moreover to know, that by means of optics or practical perspective,\
we can make crooked things look strait, hollow or rising ones flat and even, and\
cause them outwardly to appear what they really are not; as the famous . Niceron\
and others have plainly demonstrated. Wherefore, we need not wonder, that so\
few painters excel in this branch of the art, since they are little conversant with\
the practical part of perspective, though without it it is impossible to execute \
good cieling-piece. It is certain that many painters are rash enough to undertake\
such  piece of work, and sometimes they happen to perform good things, (for\
laboriousness and daily practice often contribute much) nevertneless they do not\
inquire whether their methods be the shortest or longest, commonly chusing that\
which first offers, drudging witheut certainty, and led by meer chance.\
CHAP. II. Of the usual Difficulties in Cieling-Painting.\
IRST, we are at  great stand, because we cannot use the life either in the\
nudities or flving draperies, though they be the principal objects.\
Secondly, Because we cannot, without great trouble, find the true and certain\
places of the figures we introduce ; for which reason, they must mostly be done\
by guess.\

\
Thirdly, Because we cannot duly view the work as long as it is on the easel.\
Whence,\
Lastly, It follows, that the master is always in pain for the effect of the paint-\
ing in being fixed in its place.\
These difficulties are not  little vexatious, even to one who understands his\
business; for it is otherwise with those who make more use of their hands than\
careful than others. Paint as many cielings as you please, as long as you do not\
believe that there are grounds and rules for it, and remain in this ignorance, you\
will never surmount the aforesaid difficulties. The most skilful master is often at\
 loss in this part of painting. Let us then in the first place learn perspective, and\
what it shews us ; since thereby only we may arrive at this laudable study, which\
otherwise is impossible.\
CHAP. III. Of fore-sbortening Objects in Cielings.\
VT is-obvious, that the off-scape in  common picture, is the part which retires\
or goes off from us, leffens and grows faint, and that the horizon is an utmost\
distance limiting our sight.\
Contrary, in cielings, our off-scape and boundary of sight is the firmament or\
starry sky ; whereby objects, the higher they are, lessen the more, even to insensi-\
bility, not only in their proportions and neatness, but also in their colours.\
Here we ought to observe, that all objects of what shape or form soever, keep\
their due breadth, provided they are parallel with-the horizon: for instance, placc\
 square stone so as to be viewed directly against it, or  figure in the same manner.\
Here you see that the top and bottom\
(In Plate LXI. we exhibit  square body. )\
of the said stone keep their squares, and that the upper and under corners of it fall\
perpendicularly from the point of sight; moreover, that, however the said square\
is turned, thetop and body always make  right angle, and consequently the\
hindermost extremity is parallel with the foremost. It is the same with figures of\
other objects.\
Place, for instance,  man on one or other side of the piece, standing up-\
right, and the point of sight in the middle; let him be in profile, and have both\
his shoulders of equal height, and you will then perceive that the shoulders, from\
one to the other keep to their full breath, and their figure its full thickness from\
top to toe. Thus we see evidently, that there is no other fore-shortening than in\
the length ; or to say better, in the height; and the more the figures, or other\
objects, rise and approach the point of sight, the shorter and more mis-shapen\
they become; because in their breadth they retain their measure and proportion,\
as before has been said. This is  principal rule, and ought alivays to be observed.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
As for buildings, . Boss\'e9 gives full precepts touching them, in the latter part\
of his book of Perspective ; vet  have room to say, that when we would place\
columns over columns for galleries, we ought to draw  rising line through their\
centers, from the basis or ground to the point of sight, even through each balis-\
ter, and thus find the due proportion of their heights as well as the breadths, by\
the help of  gradation line.\
This, with respect to proportion, must likewise be observed in designing figures\
and other objects, as  shall hereafter shew by examples.\
This fort of painting is not only the most artful but also the most difficult, as\
 have before said ; because, although we understand the rules and practice of it,\
it appears nevertheless disagrecable and deformed ; which no one can be \'e0 judge\
of but the master himself, unless it be put up in its proper place, and seen at\
the due distance.\
\
CHAP. IV. Of the Sizes of Cieling Figures.\
HE figures which we paint in cielings, ought not to exceed the common\
size of  man, to wit, six feet and an half, when they are so low as to be\
\
even with the cieling; but being higher, and sitting on clouds or flying, they\
must lessen and go off, as perspective teaches. Yet we may represent the deities\
as big aswe pleafe, provided they be not painted with more strength than other\
figures; it even sometimes happens, that when they almost vanisn out of sight,\
thev have yet human bigness.\
Eun-shine is the most proper and agreeable in spiritual representations.\
As for the glory of each deity in particular, they keep it when they appear to\
men, but when they are represented in heaven it is  mixture of many smaller, pro-\
ducing one great fhining. To do this artfully is not  matter of the least conse-\
quence ; and he is  great master, who, instead of dark, thick and heavy clouds\
like wool-sacks, places his figures on thin, transparent and almost inseniible va-\
pours.\
It will not be improper, in this chapter, to mention something of flying figures\
in the air.\
Though the air be seldom without  wind, and this may always be somewhat\
perceived, it is nevertheless not advisable to make it appear in cielings; because,\
if the wind were stirring, the sigures slying before it would seem to be motionless;\
and contrarily, those which are sitting or standing shew as much violence as the\
slying ones : for this reason no wind must come into the piece but what the ve-\
locity of each sigure causes, that we may plainly see by what motion the drape-\
ries are slung, as also the places the figures are going to or returning from, one\
gently waving and the other nimble and swift.\
The different stuffs are very proper to this on such occasions, and they very\
much conduce to express the matter; as the ressexion of ruffling silks for waving\
figures\
\
\
\
\
\
\
figures and which are gently descending, thin and sleazy silk for swift and down-\
flying figures, and the most pliant or thick silk or stuff for sitting, lying or stand-\
ing ones. The secret and importance of  fine stirring cieling piece lies chiesly\
herein.\
As for the making of the coloured stuffs of flying figures, because they cannot\
be put on the layman, and therefore not painted after the life, we cannot lay\
down any rules about them ; nothing but  good conception and natural judg-\
ment, joined to continual practice and observation, can bring the artist to per-\
fori it. We must use these means, and be perfect in them; observing what\
stuff is most proper to the occasion, as we have before intimated.\
We ought also to take dare that the thin stuffs be warm and transparent against\
that the flying figures never seem to be upright, as if standing, much less to be\
standing; but always sitting, kneeling, lying or slying, unless in the case of peo\'ac\
ple supposed to be on cielings or galleries, who then are either standing, stoop-\
ing or kneeling, as the subject requires.\
Let me say, that we ought sometimes to make some additions to the disposition\
of the general and particular objects; but with as much caution as possible, that\
the inability of the artist and the deficiency of the work may not appear.\
CHAP. . Method for viewing  Cieling-piece on the Easel, as if on\
the Cieling.\
 have already observed the difficulties arising in cielings, with respect to\
\
the use of the life, and in laying down rules subservient to it. Now,\
had  my sight  should certainly find out some; but since this is impracticable\
without figural demonstration, and  cannot possibly verbally do it, Ishall never-\
theless shew some methods, which, though they may seem trifling, have always\
been of service to me and of little trouble in their use.\
After sketching my ordonnance upon paper,  fixed it against  low cieling;\
then taking  looking glass and sitting under it,  with ease exactly considered\
every thing, observing what was wanting in it, and thus  marked and corrected\
faults as much as possibly  could. Next,  drew each figure, whether naked\
or cloathed, after the life, in such manner as shall hereafter be shewn. Then \
dcad-coloured my piece with such light as  thought proper. After this  took\
the looking glafs again and held it over my head, in order to view commodiously\
the piece standing behind me, inclining  little backwards on the easel as if it were\
against the cieling, and casting my eyes every where, first on the general design\
and then on the particular parts; this examen  repeated, till, by several correc-\
tions,  found that  had brought the piece to my fancy. Here be mindful not\
to take too near  distance, to the end the glafs may take in the whole piece ;\
for\
\
\
\
\
\
\
for which reason  sometimes got with the looking glass on  chair or table, and\
having my pallet and pencils in readiness, and brought my piece into such for-\
wardness,  finished it without further looking back.\
 will now, for the service of those who may find it useful and necessary, also\
treat,\
CHAP. VI. Of Designing after the Life, for the Use of Cieling\
Painters.\
TE must not flatter ourselves, that cieling painting can be performed with-\
 out good knowledge in proportion, since, as has been said, we cannot\
\
conveniently make use of the life ; for, how great soever your skill may be, you\
will find difficulty enough, though the life were before you, to bring it on the\
eloth. Nevertheless, to shew that it may be done, and that  have often used the\
life, Ishall, for the service of those who are not sparing of pains, lay down my\
manner of doing it.\
After  had set the model, whether of man or woman, on an high place, ac-\
cording to my sketch,  sat down on the floor with my back against the scaffold,\
with  looking-glass between my legs, which  moved and turned about so long,\
till the model appeared in it in such  manner as  wanted according to my point\
of sight; and then designing it on drawing paper as correct as possible,  painted\
after this design without any trouble.\
As for the dresses  managed them in the same manner, casting the garment on\
the layman accordirg to my sketch;  mean without slying, which is  thing im-\
possible and depends only on imagination.  then placed the layman, thus dres-\
sed, on an high tressel, and sat down against it in the manner aforesaid, and made\
 design of the dress; if it was  flying or lying figure,  made shift with pack-\
thread, wires, or such like means, as well as  could, sparing for no trouble, when\
the matter was important and  had  mind to do something fine.\
 used the same method in designing after all sorts of plaisters, as faces, vases,\
urns, ornaments, capitals, festoons of flowers, &. in order to have them from\
underneath. Thus  mastered the greatest difficulties occurring in this study.\
However,  did not this before my cloth was in readiness for it, that  might not\
mistake; since, notwithstanding all our care in some things, especially upright\
standing objects, we may easily be deceived.\
As to the preparation of the cloths for our design, as likewise the dead-co-\
louring, in order to sinish, and thereby refresn our memories,  shall now treat of\
them.\
First,  six the point of sight either within or without the piece, as my place\
of standing directs; then  strike with  chalked thread, from the said point, as\
many lines over my piece as  find necessary to serve all my upright standing ob-\
