CHAP. . Of Still Life in general.\
HAVING thus far treated of the power and dignity of the noble art of paint-\
ing, together with the lustre and advantage accruing to those who tho-\
\
roughly consider and put it in execution, we shall now for the sake of weak\
capacities, proceed to Still Life, or immoveable and inanimate things ; such as\
flowers, fruits, gold, silver, stone, musical instruments, dead fish, &. and shew\
which\
\
\
\
\
\
\
which are the best and of most advantage. These may in their turns serve for ma\'ac\
terials for  natural composition wherewith to please all sorts of men, the great\
as well as the little, the learned as ignorant. Wherefore out of many, we shall\
fix on the following olijects, as the most beautiful, elegant, and agreeable.\
Flowers.\
.\
 Fruit,\
 Gold, silver, and other rich things.\
 Mufical instruments.\
These four forts, artfully ordered and performed, may serve for the ornament\
of halls and cabinets as well as the best paintings, provided they have  proper\
light and hang together. But we must know in the first place, what constitutes \
good still life piece, since though it be naturally handled, nothing but  good\
choice can charm the senses, and-bring fame to the master. It is weakneis to\
think that faded flowers should please, much less in  picture : Or, who would\
have  piece of ordinary unripe or rotten fruit in his best room, and among \
cabinet collection, seeing the life itself is so disagreeable ; Such rubbish  did for-\
merly admire; but, as they only shew the deformities of nature,  have no ap-\
petite to view them any more. But, to return to the subject.\
My opinion is, that the beauty and goodness of  still life consists only in the\
most choice objects : \'92say the most choice; as among flowers, the most rare\
and beautiful, and the same in fruits and other things. These will gain the maf-\
ter credit, especially with the addition of some particular significations proper to\
them. It is not probable that wealthy people should be delighted with old-\
fafhiioned plate and furniture, when they can have every thing more beautiful and\
elegant; and, as improbable, that judicious lovers of music should be pleased\
with the modern lyre, dulcimer, or bag-pipe. As for cabages, carrots, and tur-\
nips, as likewise cod-fisn, salmon, herrings, smelts, and such like, which are\
poor and mean ornaments, and not worthy of any apartment; he who is pleased\
with them may seek them in the markets.  as little approve of horse furniture\
and hunting equipage; though these latter with wild boars, stags, hares, phea-\
sants, partridges, and other fowls, depending on princes and noblemens fancies,\
are more tollerable.\
Having- thus in general touched on still life, let the judicious determine\
which sort is best and most advantageous cither to the painter or purchaser.\
As for me,  think eloquence very charming to the ear; but goodness alone\
makes beauty aimable. What is  fine flower, apple, gold cup, or well tuned\
violin, without good smell, delicate taste, proper use, and agreeable sound ?\
Goodness  say, ought to be perfectly apparent : the smell, taste, hearing, or sound\
cannot be painted; but may be in some measure expressed by occult fignifications,\
either in bass-relief by fables, hieroglyphics, or emblematic, figures, or by ma\'ac\
ny other things, if the will be not wanting.\
As to the nature and property of the plices for still life, they are two-fold,\
close and open; the one representing it as if hanging against  wall or wainscot,\
and the other as lving on  bench or table, or on the ground.\
We also suppose, that no objects used in still life ought to be represented less\
than the life.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
It is likewise improper, and against the nature of still life, to introduce, in any\
of the before-mentioned choices, coloured back-works, or vistos, either close or\
open; that is, landscape, architecture, or any kind of living cr\'e9atures, which\
would spoil the very name of  still life : moreover, it is difficult, if not im-\
possible, for such  painter to hit every thing; and granting he can,  yet ques-\
tion whether he would be pleased with the title of  still life painter.  say, then,\
that the depth of the picture is only to be represented by an hanging curtain, or\
 bass relief of wood or stone, of such  colour and tint, as best suits the general\
decorum; th\'e9 one darkish, and the other somewhat lighter. With Flowers, \
dark grey back ground suits better than  white, yellow, or red one. With Fruit,\
white and grey marble, but not yellow or red : yet, as  fine bass relief requires\
more sxill than  flower or fruit, and such like, you may, instead thereof, in-\
troduce  niche, with  god or goddess\'92 bust therein, proper to the subject;\
as  Flora, Pomona, Bacchus, Apollo, Diana, or others, according to the intent\
of your design, and as you would have it bear either  particular or general\
meaning, which each of those sigures will supply in abundance. Flowers are\
various, and, like fruits, may be divided into three sorts, to wit, the Spring,\
Summer, and Autumn; and having different qualities, are fit for many fine and\
uncommon designs, in conjunction with bafs reliefs or busts, as  have said, with\
this caution, that with slowers suit no fruit, but ears of corn, as being airy and\
pliable ; but among fruits may be some flowers, especially such as alude to\
rest and mirth; as poppies and rofes. And yet these agree best with grapes,\
either in garlands or festoons.\
Let us now, for exercise and improvement in this point, observe what the\
learned say. The white lilly is sacred to Juno; turnsol to Apolbo; the rose to\
Venus ; Diana and Somnus claim the poppies; Ceres, the corn-flowers; Juno, the\
pomegranates ; Bacchus, the fig-tree and vine; Ceres, or lsis, the peaches and\
ears of corn ; Venus and Apollo, the apples; Ops, or Mother Earth, every thing\
she produces throughout the year. Of instruments, the lyre is dedicated to\
Apollo, Mercury, and the Muses; the flute, to Pan and Venus; the trumpet, to\
Mars, &.\
CHAP. II. Designs for Bass Reliefs proper to Still Life.\
TITH flowers fuit Zephyrus and Flora, or Venus and Adonis, in\

With fruits, Ceres and Pomona, or Pomona and Vertumnus. With grapes,\

With musical instruments, Apollo and the nine Muses ; Orpheus playing, or Arion\
on the dolphin. With.  timbrel, cornet, and cymbal,  bacchanalian sacrifice,\
feast, or dancing.\

To the three seasons, as Spring, Summer, and Autumn, in one piece, we may\
apply Venus, Ceres, and Bacchus sitting together, according to their ranks.  ex-\
clude the Winter, as improper and disagreeable, and admitting of no other than\
poor interpretations ; such as Hunger, Penury, &. which tnis season brings\
with it.\
That these bafs reliefs may have due decorum, you must observe, that in gar-\
lands they ought to be octangular; in festoons, round; and in groups, or\
bunches, square, and parallel with the frame, especially when disposed hanging\
above, below, and on the sides; but when in corners,  compartment suits better,\
and this to be square above, and semi-circular at bottom and both sides. Thus\
much as to close bafs reliefs in general. As for the relief, the slatter it is, th\'e8\
better, and without the least ground-shade, in order to prevent all mastery and\
confusion.\
Touching the other sort of still life, either standing or lying in deep niches,\
or on benches or tables, we have before observed, that it ought not to be repre-\
sented less than the life, and therefore must come quite forward in the piece, as\
appearing then in its full force and quality ; even much better with  light com-\
ing from without, than within;  front than side light.\
There are three sorts of grounds, which elegantly set off fruits. Grapes, espe-\
cially the blue, and cherries, blue plumbs, and all fruits inclinable to be dark,\
require one of free-stone; but apples, peaches, and apricots, appear better on \
dark grey ground. There is  third sort, as pumpkins, melons, oranges, straw-\
berries, and others, which best become  white ground, whether they be lying on\
 bench or table, or in  deep niche.\
 shall now describe some designs. which  hope will not be unacceptable to the\
artist. The first contains the three blooming seafons.\
Table, or Ordonnance-\
This piece exhibits  compass-headed niche, square within, and its depth equal\
to its diamcter: therein  place  beautiful vase, either of chrystal, copper, or\
gold, with flowers, of which  set the shortest stalk in the middle, and the others\
spreading on the sides : above, in the middle, on  ring,  hang two or three\
bunches of the largest sort of grapes : to the ring  fasten  small ribbon, on\
which loosely hang ears of corn, intermixed with corn-flowers, taken up and tied\
in the upper corners of the piece, and hanging down the fides : below, round the\
vase, lies sine and palatable fruit, of the largest and best fort; as melons, lemons,\
fresh figs, pomegranates, walnuts, as well as apples, peaches, China oranges, &.\
This is the substance of the piece.\
The disposition is thus. The festoons, in bunches of an hand\'92 length, are\
parted with greens, and tied, which greens cover the stalks of corn, and being in-\
termixed, as is said, with some blue flowers, produce an agreeable mixture, with-\
out mastery. The jaums setting them off, are grey stone, and the ribbon, dark\
violet. The grapes of the largest sort, tied to  copper ring, are, in the middle,\
white, and thosc hanging on each side, blue, with  green leaf or two : this group\
is\
\
\
\
\
\
\
is well set off against the shade of the hollow of the niche, without drawing the\
eye froi the principal. My intention is, to dispose the flowers into  large mafs\
of beautiful and light ones; the strongest and fullest to be in the middle, consist-\
ing of white, yellow and light red: the highest next the grapes to be  turnsol,\
and on the sides, others of less force and colour, intermixed here and there, with\
 beautiful blue one. And, because the vase, on account of the room which the\
fruits lying about it, take up, cannot stand quite forward, the flowers spreading on\
the sides must be in snade. The fruits  dispose contrary again; as the biggest on\
the left side, and the smallest and most tender, such as peaches, apricots, and\
plumbs, on the right: they should be ltalian fruits, especially the lemons, at least\
two fists big, as being the chief of the group, and governing the rest. If, besides\
the seasons, you would represent some other meanings, add  lyre, violin, or other\
musical instrument, which may be set or hung against the light side of the aforesaid\
hollow; and thus the piece is complete.\
And now, curious still life paintersl view this example with attention, and con-\
sider whether  propose to you any difficulty above your abilities. Ye flower\
painters, Is it more troublesome and artful to imitate  grape, apple, or peach,\
than  rose, lilly, or turnsol; And ye, who practice fruit only, What difficulty has\
 flower more than fruits,  pomegranate or melon, inwardly or outwardly ; Any of\
these may be set standing or lying before you, as long as you please; and so may\
an harp, violin, lyre, or slute: these can store you, and are all in your power, and\
vour eyes can determine the proportions, measures, and forms, of all that stands\
still, hangs, or lies, and the soft pencil, skilfully handled, brings them naturally\
and properly on the cloth. Why then, do ye so often obstinately build on  single\
fort;  beautiful slower will certainly please the eye, but more, in conjunction\
with some fine musical instruments. Your cloth may take in some of each, and\
vet, ye most times do it with  single sort. If it be  flower-piece, your cloth must\
however be filled, as it also must, when the subject is fruits and musical instru-\
ments. When we say, JA man is  fine still life painter, we are to suppose, he\
paints every thing, either standing still, lying, or hanging.\

CHAP. III. Representations of Still Life, applioable to particular\
Persons.\
ALTHOUGH  have before said, that the famous Kaff excelled in still\
life, yet he could give as little reason for what he did, as others before and\
fince : he only exhibited what occurred to his thoughts-; as  Porcelain pot or\
diss, gold cup, mum-glass, rummer of wine, with lemon-peel hanging on it,\
clock, Horn of mother of pearl, gold\'92or silver footed, silver dish of peaches, or else\
cut China, oranges or lemons,  carpet, and other ufual things, without any\
thought of doing something of importance, which might carry some particulai\
meaning, or be applicable to something. Neverthelefs, to shew that this may be\
done as well in still life, as in other representations.  fhall give the following\
sketches made applicable to particular persons.\
Table, or Ordonnance, adapted to  triumphing Warrier,\
Herein we exhibit some arms, viz.  steel breast-piece, an helmet, elegantly\
wrought, shield and sword, with the hilt representing an eagle or lion\'92 head, \
pike or spear, bent bow and  quiver of arrows, also iome crowns of laurel, palm,\
and olive. Above, on the frame, may be fastened on two rings,  gold chain, to\
which hangs an heart, beset with precious stones, coming down to the breastapiece,\
and over it may be the motto of the hero to whom we apply the subject. We\
exhibit further,  gold crown, bracelets and rings, an hat with feathers, and  dia-\
mond button, and  trumpet. Under these, fies an embroidered coat on the table,\
with  sleeve hanging down from it. On the wall, or in  small table, may be\
seen in bafs relief, Apollo having killed the dragon Python, or Perseus and Andro-\
meda; or  man in  lion\'92 sxin, tearing open  tiger\'92 mouth, and near him,\
 club.\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
Comment on the aforesaid Objects.\
The breaft-piece was anciently taken for  mark of understanding and defence ;\
for, as it guards the breast, it preserves life.\
The helmet denotes an inclination for war, and  martial spirit.\
The shield also,  token of defence, was so much regarded by the ancients, that\
they made  prefent of it to conquerors, in consideration of their valour and con-\
duct. Virgil, in his ninth Book, mentions Aneas\'92 ordering  fhield to be prought\
to him, wherewith to reward the fidelity and valour of Nijus. The Argives had\
eustom of marching young men (who had by notable exploits merited the hongur\
with the shield of Enhippus carried before them triumphantly, through their towi\
and territories. We also read, that the palladium, which the ancients believed\
fell from heaven, was  shield, mysteriously representing the protection of the\
Roman people and empire; and, according to Numa Pampilius\'92 explanation, the\
shield implied success and prosperity, whereby he endeavoured to buoy and com-\
fort the Roman people, on their being sorely visited, in his eighth year, with  pesti-\
lence which threatened the destruction of all staly. The shields were moreover de-\
dicated to those, who had saved the town and common-wealth from any great and\
imminent danger; and to perpetuate such.  benefaction, and as  spur to virtue,\
they caused the story to be engraved or carved on their shields. The shield and\
pike also signify war, chiefly in retrieving the damages sustained by the enemy,\
and in putting them to flight, and destroying them. Yet weapons are of little\
advantage, if not used with wisdom and understanding : wherefore, we generally\
see Pallas represented with  shield and pike; the latter signifying force, and quick-\
ness of apprehension.\
The pike, or-spear, also denotes the spreading of  glorious name; for which\
reason, according to Plutarch, Lysippus adorned. the statue of Alexander with it, tho\
others represented him with thunder in his hand, intending thereby to immor-\
talize the atchievements of that hero. The pike or arrow also being thrown or\
shot at  mark, hieroglyphically signifies the spreading of  glorious name : yet,\
according to the ancients, the pike or spear not only implied royal grandeur and\
authority, but was likewise the usual reward for those who had shewed their bra-\
very in conquering the-enemy : as Pliny says, that Sicinnius Dentatus, for his ad-\
mirable walour, was presented with twelve pikes. Festus Pompeius-thinks that ge-\
n\'e8rals received the pike or javelin, in token of their being intrusted with the prin-\
cipal management of the war and empire; and, that therefore it was customary to\
tsell the prisoners publicly, sub hasta, or under the pike or spear.\
The sword, in reference to war, signifies fury, cruelty, fright, persecution and\
threatening with death.\
The bent bow is likewise  sign of war; and ithe arrows signify the people, or\
the enlargement of power; also velocity and quick motions.\
The crown-of laurel was the token of conquerors, and those who performed any\
glerious-act, -as the-ancient remains sufficiently inform us. And we learn from\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
history, that the Roman generals and commanders used in their triumphs, to\
present  crown of laurel to Jupiter Capitolinus.\
The ancient Romans also used to bestow  palm on those who triumphed, as \
general token of victory. And the palm-tree, though pressed by \'e0 heavy weight,\
will yet grow against it; wherefore in hot battles, it is esteemed  token of vic-\
tory, which can only be got by  firm resolution to refist and despise dangers and\
adversities.\
The olive is likewise  mark of victory ; the ancients adorning their trophies\
and warlike monuments with its branches, or decking the head, of the conqueror\
with  crown of its leaves.\
The gold chain was the Roman reward for valour and virtue; it not only re-\
compensing merit, but serving for  badge of honour, glory and esteem. The\
Roman history informs us, that the son of Tarquinius Priscus, though but fourteen\
years of age, charged the enemies in the open field and conquered them; where-\
fore to imortalize his valour, he was the first who was honoured with  gold chain :\
though according to others, Hersilius the first born of the ravished Sabines at Rome,\
first received that honour. We also read that Ricinnius Dentatus was sixty-three\
times rewarded with \'e0 gold chain, and twenty-five times with other gold or gilt\
presents.\
The heart beset with- precious stones, hanging down to the breast on  gold\
chain, signifies, that wholesome advice and deliberation spring from the innermost\
of the heart ; wherefore those who triumphed were introduced with this gold\
chain about their necks, in the utmost part whereof, or the heart hanging down\
to the breast, they imagined were contained herbs and balm, which secured the\
triumphers from malice and envy. Asconius particularly remarks, that the chil-\
dren of the nobles or free citizens wore those chains; but the Liberti or freed-anen,\
for distinction\'92 sake, had them only of silver and copper : to which Juvenal in his\
Satyrs alludes, saying, The poor must be content with copper.\
The gold crown and bracelets, which adorned both the shoulders and arms,\
were likewise the rewards of great actions. These gifts were preserved for poste-\
rity, as  spur for young people indefatigably to tread in the steps of their fore-fa-\
thers. Titus Livius, in his tenth Book says, That after the victory obtained over\
the Samnites near Aquilonia, Papirius on that occasion presented Sp. Nautius, his ne-\
phew, Spurius Papirius. four captains and  troop of pikemen, with bracelets and\
gold crowns ; giving the other captains, foot-soldiers and horsemen, bracelets\
and ornaments of silver, which they called Crnicula, or little horns. And Decius :\
the Tribune received  gold crown from Aulas Cornelius Cossus, for defending \
certain strong place belonging to the Romans, against the Samnites, and forcing\
them to raise the siege.\
The Romans also esteemed the rings as badges of honour and nobility : for ac-\
cording to Titus Livius, in his third Book, treating of the second Punic war, on\
Mago\'92 being dispatched by Hannibal to notify to the Carthagenians the bloody de-\
feat of the Romans in the battle of Canna, he poured out before them an heap of\
gold rings, taken as  booty from the slain; adding, to extol. the victory, that\
among the Romans, none but the great and noble were allowed to wear them. And\
towards\
\
\
\
\
\
\
towards the close of his ninth Book, he relates, that on Flavius\'92 being, in  pub\
lic asfembly, chosen Adilis, or superintendant of the public buildings, the no-\
bility were so distinguisned, that several of them laid down their gold rings and\
other tokens ofhonour and esteem. And the eloquent Ccero, in his fourth Ora-\
tion against Verres, reproaches him for bestowing in  public asfembly of the\
people, the tokens of honour, gold rings, on mean and unworthy people : with\
whom agrees Asconius, saying, That the Fasces, civic crown and gold rings were\
by the people looked on as badges of liberty and nobility, and always attended\
with honour and profitable incomes.\
The Greeks reputed the hat or cap as  token of noble extraction; wherefore\
they represented the head of Ubsses covered with  hat or cap, as being noble both\
by father and mother. For this reason, we commonly. see on ancient coins and\
medals an hat or cap circumscribed LIBERTAS.\
The diamond is indisputably the hardest, and for its sparkling the most beau-\
tiful and perfect of all precious stones, and (which is most surprising and remark-\
able) it resists the consuming fire, without losing any of its virtue or excellence.\
Wherefore it is used as an hieroglyphic of immoveable firmness in prosperity\
and adversity : accordingly, the ancients also attributed to it  supernatural qua-\
lity of freeing the heart from vain fear and despair, and that it never left  man\
either in his pressures or dangers, when principally he ought to be master of\
himself.\
The plume of feathers also signifies honour and nobility, and,\
The trumpet, esteem and an immortal name.\
The embroidered coat called Tunica Palmata, was an under garment commonly\
worn by those who triumphed ; acording to Titus Livius in his tenth Book : and\
Isidorus Hispalensis, in his Originum, lib.  says, That those who had conquer-\
ed used to receive  gown called Toga Palmata or Toga Picta, from the victo-\
ries and palm branches worked in it. And Macrobius, lib.  Saturnal, cap.\
 affirms that Tullus Hostilius first introduced this garment among the Romans.\
The twofirst bass-reliefs explain themselves, and by the third we mean strength;\
for the lion\'92 skin implies high understanding and resolution of mind, and the club,\
conduct and intrepidity.\
Second Ordonnance, relating to  Judge.\
In this we reprefent  pair of scales,  sword, looking-glass, septre topped\
with an eye,  board with  triangle thereon inclosing the number  and the\
image of truth, an hazel wand and fasces,  scythe, rod, ax, gold chain, staff twined\
with ivy,  large folio book, whereto is affixed the coat of arms of the common-\
wealth; and on the wall,  fruitful palm-tree in bass-relief.\
Explanation of these Objects.\
The fcales commonly placed in the hand of Justice, signify that weighing all\
mens\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
mens actions, sne affigns\'92to every one what God has decreed him; wherefore the\
heathens also represented Astr\'e6a ascended to heaven, and seated there between the\
lion and scales; intimating thereby, that  judge ought resolutely to punish trans-\
greffions according to their merit, without respecting persons.\
The sword likewise signifies justice and the severity of the law ; according to\
the apostle, :  ruler is the minister of God, and bears not the sword in vain, to\
execute wrath on him that doth evil.  looking-glass in the hand of Prudenoe denotes reforming of manners.\
The ancient Agyprians, by the hieroglyphic figure of the sceptre with an open\
&ye, fignified the absolute authority of equity and prudence ; which always watch-\
ing and penetrating mens actions, justly reward each according to his deserts.\
Platarch, in his doctrine of the Phythagoreans, intimates that the triangle is the\
most perfect figure of justice. Some palce the number . within it, because we\
therein see the godly character of the Almighty.\
The image of truth explains itself.\
The hazel wand fignifies ecclesiastical, and the fasces, secular dignity; or re-\
ligion and policy.\
The fcythe is is'the hieroglyphic of chastisement; as we read in the prophet\
Zechariah, that the scythe he saw in  vision, was going forth tocut off all thofe\
who stole or swore.\
The rod also implies punishment, for the support of good discipline and laws\
according to equity and justice.\
The Romans and some of the Greeks took the ax hieroglyphically for heavy\
chastisement; we see in the medals and coins of Tenedos, mentioned by Pollux :\
for the king of Tenedos, having published  law, that any person caught in adul-\
tery should be put\'92to death with the ax, and in compliance therewith not spared\
his own son, he commanded this story to be struck on the coins and medals, in\
order to be thereby immortalized.\
The Agyptians likewise applied the Bulla, or gold chain and heart to their\
judges; intimating, that making pure truth their only aim, they ought to be\
impartial, and give judgment without respect of persons.\
The staff twined with ivy signifies, that justice ought to be protected-: for by\
the staff is understood authority, and by the ivy protection, which should always\
flourish.\
The large folio book contains the statues and ordinances of the country.\
The fruit of the palm-tree represented in bass-relief, being of equal\'92size with the\
Jeaves, the ancients would thereby signify justice and equity. This tree also con\'ac\
sisting of lasting matter, and not altering or decaying so soon as-others, serves for\
 pattern of the- maintenance of justice without impediment or alteration : and as\
it never drops its leaves as others do, and refists alla pressure and weight, thereby\
is implied that judges ought not to be biassed, but withstand those who endea-\
vour to draw them from their-duty by fair words, gifts or intrigues.\

Ordornance relating to  Lawyer.\
In this table we exhibit  plaister figure of Mercury winged at head and feet,\
standing on  square stone pedestal, having in one hand his golden caduceus twined\
with serpents, and in the other an olive branch. By him is  plaister sphinx.\
Also  sword and fhield,  lyre or harp,  burning lamp, an ink-horn with pens\
and  roll of paper,  sieve, some of the principal law books, and abible. In \
small vase or pot may be set an iris or two. Above on  ring hang three garlands,\
one composed of laurel and ivy, another of cedar and myrtle, and the third of\
oak leaves. On the wall, or in  small table, we see in bass-relief the fable of\
Minerva brought forth out of Jupiter\'92 brain.\
Explanation of the Objects.\
Mercury implies the impreffion of words upon the mind, and the force of elo-\
quence : wherefore the ancients believed, he was the messenger and the interpre-\
ter of the gods.\
The square stone whereon he ftands, fignifies the regard for and stability of the\
laws and rules whereby to direct our speeches : for which reason Mercury is stiled\
Tetragonus or square, that is firm and certain.\
His staff or caduceus intimates, that obstinate tyrants must yield to the laws and\
Huent charms of eloquence. By the serpents twined about the golden rod, the\
ancients mean that eloquence, tempered with ingenuity and prudence, can easily\
bring men\'92to reason. Some also would have the golden rod in the hand of Mer-\
cury to\'92signify, the excellence and eminence of honourable offices due to those who\
employ their eloquence (the gift of heaven) in their neighbours welfare and the\
common good.\
By the olive branch in Mercury\'92 hand is understood peace ; for the ancients be-\
lieved it composed \'92the differences of contending parties.\
His wings at head and feet, were affigned him to signify the readinefs and force\
of eloquence.\
The sphinx shews, that nothing is so abstruse or occult that  lawyer\'92 penetra-\
'ting judgment cannot clear.\
We compare jurisprudence to\'92the sword and Thield : for as  warrior thereby\
defends. himself and annoys his enemy, so  council gets his cause by dint of\
cstrong arguments, and well grounded conclusions.\
The ancient Romans signified by the lyre or harp, \'e0 man of great learning\
and judgment; for that instrument is composed of divers strings and sounds, pro-\
ducing fine harmony; like the lawyer when he reconciles the difference of things\
to r\'e9ason, in order to make contesting parties agree. By the harp or lyre we al-\
so understand, that harmony arises from different and dissonant cords, and that\
people of contrary sentiments meeting together, may by  good union, settle and\
transmit to posterity, an excellent form of government. And as Plato in his\
Timaeus, stiles the soul  concert or sweet harmony, so concord may be justly\
called\
\
\
\
\
\
\
called the soul of the state. The Gree\'e8s and Romans say, the lyre was partly in-\
vented by Mercury, and partly by others.\
The shape of the ancient lyre is this : it was bent like two horns joined to-\
gether, having  swelling belly-and on top- handle. It is said to have had but\
three strings, and these could produce seven tunes, making  perfect harmony.\
The three strings were assigned in imitation of the three seasons of the year known\

The Agyptians understood by the ink-horn, pens, and roll of paper, all things\
whereby arts and sciences might be represented.\
by the sieve, the same people hieroglyphically meant, the fruitfulness of in-\
struction in arts and sciences; also the writers of sacred and mysterious things :\
foras the sieve separates the good from the bad, so their lawyers, who were also\
stiled priests, knew how, through their prudence and wisdom, to distinguisn be-\
tween things concerning life and death ; accordingly they made use of the word\
sieve for expressing what is true and known. Others sav, that by that implement\
is signisied  man of great knowledge and perfection, who can discourse of things\
divine and human with equal penetration. Moreover, as the sieve separates the\
slour from the bran, so experience fits us for discerning between good or bad,\
right or wrong. Wherefore Virgil in the first Book of his Georgics, rightly\
stiles it Mystica Vannus lacchi, the mystic fan, or sieve of lacchus. Some apply\
to this point the saying of the philosopher Antisthenes; that it were great folly not\
toknow how to distinguisn the corn from the chaff; meaning the learned and be-\
neficial citizens from illiterate.\

The Jewissi priests were commanded to set the people  pattern of chastity, piety\
and good behaviour, and to be always ready to walk in God\'92 paths, as appears by\
the white linen girdle, signifying the most sacred and mysterious doctrine, by which\

The seed of flax comes up as green as grass in  fhort time after sowing; and\
having blossomed and set its seed, it is then pulled, and rotted in water, and after-\
wards laid to dry in the sun; and then being beaten to  softness, it is combed\
and hatchelled, and spun into thread : after boiling in strong lye, it is made into\
 web of linen, and whitened for  dress to appear in before God. The care ne-\
necessary about this herb, which is an emblem of undefiled life, ought continually\
to be had in view, that laying afide things vain and unprofitable, we may, by\
means of science, render ourselves irreproveable, and through adverfity and temp-\
tations obtain the white garment of glory. The quick and easy growth of flax,\
shews how easily virtues and sciences are attainable, if we set readily about them.\

Almighty\'92 will always remaining with us: wherefore it is the duty of the mini-\

ferve. Next to the science for the improvement of manners,- divines should en-\
which the learned\
deavour at distinctness, plainness and order in their speeches,\
call dialectica; whose province is to determine controversies; and resolve doubts by\
reasoning justly ; for although, like Moses, they practised moral duties, and were\
received into the sanctuary, yet they touched not on sacred things, otherwise than\
by means of their speeches. After this, they enquired into natural philosophy, or\
physics, having for the subject, the universe, and all created beings; this science\
cleared their doubts and scruples, and prepared them for contemplating the glori-\
ous building of the heavens, in order to thank their Creator for the knowledge\
received. Their last study was theology, which as we have said, comprehends all\
sciences : this gives divines such  constant peace, as neither the regularity of hu-\
man deportment, purest eloquence, or the most exact enquiries into nature, could\
afford them. But this unchangeable peace and firm alliance with God, they ob-\
tain by submitting their knowledge, inclinations, and carnal affections to the rules\
prescribed by reason. This mutual friendship, which the Pythagoreans esteemed the\
main point in philosophy, leads us into the most secret part of the sanctuary, in or-\
der to view the glory of God, till at last, arriving at the highest degree of know-\
ledge, we courageously defeat Ofiris, or the enemy of our souls.\
Though the aforesaid instruction consists of four parts, yet Solomon, the wisest of\
men divides it into three sciences, to wit, ethics, physics, and metaphysics, which\
he has handled in his Proverbs, Ecclefiastes, and Song, or Canticum Canticoaum;\
teaching in the Proverbs, moral obligations; in Ecclefiastes, the nature of things ;\
and in his Song, the contemplation of things supernatural. This seems well to\
agree with the mention in scripture of the God of Abraham, lsaac, and Jacob; for\
Abraham\'92 obeying God in all things, shews an example of moral duties : lsaac\'92\
digging wells, and searching the depths of the earth, signifies physics, or natural\
philosophy ; and Jacob\'92 dream of the ladder, and the ascent and descent of the\
angels thereon, the contemplation of divine things. Both the Hebrez, Greck, and\
Latin divines have largely handled this subject. Even the elements seem to in-\
culcate this doctrine; for the earth, water, and air, by their wonderful conjunction,\
represent the different degrees of the sciences; the earth and moisture, implying\
the history of things, as  teacher of moral duties : the waters, disturbed by the\
winds, shew the turmoils happening in human actions, which ethics serve to allay :\
the air admonisnes, that, at length raising the whole force of our thoughts up-\
wards, we ought continually to contemplate the divine nature, called by the Grecks,\
Theologia, which is the top and limit of our understanding. We say nothing here\
of dialecticu, because it is subservient to ethics, physics and theology, in order t of those three sciences.\

tial justice. The Agyptians and Greeks, who were chiefly famous for emblematic\

The ancients understood by the emblem of the cock, the immortality and- divi\'ac\

CHAP. IV. Of the Origin, Nature and Quality of the Roman Tri-\
umphal Crowns, and other Rewards of Honour.\
WE learn from history, how noble the ancient Romans were in gratifving\
 the virtue, valour and conduct of their citizens, soldiers and commanders,\
besides their ordinary pay, with triumphal crowns, jewels and other presents, as an\
example to others to tread in the same steps, for the good of their country : and\
we judge this point very proper to be handled next to still life, as it will conduce\
to make the emblematic sense of  good piece more perfect.\
After  commander had gained either  great advantage or victory over the\
enemy in  siege, battle or sea-fight, he according to custom, made an exact in-\
quiry what persons had behaved with the greatest valour and resolution : and then\
placing himself on  stage, raised for that purpose, and returning thanks to the\
gods for the victory obtained, he commended the army in general for their steady\
adherence, and each company in particular which had shewed the greatest courage :\
then naming them one after another, he extolled their valour, stiling them friends\
and lovers of their country, and telling them how highly they obliged the com-\
monwealth by their loyalty and brave behaviour : and thereupon, in the name of\
the senate, he distributed among them many rich presents, consisting of crowns\
of gold and silver, girdles, gold chains, bracelets, rings for ears and fingers, ar-\
mour, shields, pikes, swords, javelins, standards, sine horse furniture, and other\
elegantly wrought warlike instruments ; which none durst use or wear, but those\
who had purchased them in the manner aforesaid. The Roman story abounds\
with such occurrences, but especially Titus Livius, who relates that the consul Pa-\
irius Cursor bestowed gold bracelets among four hundred men, and afterwards\
magnificently rewarded  whole legion. He tells us the same things of Scipio\
when he waged war with Spain and other countries : and we read, Lucius Antontus,\
son of Lucius Fubius Quadratus, was twice dignified by the emperor of Tiberius, with\
gold ornaments for the neck and arms.\
But besides the native valour and military discipline for which the ancient Ro-\
mans were particularly famous, we learn from Pliny and Salinus, that oftentimes \
single person by his virtue and valour obtained all the aforesaid ornaments; as we\
see in Marcus Sergius, who received almost oll those tokens of honour, and even in\
the battles of Thrasimenus and Trebir, and the bloody one at Canna, (in all which\
the Romans were defeated by Hanibal) he obtained  civic crown. It is related of\
this Sergius, that having in battle lost his right hand, and fixed an iron one in its\
place, he so managed with his left, as one day to slay four armed men one after\
another; and that in fights and skirmishies he had received twenty-three wounds\
in the fore-parts of his body : and yet this man is inferior to Lucius Sicinnius Den-\
tat\'e6s,\
\
\
\
\
\
\
tatus, overseer of the city of Rome ; of whom Pliny, Solinus, Valerius Maximus, and\
Aulus Gillius unanimoussy report that his great merit had gained him from the se-\
nate, above three hundred and twenty honorary presents of all forts, and that he\
nine times made his entrance in triumph with the generals, whom by his valour\
and conduct, he had assisted in their conquests; and that he could shew  great\
number of lances and pikes unironed, which as so many tokens of honour fell to\
his share ; as also eighteen gold and eighty-three silver neck-ornaments, twenty-\
five costly horse-furnitures, an hundred and forty bracelets, fourteen civic crowns,\
eight castrenses, three murals, one obsidional, and  know not how many naval\
or rostral crowns: he had received forty-five wounds, and those in his fore-parts\
only; disarmed the enemy thirty-four times, and fought one hundred and twenty\
battles : in  word, he was stiled the Roman Achilles.\
The crowns bestowed on men of particular merit, had degrees of dignity, and\
particular names suiting the nature of the victories; as, corana obsidionalis, cixica,\
triumphalis. ovalis, muralis, navalis and castrensis.\
The corona obsidionalis, or obsidional crown, was the most excellent of all ; for\
when  Roman town or camp, besieged and reduced to extremity, was relieved by\
 Roman captain, the commonwealth rewarded the action in the most noble man\'ac\
ner, viz. This crown, though made of grass, was accounted of more worth than\
if of gold and enriched with precious stones: the grass was pulled up in the field\
of battle, wherefore this crown is said to be sacred to Mars, which Boccatius seems\
to affirm) possibly because the grafs grows mostly in open places and fields of en-\
campment. The great Quintus Fabius was, in reward of his merit, by the general.\
consent of the senate and Roman people honoured with this crown, when in the\
second Punic war he delivered the city from the approaching ruin and extremity\
which Hannibal had brought it to. Amilius Scipio had the same gift in Africa, for\
rescuing the consul Manlius and his forces out of the power of the enemy. Calpur-\
nius obtained the same honour in Sicily ; as did also the incomparable Lucius Sicin-\
nius Dentatus.\
The corona-civica, or civic crown, was given to him who hrd preserved  Roman\
citizen from imminent danger, or released from captivity : this crown was made\
of oak sprigs and leaves with the fruit hanging at it, and by the general\'92 order,\
who gave it to the person set at liberty, put on the deliverer\'92 head : but though \
person had saved  king or other great ally of the Romans from falling into the\
enemies hands, yet he got not this crown which was only due to him who had freed\
 Roman citizen from death or slavery. Pliny says, this crown was also presented\
to him who slew the first of the enemy besieging  Roman town. It was next in\
dignity to the corona obsidionalis, and worn on several occasions, especially on the\
great festivals and solemnities; and in the plays and other public sports, those\
who were honoured withit sat next to the senate, and at their entrance were re-\
ceived by them with all the marks of respect. These persons, with their fathers\
and grandfathers, were entirely exempted from all charges and taxes, as having\
begot sons so beneficial to the commonwealth; they were also at liberty to accept\
or refuse public offices. Several Romans obtained this honour, especially the afore-\
said valiant Lucius Sicinnius Dentatus who fourteen times gloried in it; as Capitolinus\
did\

The famous Cicero was fo erowned by  partieular decree of th\'e8 senate, for having\
happily delivered the city from the imminent danger of the Catiline conspiracy.\
Thefe crowns though seemingly simple, as being made of grafs and sprigs of trees,\
were yet of greater account than thofe of gold and jewels: they were of oak, be-\
cause the acorn was the most ancient food, and because that tree was sacred to Ju-\
piter the tutelar god. The victors in the Capitoline games, instituted by Domitian,\
as also stage-players muficians and poets, were likewise crowned with oak-\
leaves.\
The corona triumphalis, or triumphal crown was given to the general, who hav\'ac\
ing overthrown the enemy in  pitched battle, had thereby either saved  Roman\
ally, or annexed some dominion to the commonwealth ; wherefore he was also in\'ac\
troduced inta the city in triumph riding in  gilt chariot drawn by four, or accor-\

its greennefs and red berries, and signified that the victory is attended with much\
trouble, danger and bloodfhed. Sextus relates that the soldiers used to follow the\
chariot of the conqueror, also crouned with laurel, to purify them on entering the\
city from the blood of the slain. By the fuffiage of the fenate, the victors in the\
wrestling games were honoured with the same crown : and it was anciently given\
oo men eminent for heroic poetry and eloquence : wherefore Hefiod says, ee The\
musea had crouned him with  sceptre and erown of laurel.  Roman priests\
and footh-sayers. likewise crowned themselves with laurel; even those who followed\
the army wore  sprig of it on their helmet, instead of  feather, because the tree\
was accounted and oakled by them  foretelling one.\
The coronz ovalis, (given to  genenal or other prime person, who had beat the\
enemy with little refistance, or having undertook the war without the exprefs com-\
mand of the senate had gined some considerable fortress, town or place) was made\
of myrtle-leaves,  tree facred to Venus. This crown denoted that the war was car-\
ried on without great bloodshed ; and therefore public rejoicings were made for it,\
but without much triumpb. When  victory was gained over slaves or pirates\
and robbers, the victors had the same sort of crowns, because such enemies were\
judged unworthy of feeling the Roman valour. The principal generals who ob-\
tained this, and the triumphal crowns aforesaid,  fhall mention among the\
triumphs.\
The coroua muralis, or mural crown, was the reward of  soldier or officer, who\
in asfaulting  town of the enemy, first advanced  ladder, and valiantly mounted\
the walls and made way for conquest. This: crown was of gold, reprefenting\
the battlements of the town-wall they had conquered; or else being like that\
which the poets afcribe to Cybele, the mother of the gods or Mother Earth ; round\
it were engraven lions, the emblems. of valour and generosity. Suetonius relates,\
that common soldiers received it as well as captains and generals, on  public\

The caroua navalis, or naval crown, was given to him who in  sea-engagement,\
first entered into an enemy\'92 ship, and made himself master of it. This crown\
was also of gold, and its circie set round with ships prows, Marcus Varro disdained\
not to receive it at the hands of Pompey the Great, for subduing the sea-rovers.\
Augustus presented it to Marcus Agrippa, on his gaining the upper hand in the sea-\

The same crown was the present of the ancient Athenians to those who fitted out\
ships of war for the public service, or first. landed and intrenched on the enemies\
ground.\
The Romans in process of time, placed an hedge-hog on the circle of this crown,\
because that creature\'92 defence lying in his sxin wherein he rolls himself up, he\
was esteemed the emblem of  sea-fight. This crown is ascribed to Diana, or the\
moon, as she inffuences the sea and its floods.\
The corona castrensis, was given by the chief commander, to him who in battle\
first entered the enemies camp. This crown was  gold circle, to which were af-\
fixed palisades of the same metal. They also had it who first destroyed the pali-\
sades of the enemy, and th\'e8reby opened  door for victory. This crown was\
the reward of  great number of Romans in those times of valour.\
Besides these degrees of honour, the Romans bestowed several privileges on those\
who excelled in warlike atchievements, causing them in the public pleadings, to\
fit in the sella cuyulis, or the pretor\'92 ivory chair, as we read of the great Scipie; and\
it often happened since all things centered in the voice and consent of the people,\
that some of the soldiery were invested with greater power and privileges. All\
generals who by conquest had enlarged the empire, were allowed to set up their\
statues in the consular dress. Augustus, to eternize the memories of all such gene\'ac\
rals as had augmented the state, ordamed, that next to the gods, the first veneration\
should be paid to them ; and for that purpose built  gallery in his palace, wherein\
to set 'their statues with all their honorary titles, notifying by proclamation, that\
he did this for bimself and succeslors, as an example to posterity to imitate the\
virtues and valour of such illustrious personages. Moreover it was  laudable and\
constant custom of the senate, to affign the children of fuch as fell in battle, the\
liberal enjoyment of the pay of their deceafed parents ; and to the old and maimed\
soldiers, as many lands in the provinces they conquered, as would comfortably\
support them and their families for the remainder of their lives. On this footing,\
the city of Seville in Spain and the fruitful country round it, were made  Roman\
colony by Julius Casar, and Corduba and several other places in divers parts of the\
world were applied to the same purpose. In  word, Roman services never missed\
 reward ; and for this reason the commonwealth produced more brave men than\
any other nation whatsoever ;. every one exerting himself to attain all the degrees\
of\

CHAP. . Of the Solemnities of the Roman Triumphs.\
TWO motives generally incite  man to do great things, either in times of\
peace or war; to wit, honour and immortal fame, or riches and profit.\
Generous souls always aspire at the former, and reject the latter, as below them.\
The Roman governinent knew perfectly well how to make its advantage of these in-\
ducements, in the encouragements given to its subjects; and we shall begin with\
the triumphs, by which they honoured and rouzed the valiour of their heros.\
The triumph was an entrance and welcome of  general, by decree of the se-\
nate after an happy expedition and the conclusion of  war, whereby, in the\
most solemn and pompous manner they shewed him their great esteem. On\
the day of entry, the inhabitants of all the towns flocked to Rome, and the whole\
citv, temples, streets, gates, houses and windows were hung with all sorts of\
costly stuffs, in gold, silver, and silk, and beautifully decked with great variety of\
green branches and flowers. In  word, nothing was wanting to shew either the\
power, magnificence or joy of the Romans on this occasion. The senate, clergy,\
nobility and most eminent citizens (and therefore the greatest part of Rome) richly\
dressed, met the conqueror without the town gates. He sat in an ivory chair,\
called sedes curulis, in  gold chariot sparkling with precious stones, and drawn\
cither by four or six white horses magnificiently equipped, and was dressed in \
garment of purple and gold, called toga palmata, crowned with laurel and the staff\
of\
\
\
\
\
\
\
of command in his hand, or else  winged image of Victory holding  crown of\
laurel or  palm branch.\
Sometimes this figure was placed behind him, holding\
in its right hand  crown of laurel over his head, as we see it both ways in the\
ancient bass reliefs and medals. The prisoners of war dressed like slaves, and\
with shorn heads, and the king or general, with the most eminent of the van-\
quishied were led in fettered couples before the chariot, which the Roman legions-\
followed in troops or companies, on foot and horse-back in their order, richly\
armed, and with their pikes and lances twined with laurel, as  token of general\
joy ; but they who had most singulized themselves in valour, marched on each\
side of the chariot with crowns of laurel on their heads, and palm branches in\
their hands. Before the conqueror went likewise some carriages laden with the\
arms, banners, gold and silver vases, jewels, gold and silver coin, taken as booty\
from the eneniy, together with the gifts and presents he had received from the\
friends and allies of the Romans. Next came some castles and towers of wood, ele-\
gantly carved, resembling the towns and fortresses gained of the enemy. In their\
passage the army feigned some battles, in so lively  manner as thereby to affect\
the spectators with all sorts of paffions, as forrow, joy, and fright. The variety\
of those sights was so great and excessive as to spin out the cavalcade for three or\
four days; and being arrived at the capital, all the arms. and booty, called Ma-\
nubiae, taken from the enemy, were hung up and deposited in the temple of Ju-\
piter, as an eternal memorial of the virtue of the conquerers. Here the senate re-\
turned them thanks for the service done to their country, and commonly chusing\
the victor as  coajutor in the government, the joy concluded with  magnificent\
entertainment. But for forming  better idea of these triumphs, and the order\
therein observed, Ishall, as far as my memory will permit, give some examples\
of them out of the Roman histories.\
Plutarch describes the triumph voted to Paulus Amilias, for his victory over the\
great Perseus, king of Macedonia, in this manner.\
First, the people of Rome and the neighbouring towns magnificiently dressed,\
appeared at the doors and windows in the balconies, garrets and on tops of\
houses in great multitudes, as spectators of the solemnity. All the temples in\
Rome richly adorned, were set open. The houses and streets were wonderfully\
garnished with all sorts of costly hangings and filled with greens, flowers, choice\
perfumes, and  thousand other fine and delightful things. And as the concourse\
of people was very great, men with staves were appointed to make and preseve \
lane or passage through them, for the march of the triumphers. The first day\
was spent in the procession of the banners, standards, ensigns, statutes, colosses,\
pictures, and figures, all placed on carriages elegantly painted, and slowly driven.\
The second day was taken up with the passage of the bright armour of the van-\
quishied king and Macedonians, placed on neat chariots or carriages made for that\
purpose. To these succeeded three thousand men, partly carrying the gold and\
silver coin in three hundred and fifty large silver dishes and vases, each weighing\
three talents, and carried by four men. The remainder of these men bore\
fountains and stately vases of filver, artfully wrought. On the third day appeared\
the first company, preceded by  great number of pipers, drums, hautboys and\
trumpets,\
\
\
\
\
\
\
trumpets, making  warlike mufic, as if preparing for an onset. These were\
followed by  hundred and twenty cows, decked with gilt horns and sacred linen\
coverings, and all sorts of green garlands wreathed with flowers, led for victims,\
by beautiful voung men richly dressed, and succeeded by  company of children,\
carrying gold and silver dissies for the use of the sacrifice. After these came the\
bearers of the gold vases with gold coin, in numberseventy-two, followed by several\
great officers of the retinue of Antigonus and Seleucus, late kings of Mucedonia, and\
even of Perseus himself,carry ing the excessive large gold vessel, weigh ing ten talents,\
and enriched with all sorts of precious stones and diamonds, which was made\
by Amilius\'92 express order. Next to these appeared the body-chariot of the con-\
quered king, and therein his coat of arms, diadem, or royal head-band, crown\
and ceptre. Then followed the children of the unhappy prince, attended by \
great numnber of his courtiers, as stewards, secretaries, and other such domestics,\
weeping and lamenting their slavery in such  manner, as, considering the vicitude\
of human affairs, to raise compassion in the spectators; especially the sight of the\
three innocent children, two sons and  daughter, who by reason of their tender\
age, were insensible of their unhappy condition. After these appeared the father\
dressed in black, according to the custom of his country, and walking full of\
terror and concern on this occasion. Next to him came his friends, favourites,\
and confidents, who fixing their eyes onhim, and bitterly weeping, moved many\
of the Romans themselves with tears in their eyes to pity both their and the king\'92\
forrowful condition. To these fucceeded the gold crowns which the ancient free\
cities had presented to the conquerer, as  gratulation for his victory : and then\
came Amilius himself, sitting on  gold triumphal chariot, dressed in  purple\
garment richly wrought with gold, with  larurel branch in his hand, and \
crown of the same on his head. He was followed by the army, horse and foot,\
orderly marshalled under their proper ensigns, having garlands of laurel and palm\
branches in their hands, and singing hymns in praise of the victor and victory.\
Thus Paulus Amilius made his triumphal entrance into the famous city of Romt,\
where he offered the booty in the temple of Jupiter Capitolinus, and returned the\
god thanks for his victory and triumph.\
All other triumphs were managed much in the same manner, with abatement\
of some circumstances, according to the pleasure of the general who was honoured\
with them.\
And, though we find the solemnities regulated by laws, precisely directing in\
what manner, at what time, and through what gates and streets the cavalcade\
was to pafs ; yet, as for the plays, shews, and other less appertenances, they were\
lessened or augmented at the will of the victor, with  liberty to chuse the chariot.\
History tells us, that the chariot was commonly drawn by four white horses; but\
we also find bulls used for the same purpose. Pompy the Great, having subdued\
Africa, made his entry on  chariot drawn by elephants. Suctonius relates, that\
Julus Caesar triumphed in one with forty elephants. The emperor, Gordianus\
triumphed in the same manner. Caius Marius having subjected Afiica and extend-\
ed the Roman jurisdiction into Agypt, was drawn by the same kind of beasts. Sci-\
po Agricanus triumphed with-elephants for the same reafon. The emperor Augustas\
on\
\
\
\
\
\
\
on his victorious return from the East, and ending the war with Anthony, was, by\
the confent of the senate and people of Rome drawn by four elephants. The em-\
peror Vespasian had the same honour on finishing several great wars in the East;\
the elephants denoting the. conquest of countries, where those creatures breed.\
Flavius, in his histories, tells us, that the emperor Aurelian, who was king of the\
Goths, made his entrance on  chariot drawn by stags. But, Marcus Antonius made\
use of tame lions, intimating, that in the civil wars, he would make the most va-\
liant submit to his commands : which Cicero in his Orations, called Philippicae,\
objects him, saying, That his triumphal chariot with lions, implied an arbitrary\
man aiming at monarchy.\
The Roman generals when they triumphed, had also  custom of carrying one\
or more young children in their chariots ; as we gather from Cicero\'92 speech before\
Murena. Some used to be attended with  great number of strange wild beasts,\
as lions, bears, tigers, rhinoceroses, panthers, dromedaries, and such like ; as\
Josephus, in his histories of the Vespasians, mentions. Others had vocal and instru-\
mental music and other diversions. Among these triumphs, those of Pompy the\
Great, Casar, the two Scipio\'92, brothers, and several emperors had something\
singular, as Blondus in his Treatise, intitled Rome Triumphant, largely discourses.\
The triumphing conquerors were likewise allowed to set up their statues in temples\
and public places, and to erect columns and costly structures of marble, called\
Arcus Triumphales, whereon were carved in bass relief their battles and victories\
for eternal monuments to posterity ; remains whereof we see to this day at Rome\
and elsewhere. Herein the Romans imitated the ancient Greeks, who, for  memo-\
rial of great actions, set up trophies, made in the following manner.\
In the place of victory, they fixed the highest tree to be found in the neigh-\
bourhood, and then chopping of the branches, they, in honour to the victor,\
hung on the remaining limbs, the arms of the vanquished, calling that tree, Tro-\
phaeum, from the Greek word Tropi, which signifies overthrow, slight and giving\
way, because the enemies were in that place put to flight. The Romans afterwards\
made use of them for the same purpose; for Sallustius in his Memoirs relates that\
Pompey having conquered the Spaniards, planted his trophies on the tops of the\
highest Pyrenees. And this custom afterwards grew into such esteem, that they\
were made of stone.  But according to scripture, the usuage was very ancient\
among other nations ; for it appears in chap.  of  Sam. that Saul having\
vanquished Agag, king of the Amalelites, and being come to Mount Carmel, set up\
an Arcus Triumphalis, or Place. In  word, the honour of triumphing was ac-\
counted by the Romans as  token of the highest esteem ; and therefore, to obtain\
it, their generals spared for no toils or dangers in warlike atchievements. Add to\
this, the riches commonly arising from such glory, by the presents made them\
by the allies and the booty of the enemy.\
In my opinion, historians have described the matter so circumstantially, on\
purpose to put princes and governors in mind of rewarding the deserts of their\
generals, soldiers and men of merit, and that the uuskilful, cowardly, and unfit\
for command iight not be ranged with those who willingly sacrificed their for-\
tunes, capacities and bodily labours to the benefit of their country. Accordingly\
to\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
to Paulus Orofiris three hundred and twenty persons have been honoured with the\
Roman triumph, of whom the emperor Probut, in whose feign the fabric of the\
Roman monarchy began to decay, was the last.\
Let us here subjoin  Grecian triump. Antiochus surnamed Epiphanes, or the il-\
lustrious king of Sria, having heard of the aforesaid glorious triumph of Paalus\
Amilius, was so puffed up with ambition, that he resolved to make  fort of one\
furpassing it in magnificence. To which end he caused proclamation to be made\
throughout his kingdom, that at  certain time, he would at Daphnes hold  grand\
and uncommon tournament : which curiofity drew out of Greece and the neigh-\

following manner.\
First, marched five thousand Grecian young men armed Roman like, followed\
by as many Mysians finely habited after their fafhion. Next appeared three\
thousand Thracians and five thousand Galatians, followed by  vast number of other\
nations, called, for their silver shields, Argyraspides. After these came two hun-\
dred and fifty ranks of sword-players, called by the Romans, gladiators ; and then\
 thousand knights, with chaplets of gold about their heads, and their horses\
costly equipped with gold embroidered housings, and gold and silver bridles.\
These were followed by  thousand other knights, called companions, afsociated\
with some of the king\'92 friends and confidents. Then appeared  thousand\
noblemen on foot, and after them  thousand other knights, called the king\'92\
troops. Next came one thousand five hundred knights in gold armour over\
which they had coats of armour richly embroidered with gold and silver, and art-\
fully adorned with all sorts of animals. To these succeeded an hundred chariots,\
each drawn by fix horses, followed by forty others, each with four. After these\
appeared  chariot with elephants, followed by thirty-six of the same kind of\
creatures, and those by eight hundred boys, having garlands aud crowns orna-\
mented with gold in their hands. Next came  thousand fat oxen with eight\
hundred Indian elephants teeth. After these were carried an infinite number of\
idols and figures of deceased persons who had been famous for arts and sciences,\
dressed in gold and silver stuffs adorned with precious stones, with their names,\
dignities, and actions written on the pedestals. Then came slaves bearing idols,\
representing night and morning, mid-day and evening, and an infinite number\
of gold and silver vessels of great value. Next appeared six hundred of the king\'92\
pages dressed in gold stuffs, followed by two hundred ladies carrying gold boxes,\
silled with all manner of rich perfumes and ordoriferious balm, and these by forty\
sedans of massy silver, carrying as many ladies, and those by eighty gold sedans\
with ladies dressed in gold, silver and jewels. The streets abounded with all sorts\
of rich oils, balms and perfumes. This cavalcade lasted thirty days successively,\
attended with plays, tournaments and shews ; during which time every person,\
after perfuming himself, was allowed to fit at the royal tables, one thousand five\
hundred in number, and to feast at the, king\'92 expence. To proceed to the\
Romans.\

Another solemnity obtained among them, cafled ovatio ; which was inferior\
to the triumph in fome of its requisites: for instance, if the victor was not of\
consular or proconsular dignity, or had met with little resistence from the enemy,\
or gained the victory without great blood-shed, or had overcome people of fmal\
worth, or, as we faid, speaking of the corona ovalis, when the war was under-\
taken without the express command of the senate, &. in such cases, the victorg\
were solemnly welcomed with the ovatio, in the following manner.\
The general entered the city on horse-back, or as anciently, on foot, crowned\
with myrtle, ( tree facred to Venus) because the victory was gained not in \
martial manner, but in  manner becoming that goddess and women, as Aulus\
Gellius says; and the troops in their possession appeared not in arms, and instead\
of druins, trumpets, and other warlike instruments, their music was flutes and\
other soft founds. The general entered with the booty in an orderly manner,\
followed by his army, and the senate solemnly received him without the city-gates\
highly commending his actions. Histories tells us, that several great generals\
sued for and accepted this honour. The first was Posthumius Libertus, on his having\
subdued the Sabines, and next Marcus Marcellus after the conquest of Svracuse. Sue-\
tonius telates, that Augustus, after the battle of Philippi, and on finilhing the war\
in Sicily, obtained that honour. And Pliny says, that several generals denied by\
the senate the honour of the great triumph, were decreed the ovatio ; which was\
so called, from the general\'92 offering  fheep, in Latin ovis, when he came to the\
is derived from the shouts of the people, who used to cry, Oel or else Ove \
Whatever the truth is, this solemn entrance was always called by the Romans,\
ovatio.\
Other triumphs of these people Ishall for brevity omit speaking of. He who\
wants further information may read Appianus Alexandrinus and Ammiannus Marcellinus,\
the former describing the triumph of Scipio Africanus, and the latter that of the\
emperor Constantius.\
CHAP. VI. Of the Manner of the four principal and public\
Grecian Games, and to whose honour instituted.\
HHE antiquity and manners of the Grecian games being somewhat unknown\
to many curious artists, Ithink it will be acceptable to give  fhort descrip-\
tion, as well as  can, of the four principal games so highly and so often extolled\
by the Greek and Roman writers.\
The first and principal were called the Obympic games, held near the city of\
Obmpia, in the province of Elis, and instituted in honour of Jupiter Olympius, by\
the Idan Hercules and his four brothers, P\'9coneus, Idas, Jasius and Epimedes, meet-\
ing together from Mount lda in Candia, and being five brethren, they were stiled\
the\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
ldaan dactyls. These games being celebrated every five years with great solem-\
nity, the ancients therefore reckoned their time by Obympiads, thereby under-\
standing  period of five years. They consisted of five sorts of exercifes, viz.\
running, wrestling, boxing, throwing the coit, and leaping. The place of exer-\
cise was fenced in with pales, and no spectator was suffered to come\
within it.\
Some pretend these games were instituted by Jupiter, after he had destroyed the\
giants who attempted to storm heaven ; and that Apollo had got the preference in\
out-running Mercury ; that Mars bore away the prize in wrestling, boxing, &.\
Others prove, that each of the aforesaid bretheren invented his game and ex-\
ercise, and that being five in number, they were from the five fingers named\
dactyls, Dactylos in Greek signifying  finger.\
The Greeks called these five exercises Pentathlon, and the Lattins Quinquertium.\
Two of them had  dependence on the legs, viz. running and leaping; two on the\
arms, as coits and boxing, and the wrestling respected both arms and legs. The\
victor in all the five exercises was by the Greeks called Pancratiastes ;  word com-\
pounded of Pan and Kratos, signifying  bestowing  whole force of the body.\
prize was adjudged to him who gained his point most dextrouslsy. Accordingly,\
they struck with fists and elbows, kicked, bit, scratched, and sprained the\
fingers, hands and other parts of the body. They even endeavoured to thrust out\
each others eyes with their thumbs. In short, no artifice was omitted for gaining\
the victory.\
We shall briefly relate in what manner the aforesaid five excercises were\
performed.\
The circus, wherein they run on foot was originally  stadium, or six hundred\
geomitrical feet in length : but in the fourth Olympiad they doubled it. This\
race was at first on foot, and in alight dress; but afterwards on horse-back and in\
armour. men called runners on foot, were also admitted armed srom top to\
toe ; this excercise being judged very proper for the bodies of warriors. The\
first victor herein was Demaratus of Herea : and the hymns sung in their honour,\
sufficiently testify their running in armour. But the first who got the prize in run-\
ning without armour, was Chorabus of Elis, after  long contest with him about\
it. Arrachion of Phigalia obtained the prize in the second and third excercifes; and\
Polycrates of Messene  man of noble extraction, got much honour and glory in\
the fourth wherein he was victor.\
The wrestling was undertaken after the body had been thoroughly anointed\
in order to prevent  gripe, and then daubed with fine dust to dry the sweat.\
Thus prepared, the wrestlers entered the lists, and began with seizing the hands,\
then the arms and body under the short ribs, &. Thus endeavouring by va-\
rious methods of strength and dexterity in kicking, pufhing, and othet tricks to\
fling one another on their backs ; for  fall on the belly went for nothing. Be\'ac\
fore they entered the ring they caused their parts to be soundly rubbed, to make\
them more supple and agile.\
Boxing\
\
\
\
\
\
\
\
Boxing and fighting with flings were the most dangerous exercifes. The for-\
mer was anciently performed with ox-leather thongs tied about the hands, by\
But\
which with wonderful activity they dealt each other with very hard blows.\
the slings consisted of small leather straps, armed at the ends with little leaden balls,\
the blow whereof when it happened on the head, laid the adversary dead.\
The coit was  flat, round, heavy piece of stone or lead, to try the force of\
armsand hands, and to see who could sling highest and furthest : an excercise still\
in use in many places to this day ; but with this difference, that the ancients with\
 leg lifted up, threw the coit at  mark set up on  small pyramid, and resembling\
 pine-apple.\
The fifth excercise was lefs perilous, as consisting only of divers manners of\
leaping.\
The ancient garlands or crowns given as  prize to the victor on these occasions,\
were made of olive leaves, but they varied according to the times; for they\
were afterwards composed of couch grass, willow, laurel, myrtle, oak, palm\
and wild parsley leaves ; as Plutarch in the life of Cato Uticensis relates. But when\
made of olive leaves, they chose  select kind called Calistephancs, . . beauti-\
ful crown, having hanging branches like the myrtle, very proper for twisting\
garlands. The leaves of such garland differed much from others, in that being\
white without, the green when twisted was inward ; whereas the others were\
white within, and appeared green without. Hercules and his bretheren first\
brought this plant into Greece from the northern countries, as Pausanias in his\
Olympus tells us.\
The Phythian games were instituted long before the lsthmian, yet after the Olym-\
pic, and celebrated in honour of Apollo for his victory over the frightful serpent\
Python. Some think they were so called from Pythos, the place of celebra-\
tion, or else from the Greek word Pythestai, to consult ; because they there con-\
sulted the oracle, in order to know the events of things to come. The exercifes\
in these games only differed in the Olympic in this, that the Phythian were performed\
under the sound of all sorts of vocal and instrumental music. These games from\
time to time, had several alterations in form and solemnity, after the institution of\
the Pancratium or Quinquertiam: and it is related, that in the first \'e0 Pythiades,\
wherein the most illustrious heroes and gods of the ancients, entered the lists for\
the sake of the prize; Castor prevailed in the horse-race, Pollux in boxing, Callais\
in running on foot, Zethees in running in compleat armour, Peleus in throwing.\
the coit, Telamon in wrestling, and Hercules in the Pancratium, or all the games.\
In each of these games and exercifes the victors were crowned with laurel,\
which in particular was consecrated to them; because the ancients believed, by\
what they have feigned of Peneus\'92 daughter, with whom Apollo was so much en-\
amoured, and who was metamorphosed into that tree, that the god took  singu-\
lar delight in it. But others will have the institution of the Phythian games to\
be long before Apollo\'92 amour with the beautiful Daphne: and before the laurel\

The Nemaan games were kept in  wood of that name, situate between Phliuns\
and Cleona, two cities of Achia, in honour and memory of Archemorus, otherwise\
called Opheltes son of Lycurgus, on account of his being killed by  serpent in this\

Eteocles and Polynice to whom he resigned the royal dignity, on condition they\
governed by turns: but Eteocles as the eldest, having obtained the first year\'92 admi\'ac\

law zydeus, raised  great army, in order to wage war with the Thebans and bring\
them to reafon. The issue of-this war was the death of the two \'92others in \
duel; and their bodies according to custom, being laid on  large pile of wood\
to be burnt, the flames happened to divide and seperate, as if they bore witness\
of their immortal hatred of the two bretheren in their life-times, which ceased not\
with their deaths. Now in the army which Adrastus sent to Polynices\'92 assistance\
were seven commanders, who being arrived in the island Lemnos, pertaining to\
Thracia, and seized with an extreme thirst, met Hypsipyle, carying in her arms\
the child Opheltes, son of Lycurgus. (priest of Jupiter) and Euridice, whom being\
 native of that country, they intreated to shew them where to get some water.\
Whereupon she in haste, vet fearful of laying the child on the ground, as for-\

of wild parsley near  fountain, where  serpent lying perdue, suddenly wound it-\

The commanders being apprised of this accident, killed the serpent, and to solace\

The Nemaan games were therefore instituted in memory and consolation of\
Lycurgus, Etridice, and Opheltes, and the judges who determined the prizes, were\
dressed in black and mourning garments. For Opheltes was afterwards called Ar-\
chemorus, because Amphiaraus had at his birth presaged him an early and untime-\

ee Dying shortly after his birth:, in which sense speaks the poet,\
ee Naseentes morimur, finisque ab origine pendet ,'\
That is, ee We begin dying from our births, and our beginnings and ends have\
an inseparable union.\
The excercises in these games were the same as in others: but the victors\

For of the four principal games which we handle in this chapter, two according\

This solemnity was performed at night in the Isthmus of Corinth, parting Morea\

Plutarch writes, that Theseus, after having killed the bull of Minos, and performed.\

Yet according to Pausanias and others, these games were not set up for that\

The dead body of Melicerta being afterwards brought on shore by  dolphin,\

Leucotha, called by the Latins Matuta, is day-break ; and Palamon, or Portunus,\

Others tell us, that Melicerta\'92 dead body being cast on the shore of the Isthmus,\

The prize in the Isthmian games, was originally  crown of parsl\'e9y, elegantly\
wreathed; but afterwards,  garland of pine-leaves, on account of their neigh-\

their entrance through the common gates, like other people, but triumphantly\

We shall, to conclude this chapter, subjoin  fhort description of some particu-\
lar garlands or crowns, sacred to the heathenisn deities.\
The Phaonicians, as Euscbius testifies, honoured and worshipped the herbs and\
plants. The Grec\'e8s in imitation of them, rendered almost the same duties, not\
only to trees, but also to herbs and flowers: these maintained that the Charities, or\
Three Graces were the very crowners of Pandora. Pherecydes says, that Saturn was\
crowned before any others : yet, according to Diodorus, Jupiter claims this honour\
for his conquest over the giants : but, not to pretermit the Agyptian monuments,\
Isis first crowned herself with green sprigs and ears of corn; of which, according\
to the Agyptian writer, Leon, she was the inventor.\
The oak and its fruit, as Apollodorus writes, were sacred to the goddess Rhea,\
otherwise called the Earth, that mortals who proceed from it, might wear the\
badges of their universal mother: the same tree was also peculiar to Jupiter, the\
tutelar god.\

The pine and its fruit were consecrated to the goddess Cbele, whom the an-\
cients believed to be the mother of all things; because she, carefully containing the\
seed originally given her, does by the warmth of the sun yearly bring forth new\
shoots. The pine-apple in its shape, also resembles  rising flame, and keeps its\
seed in small and separate cells, which by the earth\'92 heat, in time springs up and\
grows. We likewise see  certain medal, with the head of Gbele on one side, and\
 small garland of pine-twigs on the other, and inscribed EMTPNAIAN, . .\
those of Smyrna. The Arcadians believing Pan to be the god of the universe, de-\
dicated the pine-apple to him also.\
Saturn, Jupiter, Apollo and Asculapius were crowned with laurel ; Saturn as the\
god of triumphs; Jupiter, for his victory over the giants ; Apollo, for the love of\
Daphne, metamorphosed into that tree ; though before, the palm was sacred to\
him, on his killing the Delphic dragon; but Asculapius wears it for no other rea-\
son, than that it is useful for several remedies.\
The ancient Romans, on their nona capretinae, or festivals kept monthly, in honour\
of Juno, crowned that goddess with fig-leaves, as  memorial of the city of Rome\
(reduced to the utmoss extremity by the Gauls, who demanded of the senate seve-\
ral noble virgins as hostages) regaining its fre\'ebdom by the contrivance of the vir-\
gin Philoris, who shewing the Romans how to slide down from the walls, by the\
branches of  fig-tree growing thercon, and sacred to Juno, gave them an oppor-\
tunity of falling on the enemy when drunk and asleep (which sne had cunningly\
inticed them to) and by  great slaughter, to obtain  compleat victory over them.\
The pomegranate was consecrated to Juno, by the people of Mycena. The white\
lilly is alfo sacred to her, and therefore called Flos Junonius, or, according to some,\
flos regalis; not so much out of respect to the queen or goddess, but because that\
slower almost surpasses all others in height.\
Minerva who is said to be  virgin, rightfully laid claim to the olive-tree, which\
affects purity and chastity, as well as she.\
 find no trees particularly sacred to Mars; but it is notorious that the herb\
commonly called dog\'92 grass is appropriated to him.\
They who are conversant with poets, know that the myrtle-tree signifies delight\
and  mind richly endowed. The ancients say that tree surpasses all others in ten-\
der and beautiful leaves, and their continual greenness and smell, which recom-\
mends it to Venus, the most beautiful, most tender, and most perfect of the god-\
desses. In- old times, men on festival days used to put into each others hands\
branches of this tree as tokens of joy, and that they should join in chorus : and\
Horace says that in Lent-time, when the earth by her variety of flowers seems to\
rejoice, we ought to adorn our heads with wreaths of myrtle. The apple-tree sig-\
nifying love, is also sacred to Venus; and the ears of corn to Ceres.\
The ivy dedicated to Bacchus, was in great esteem among the Agyptians for being\
always green and not shedding\
its leaves till after harvest: they mostly used it in\
garlands, and the kind bearing\
black-berries was especially consecrated to Bacchus,\
who by that people is called Ofiris, and from whence this green also borrows its\
nanie ; for they called it Cenofiris, . . the plant of Ofiris : and Dionysius, which is\
likewise the name of Bacchus) having carried his victories into India, built there\
Nsa,\
